THE RED PAGE.
ANTHOLOGY —Greek, anthos, a flower;
legem, to gather wherefore Alice
Meynell calls her anthology, “The
Flower of the Mind.” If the good
lady would permit her temperament to be
seen merely in the collection—in the hues,
and scents, and arrangement ! But she will
pluck out stained petals (as she thinks)—she
will omit the verses and passages which do
not please her private taste—and so will
insist on misrepresenting the minds whose
flowers she plucks. Palgrave was as bad in
his arbitrary way—lopping and altering to
suit his early-Victorian maxims and morals.
Concede that in a “ popular ” collection you
cannot, always print the lively Elizabethans
and the loose Cavaliers as they were origi-
nally printed ; but you can at least add a
note of regret for their too-mucli, or your
too-little. And the Rev. H. C. Beeching,
whose anthology is perhaps the most satis-
factory of all, can omit half-a-dozen lines of
Marvell's “ To His Coy Mistress ” without a
hint of loss.
Professor Arber—that famous grubber
among old books and manuscripts, and re-
printer of forgotten worthies, has a greater
reverence for the written word and the
author’s privilege ; and he cuts, apparently,
as little as he can help. His ten volumes of
anthologies, of which five are already issued
by the Oxford Press, will afford presumably
the best view of English lyric poetry so-far
obtainable. He takes his authors by histori-
cal periods ; and gives you a Shakespeare
anthology, a Jonson anthology, a Dryden
anthology, and so on—getting his titles from
the leading poets of his periods. And as his
volumes are well printed and bound in cloth
for only half-a-crown each, the series can be
honestly recommended to poetry-lovers.
“The Dryden Anthology,” one of the
'-volumes latest to hand* covers the period ©f
the Restoration—the time of England’s
lyrical afterglow. The young intensity, the
magical imagination, of the mighty Eliza-
bethans were no more ; and instead of the
poetry of nature and the lover in the grove
came the poetry of artifice and the lover
at the court. There was learning, and
leisure, and the mode of France swayed all
who came within its influence. Charles Rex
led the way of easy morals, and a troop of
lyrists followed in uneasy metres—for the
French dress did not accommodate it-
self too well to the stiff angles of the
sombre English mind, joyous almost in
spite of itself. Everybody’s mistress ex-
pected a tripping measure in celebration
of her bright eyes, or white bosom ; and as
often as not she retaliated in kind. It was
a gentleman’s part to rhyme as well as he
rode ; and the graces of amorous verbiage
were cultivated almost as much as the graces
of L’Ainour herself. And withal it was a
manly age, even if the manliness was running
to seed. The stern and virile virtues had
been tried and refined in the long wars
of the Commonwealth ; and minds retained
a residue of heat to fill their fervid verses.
There was no more Shakespearean linnet-
singing by a brook, to a star ; yet the linnet
caged and preened piped brilliantly of lovers’
passion and a'lover’s feverish delights. It
was an afterflow with a hectic flush, maybe ;
but the light has lasted, and the throb of
the lyre still thrills.
Professor Arber’s “Dryden Anthology”
keynotes on Dryden naturally—on Dryden
master of the artificial Muse, and just com-
ing to his noon-age at the Restoration. Ten
years later he was Laureate, and faithful to
the king who fed him ; but there was
always too much intellectual admixture
in Dryden’s lyrics. Andrew Marvell, maybe
more sophisticated, seems yet to cull a more
fragrant blossom of emotion. Avoiding his
better-known pieces, has not this a dainty
flavour ?■—of Ametas and Thestylis making
hay-ropes—
Ametas. Think’st thou, that this love can stand,
Whilst thou still dost say me “Nay !”?
Love unpaid does soon disband ;
Love binds love, as hay binds hay !
Thestylis. Think’st thou, that this rope would
twine
If we both should turn one. way ?
Where both parties so combine,
Neither love will twist ; nor hay.
Ametas. Thus, you vain excuses find ;
Which yourself and us delay !
And Love ties a woman’s mind
Looser than with ropes of hay!
Thestylis. What you cannot constant hope,
Must be taken as you may I
Ametas. Then let’s both lay by our rope;
And go kiss within the hay !
But it is John Wilmot, Earl of Roches-
ter, who is the type of the Restoration lyrist
—dead at 33, but crowding into his short life
more passion, more emotion, more pang of*
pain arid pleasure than half-a-dozen devout cits,
could boast on turning up respectable toes
at respectable 72. The moralists are dis-
gusted with himx-but he lived. He was
born in the spring of 1647, was a scholar
and traveller in his teens, and before he was
twenty was fighting the Dutch with a gay
courage which made men marvel. When he
came back to Court he diced, wenched,
swore, fought, and wrote with the best of the
roystering brotherhood; and—perhaps—re-
pented on his deathbed—when there was
nothing else to do. And he wrote this for
Sylvia—
I promised Sylvia to be true ;
Nay, out of zeal, I swore it too !
And that she might believe me more,
Gave her in writing what I swore.
Not vows, nor oaths, can Lovers bind 1
So long as blessed, so long they’re kind !
’T was in a leaf ! The wind but blew;
Away both Leaf and Promise flew !
George Etherege, the dramatist, was a
bottle of the same vintage, but not from the
same cuvee. He carried the English melan-
choly with a light hand—
TO A LADY,
Asking him how long he would love her.
It is not, Celia, in our power
To say how long our love will last!
It may be, we, within this hour,
May lose those joys we now do taste I
The Blessed, that immortal be,
From change in love are only free !
Then, since we mortal Lovers are,
Ask not, How long our love will last ?
But while it does let us take care
Each minute be with pleasure past !
Were it not madness, to deny
To live; because we ’re sure to die !
And this is no less neatly finished with the
antithesis—
Ye happy Swains, whose hearts are free
Erom Love’s imperial chain,
Take warning, and be taught by me,
T’ avoid th’ enchanting pain !
Fatal the wolves, to trembling flocks !
Fierce*winds, to blossoms prove !
To careless seamen, hidden rocks!
To human quiet, Love !
Ely the Eair Sex ! if bliss you prize.
The snake’s beneath the flower !
Who ever gazed on beauteous eyes,
That tasted quiet more ?
How faithless is the Lovers’ joy !
How oonstant is their care !
The Kind, with falsehood do destroy ;
The Cruel, with despair !
There are Prior, and Otway, and George
Granville to reckon with ; but pass to Sir
Charles .Sedley, “ iiotoriousest for debauch-
ery and wit ’’—and in virtue of a dozen
songs immortal. For he wrote
Ah ! Cloris ! that I now could sit,
And
Phillis ! men say, that all my vows
And
Love still has something of the sea ;
From whence his mother rose.
And all the other tender pieces that two
centuries have cherished. This is no less
well known, but
Not, Celia ! that I juster am,
Or better than the rest!
For I would change, each hour, like them ;
Were not my heart at rest ! ’
But I am tied to very Thee,
By every thought I have !
Thy face I only care to see !
Thy heart I only crave !
All that in Woman is adored,
In thy dear self I find !
For the whole Sex can but afford
The Handsome and the Kind !
Why then, should I seek farther store ;
And still make love anew ?
When Change itself can give no more,
’T is easy to be true !
It is not possible to surpass that.
Then the gay D’Urfey, who rather wrote
in the mode than lived in it—
Some thirty, or forty, or fifty, at least,
Or more, I have loved in vain, in vain !
But if you ’ll vouchsafe to receive a poor guest;
For once, I will venture again, again !
How long I shall be in this mind, this mind,
Is totally in your own power !
All my days I can pass with the Kind, the Kind;
But I ’ll part with the Proud in an hour !
Then if you ’ll be good-natured, and civil, and
civil;
You ’ll find I can be so too, so too !
But if not, you may go, you may go to the
Devil;
Or the Devil may come to you, to you !
And ’fc is time to end this roundabout,
passing even Nahum Tate’s “ Hurricane,”
and a score of other famous pieces. Close
with the best tobacco-poem extant—and the
author unknown ! It was published with
music in a “Wit and Mirth” collection,
1699.,
Tobacco is but an Indian weed,
Grows green in the morn; cut down at eve.
It shows our decay.
We are but clay !
Think of this, and take Tobacco !
The Pipe, that is so lily-white,
Wherein so many take delight,
Is broke with a touch.
Man’s life is such !
Think of this, and take Tobacco !
The Pipe, that is so foul within,
Shews how Man’s soul is stained with sin.
It does require
To be purged with fire !
Think of this, and take Tobacoo !
The Ashes, that are left behind,
Do serve to put us all in mind,
That unto dust,
Return we must!
Think of this, and take Tobacoo !
The Smoke, that does so high ascend,
Shews you Man’s life must have an end.
The vapour’s gone !
Man’s life is done !
Think of this, and take Tobacco !
Circular received —
Mr. Arliss Andrews has pleasure in presenting for
review this copy of “ Aurelia,” by Arthur Maquarie, the
published price of which is Is. Note.—Mr. Arthur Ma-
quarie, who is an Australian and a B.A. of the Sydney
University, has recently been much quoted in London,
where he has gained appreciation both in prose and verse,
and one of his poems has been set to music by Sir
Alexander Mackenzie. His first literary effort to appear
in the public press was honoured with a place at the head
of the “Bards from the Backblocks” column in the
Sydney . Bulletin. The accompanying short poem
(“ Aurelia ”) was written in Spain.
It might as well have been printed in Spain-
though there is no reason why it should be printed
anywhere.
When David Mitchell, who is by way of being
a millionaire (of books), offered to bequeath his
library to N.S. Wales, he conditioned a special
trust to preserve them ; and it seemed a good
occasion to remodel existing trusts. So a short
Act discussed N.S.W. Art Gallery and
Public Library together, in a manner of
speaking ; and a Leg. Council friend of
the Art Society interpolated a clause permitting
them to nominate two trustees-to balance the exe-
crated protagonist of the Society of Artists.
Julian Ashton (there may be better artists round
about, but none with better brains) has long
renounced, deferent to clamour, the privi-
lege of selling pictures to the Gallery which
he trustees ; and the trustees of the other
Society —— could do no less. Couldn’t
they ? The Art Society nominated Lister
Lister and Arthur Collingridge, and specially
resolved that they might continue to sell pictures
to the Gallery over which they are placed in
authority. There was, it is alleged, a tacit un-
derstanding that they should not sell, and they
say they will not; but, O, the hold they give the
enemy ! —who has a move in reserve, by the way.
For the gate is opened to all the old strife which
it closed.
Obituary notices of R. D. Blackmore have
stressed the superiority of “Lorna Doone” with a
note of wonder; and it is true, as alleged, that
other of Blaokmore’s romances show an equal
skill. The difference is that V Lorna Doone ”is
based on fact, and has the vitality of fact;
“ Cradock Nowell ” and the rest are invented, and
are only the shadows of fact. Nothing, nothing
like a basis of life to rest a living story on. Lack-
ing the real D’Artagnan, Dumas would never
have created that unimaginable Musketeer. Then
“Lorna Doone ” has the two obvious elements of
romance in the highest degree. Romance in-
weaves strength with beauty. Blackmore took
the giant strength of Jan Ridd and married it to
the fairy-beauty of “Lorna Doone” —and a good
deal of the rest went without telling. But Lorna,
whom he imagined, is always less vital than girt
Jan, whom he described on the basis of tradition.
Add the smack of the Devonshire soil —the taste
of the. Devonshire cream —and you have the where-
withal of semi-perennial charm.
Whisper, spring-wind softly singing ;
Whisper in my ear,
Respite and nepenthe bringing,
Can the goal be near :
.Laden with the dew of vespers,
From the fragrant sky ;
In my ear the wind that whispers,
Seems to make reply
“ Question not, but live and labour
Till yon goal be won ;
Helping every feeble neighbour,
Seeking help from none;
Life is mostly froth and bubble,
Two things stand like stone :
Kindness in another’s trouble,
Courage in your own.”
Gordon therefore may either use the inverted commas to
show he is in effect quoting what the wind whispers, or
again that the lines are not original. He uses quotation
marks in two other places in same poem. As to the old-
lettered variant with “ mainly ” for “ mostly,” it was first
pointed out —as occurring over the mantel in a room of a
great ancient house at Windsor —through an article on
“A Hying Ghost” in The Speaker for Aug. 19, 1899.
Surely, setting aside the question of inverted commas not
meaning he.was quoting—is it not possible for the mantel
motto to have been copied from Gordon’s poem itself—
-1866- For Gordon’s admitted references to the sources of
his quoted lines, as instanced in “ A Preface and a Piracy”
from E. A. Poe—and elsewhere—hardly warrant one
accusing Gordon of actual bare-faced plagiary on evidence
such as you mention.
Well, you sec, it was “pretty Fanny’s way” (see
R.P. 22/5/97). But Gordon was only arraigned;
he was not judged. Perhaps somebody will tell
us the apparent date of the Windsor verse.
The Bookfellow.
THE COFFIN-SHIP.
They patched her topsides here and there —they could not
patch beneath :
Her timbers were a rotten pulp inside their copper sheath ;
The shipwright swore to the carpenter, as the carpenter
looked askance,
“By God 1 there’s never a bolt will hold': the must
take her chance !
Her keel’s adrift, and her bilges shift—to call her ship is
a shame ;
But the sailors’ lives are the owner’s risk—let the owner
take the blame!
We ’re paid to paint and potter around, and we ’ll paint
and potter her well;
It’s none of our business where she goes—let the go
to hell 1 ”
They patched her topsides here and there ; they dumped
the coal-freight in:
And every ton was another blow t<3 sever her bones from
her skin ;
She’d spued her oakum in the dock, she sprung a leak in
port,
A child could have read her doom of dread—but sailors’
sight is. short;
For a sailor’s life is a reckless life, and Jack must smother
his whim:
When women and liquor have eaten his pay, he’s articled
,—sink or swim !
Nine times in ten he’s safe again, and the tenth time
he ’ll pull through :
The eleventh time is the Devil’s own time—and the coffin-
ship gets her due.
They shipped her crew of reckless men ; they towed her
out to sea,
With her quaking hull.and her shivering spars; that
groaned of the grave to be ;
She had been a good-enough ship in her day, but her day
had long since ceased,
And the old hulk sung her dirge as she flung a desperate
head to the East; —
Wallowing in the trough of the waves, lumbering like an
ox,
With her sticks astrain till they yelled with the pain, and
no more lift than a box ;
While the captain watched by the ripping side, and said,
with his red face pale,
“ If she sings her psalm like this in a calm, what ’ll she do
in a gale ?”
The lighthouse hardly winked its last before the forehold
rose :
They manned the pumps and split their hearts to raise
her ducking nose ;
The boats were rotten as the ship—they sweated at a raft
. Till a black wave found her waterlogged and drove the
wretches aft;
Her bows were weltering in the seas, her stem was shiver-
ing high,
They heard the coals go thundering, and knew the end
was nigh ;
They cursed the owner and the craft, and braced them-
selves to drown,
And ’twixt a flurry and a sob the coffin-ship dived down.
The owner vents a chastened grief—God’s will must be
endured,
The sailors’ children cry for bread—but they were not
insured;
The owner eats with appetite, and drinks the best of
wine—
. The y’ve done with appetites who gulped their last long
drink of brine;
The owner’s bed is softest down, and all is to his whim,
Yet they sleep sounder in the ooze who ’re sacrificed to
him •
And I swear that theirs is the happier fate, who gave
their lives for his coal —
They only sank with his rotten ship, he swims with his
rotten soul.
"The Dryden Anthology.”
’Tis Woman makes us love!
’T is Love that makes us sad I
’Tis Sadness makes us drink;
And Drinking makes us mad !
ANON.
Writes E. Wilson Dobbs—
Regarding reference to Gordon’s (so-called) plagiarisms
in issue of Feb. 10, the lines in question—with “ mostly ’’
for “ mainly ” (Gordon’s own variant) are often printed
with modernised spelling in English periodicals—without
author’s name—as a sort of motto. As example, see
under “ Two Things ” in a booklet called “ Home Chat,”
1 for Feb. 12th, 1898. Gordon may thus have utilised them
from some old ballad. As originally appearing in “ Bell’s
Life in Victoria” for Nov., 1866—and in all subsequent
editions of G’s poems—this penultimate verse of “ Finis
Exqptatus ” is enclosed within quotation marks. The
lines run-
THE OLD MAWS WISH.
If I live to grow old (for I find I go down !)
Let this be my fate in a country town !
Let me have a warm house, with a stone at the
gate ;
And a cleanly young girl to rub my bald pate !
May I govern my Passion with, an absolute sway!
And grow wiser and better, as my strength wears
away,
Without gout or stone, by. a gentle decay !
In a country town, by a murmuring brook,
The ocean at distance, on which I may look ;
With a spacious plain, without hedge or stile,
And an easy pad-nag to ride out a mile.
With a pudding on Sunday, and stout humming
liquor,
And remnants of Latin to puzzle the Vicar;
With a hidden reserve of Burgundy wine,
To drink the King’s Health as oft as I dine.
With Plutarch and Horace, and one or two more
Of the best Wits that lived in the Ages before;
With a dish of roast mutton, not venison, or teal,
And clean, though coarse, lirten at every meal...
With courage undaunted, may I face my last day;
And, when I am dead, may the better sort say,
“ In the morning, when sober ; in the evening,
when mellow :
He is gone, and has left not behind him his
fellow !
For he governed his Passion with an absolute
sway !
And grew wiser and better, as his strength wore
away,
Without gout or stone, by a gentle decay.”
WALER POPE(?— 1714).

What looks like originality, but is only
another instance of the hold “ shop ” has on the
minds of some people, is the recent gruesome
suicide of a London baker. After baking a batch
of bread he appears to have become suddenly tired
of the awful monotony of dough, got into his oven
and secured the door, undressed himself and laid
down on a bag. Being a careful baker with a
reputation to consider he evidently regulated the
heat to suit the circumstances, and when found
was done to a turn, or as the cheerful papers
reported it “ with a light brown er 't all over.”

Various Verses.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
“His” Grievance!
When Rome had reft with impious hand
The wives and maids of Sabine land,
All right and justice spurning ;
The injured nation gripped the steel
And marched along to battle-peal,
For blood and vengeance burning.
The night before they drew to Rome,
The warriors from a distant home
In soldier-wise uproarious,
Were wild of speech, and loud and long,
For each was bent to right his Wrong
By valiant fight and glorious.
But one there was who walked apart
With sighs that seemed to rend his heart
And words of bitter railing ;
It grieved his gallant comrades sore
To note the weary look he bore,
His sorrow unavailing.
“ ’Tis sure a wight,” the warriors cried,
“ Who also lost his tender bride
The time of our disaster ! ”
They spoke him kindly, bade him cheer —
“ To-morrow hails a vict’ry dear,
And Rome a Sabine master ! ”
The soldier slowly raised his head,
“ ’Tis true, my comrades dear,” he said,
“ I fiercely hate the Roman.
I hope to meet him, steel to steel,
And make his perjured bosom feel
In me his fellest foeman.
“ I know the Wrong that fires your brain,
The stolen spouse and virgin train
The hearth and home forsaken ;
But Pve a heavier score to pay,
For by some chance that fatal day,
My wife was never taken ! ”
Brisbane.
P. LUGTIG.
The GreyhounD of the Moon.
They harry the wild Antarctic,
They sweep the still lagoon —
The Chargers of the Ocean,
The Greyhounds of the Moon.
They follow her round the Planet
In a lon-/ unending siuin / ;
They break on the sandy beaches,
And this is the song they sing
We have come from the Uttermost Ocean,
We have followed the changing sphere
From island unto island,
In the wake of the marching year.
We have plunged in the Roaring Forties,
A thousand leagues from shore :
We have raced round the world for ages,
We will race for ages more.
We have ridden past lone Kerguelen,
We have washed the Golden Gate ;
We have dashed up the bleak Lolander
As we surged through Foveaux Strait.
We have fretted the Falkland Islands
Since the Falkland Isles were born ;
We have cast the wrecks on Agulhas,
And the icebergs on Cape Horn.
Where the burning mountain’s beacon
Lights up the midn 'g'it sky,
Where the sperm whales all assemble
And the albatrosses fly,
The whaler, fleeing northward
From the long midwinter night,
And the floes that crash and splinter
Acknowledge our sovereign might.
We stretch from the Pole to the Tropics—
From atoll to iceberg drear,
Below us lie the cables,
Above, the steamers steer ;
We carry the white-wing’d wool-ships,
We hurry the Homeward Mail,
And we bear the ’Frisco clippers
On the long Pacific trail.
We have come from the Uttermost Ocean,
We have followed the changing sphere
From island unto island,
In the wake of the marching year ;
We harry the wild Antarctic,
We sweep the still lagoon—
The Chargers of the Ocean,
The Greyhounds of the moon.
A. E. CURRIE.
New Version.
I made a song to Love—
And Love in kingly might
Unbent and said:
“ Thy song hath pleased me quite,
What wouldst thou have ? ”
I answered, “ Give me sight.”
He gave, and 10, 1 saw
The fool his breath had bred
In shape of me ;
I made a dagger red
With blood —but found
’Twas my own heart that bled.
Then where his praise was spoke
I raised a song above
In his despite,
But powerlessly I strove ;
For he sings ill
Who says harsh things of Love.
RODERIC QUINN.

the publishers. The right to reproduce or translate any

any other part of the contents of this number, is hereby
expressly reserved by the publishers.
The Editor will carefully read and acknowledge in the
‘Correspondence" column all contributions submitted
whether in the form of Political Social, or other Articles
Verse, Short Tales or Sketches (those dealing with Aus-
tralian subjects, and not extending two columns in
length, or, say, 2000 words, are specially acceptable),
Paragraphs, Letters, or Newspaper-Clippings. All
communications will be regarded as strictly confidential.
The Bulletin will return all unsuitable MS. when
the address is specified and stamps are enclosed. But we
desire those sending MS. to distinctly understand that
we are not responsible for its preservation or transmission.

SATURDAY, FEBRUARY 24, 1900.
After the War.
According to common report many changes
are liable to come about when the present
war is over. Possibly they won’t come
about just then—after every war there is
going to be a stupendous re-organisation of
things, but, as a rule, the weariness of large
bodies asserts itself, and the re-organisation
is postponed till a more convenient season.
Still there always comes a time when it can
be postponed no longer, and if this time
isn’t the time in question it looks something
very like it. Britain is beginning to talk
seriously about a conscription now, and
about adopting the hated military servitude
that weighs down continental nations, and
dropping a whole lot of its boasted freedom
overboard. Also, it is beginning to talk of
many other things that used to be abhorrent
to British ideas. The dire necessity for
becoming a great military power is fairly in
sight, and a very pleasant fool's paradise has
burst, or is going to burst, or at least is a
good deal cracked.
• • * *
For the first time in two centuries or so
Britain has gone into a serious war without
an ally. Certainly it didn’t look like a
serious war to begin with, and it shouldn’t
have been one, but it proved to be one all
the same. It was also a war in which the
British navy could do no actual fighting.
The enemy was just about the smallest
civilised nation on this earth, with little
money as well as few men. Despite all that
has been said about the great wealth which
the Transvaal Government derived from tax-
ing the Outlander population the annual
revenue of both the Boer States put
together was only about half that of
N.S.W., and the Boer population of both
States isn’t half the population of Sydney !
They are vastly less fitted in visible
resources to give back-talk to the big-
gest empire on earth than N.S.W. is. Even
the small revenue they have is mostly of
recent date. The Free State has never had
an income of over £400,000 a year. The
Transvaal was in the same situation till the
gold discoveries, and the whole revenue
which it collected in all the years since then
is just about equal to what Britain spends on
defence in a single year of peace. The im-
mense armaments and the immense array of
mercenaries which the Transvaal is said to
possess can’t be so very large after all, for
the means to purchase them has always been
wanting. A country whose revenue has
never been more than a quarter that of
Belgium, or nearly half that of Holland, or
more than half that of N.S.W.—which
stands about on a par with Norway and
Switzerland, and doesn’t even approach
Victoria, and which had very little revenue
of any kind till a few years ago, can’t very
well have built up such stupendous defences
as the Transvaal is often credited with.
• * • »,
And now that these two flyspecks have
kept Britain at bay for four months the fact
has become obvious that the British army,
as armies go nowadays, is small potatoes and
few in a hill. In Napoleon’s time it was a
reasonably large army as compared with
those of Continental Europe. Now, when
Britain meets even a very small enemy who
can force it to fight wholly on land, its forces
appear to be wretchedly inadequate. By
reason of the enormous spreading of the
Empire, and the ceaseless earth-swallowing
of recent years, the number of enemies who
can force Britain to fight them on land grows
constantly larger. Also, the spread of rail-
ways is constantly making the big Empire
more accessible by land and less defensible
by sea. Some day Russia, which is
practically unapproachable by sea, must
needs compel Britain to fight on land
in Asia, and Russia is jn’obably worth
the two Boer Republics 12 times over and
something to spare. The last gaudy war
episode in British history was that when
Britain and three other nations spent two
years taking one Russian town, and that was
a coastal row where the British navy was of
some service. So Britain, having built up a
huge empire without making any adequate
arrangements for its defence, begins to talk
at last of the necessity for conscription or
some sort of compulsory military service.
It may not come just yet, but it is coming
almost to a certainty.
* * * *
Already the British Government sees its
way to a necessity for spending £.10,000,000
a year more in defence, and even this scheme
is declared to be a mere makeshift and
wholly inadequate. If the defence bill
piles up very much higher something will
almost certainly happen to the great Free-
trade policy merely through the need for
more revenue, for the burden of direct
taxation is already very heavy and can’t
stand indefinite increase. The millennium,
when the British national debt will be
all paid off, and the appalling burden which
Britain accumulated at the beginning of this
century by trying to act the part of a father
to Europe in general will be cleared away,
seems to be postponed for a while, and the
necessary increase of armaments may further
postpone it. Hitherto none of the great
States of Europe has shouldered the immense
contract of trying to be a great sea and land
power at the same time, but now Britain
appears to have run up hard against the
..necessity of being both. Or if it hasn’t yet
actually run up against it, the time can be
foreseen when the necessity will certainly
arise. And when considering the need for
a greatly enhanced army it may also be
necessary to consider where that army is to
come from. In the days of the Napoleonic
wars Ireland and the Scotch Highlands and
the English agricultural districts supplied
Britain’s best troops. They have all been
more or less depopulated since then. Rich
Jews and rich Gentiles, imported Germans,
and Americans who made vast fortunes in
pork have bought up Highland estates
wholesale, and given Britain’s best fighting
material notice to quit so as to make room
for sheep or grouse or deer. Also British
noblemen have fired out their own country-
men wholesale for similar reasons. Some-
body in Poland made a desert and called it
peace. The Highland landlord made a
desert and called it a grouse moor. Out of
its intense regard for the rights of property
and the maxims of Freetrade, the British
Government has allowed short fat foreigners
and others to eject from Britain a great part
of Britain’s best defenders. Some day the
need for suppressing the old British nobility
and the rich British landowners as disloyal
institutions will have to be seriously con-
sidered.
» • * *
As another result of the present trouble
in South Africa, Imperial Federation is
again beginning to come in sight in an
uncertain sort of way. Sundry Australian
politicians, with little discretion and a great
rush of military manoeuvres to the head, have
been allowed to lay it down as an axiom
that it is now Australia’s duty to
bear a large and cordial hand in all Britain’s
wars, be the cause good or indifferent.
They have even gone further and at-
tempted to lay it down as an axiom that
it is treason and base disloyalty to have
any doubts about the goodness of any cause
in which Britain is involved. And a very large
section of the community has accepted both
ideas in a time of wild excitement, and gone
into many public-houses in ferocious search of
people who looked as if they disagreed with
them. If these ideas continue on top it seems
reasonably certain that Imperial Federation
is coming sooner or later. The honor and
privilege of taking part in all British wars
will naturally suggest the equally notable
honor and privilege of taking a hand in the
foreign policy which makes these wars. The
country which wants to take part in shaping
the destinies of the empire by doing the
commonplace job of carrying a heavy gun
and a lot of ammunition and a bag of pro-
vender on the field of action isn’t likely to
stop there. It is almost certain to want to
go further and help shape these destinies on
a larger scale. Then Australia will carry its
share of the burdens of the empire regularly,
instead of doing a casual lift when it feels
that way, and its regular share is estimated
at about £2,000,000 a year, with more to
follow as population increases. Already this
country’s willingness to bear a hand has
induced sundry British authorities to point
out that £2,000,000 a year is the amount
Australia ought fairly to contribute, and one
or two Australian politicians have declared,
at banquets and elsewhere, that the idea is
very reasonable, and the remark has been
well received on the whole.
* * * •
It begins to look by no means certain that
a time may not come when Imperial Federa-
tion will be the less of two evils. If it is to
be a question of Australia taking part in all
British wars without any voice in the making
of them, or of doing the same with a voice
in the making of them, the latter is
certainly the more dignified arrangement. If
it is also the more expensive—well, Aus-
tralia has risen up so extensively to declare
that it doesn’t mind the expense when the
interests of Britain are involved that it
can’t seriously object to that. If an arrange-
ment is made whereby Australia has to
supply troops as a matter of duty and plain
business, it has this advantage over supply-
ing them voluntarily—there will be less frill
about the proceedings, fewer speeches, less
pomp, glory, drink, banquet and upheav-
ing of small men to pose as the parties
who saved the empire because they
donated a horse with a dubious fore-
leg. And if Australia is going into the
contingent business as a continuous thing,
there is this much to be gained by sending
plenty of contingents : Where 1000 contin-
genters are liable to be offensively and arro-
gantly drunk for many days on their return,
40,000 of them would doubtless be reason-
ably sober because there wouldn’t be enough
people to buy drink for so large a number,
and being so much more common, they
would also be much less arrogant.
* » * •
In one way and another it certainly looks
just now as if the little Boer scramble was
going to produce a great many changes for
its size. Certainly it seems to have brought
the possibility of Imperial Federation nearer
than it ever was before. Australia at pre-
sent is in the mood to do almost anything.
By way of a casual sign of the times it
is worth noting that the A.N.A., that
organisation which is so surprised at being
born in its own country that it has formed a
great league and covenant by way of record-
ing f he fact, has began to talk over the
proposition that the time really has arrived
at last when a permanent legislative union
with Britain is called for. If the Salis-
bury Government had a feasible scheme of
Imperial Federation ready now, when the
popular excitement is still at red heat, almost
anything might happen—even an agreement
to pay £2,000,000 a year as our share of the
Imperial defence bill. Certainly if popular
frenzy keeps up to boiling point much
longer it is almost certain that Australia will
do something remarkable and permanently
unusual, and joining an Imperial Federation
looks about as probable as many other
follies.
Queensland—a Suburb of Asia.
Queensland just now is very like a meerschaum
pipe in the process of being colored. The popula-
tion was not long ago white, but the gradual
mixture of Malays, Chinese, Javanese, Japanese,
Kanakas, and the rest, is gradually turning it to
a warm brown chocolate color. A Geraldton
(N.Q.) correspondent gives an idea of how things
are done in that wettest township of Australia.
At Geraldton, on the Johnstone River, N.Q., there are
two main industries—first, that carried on exclusively by
Japanese, called by someone the oldest trade in the
world ; secondly, gambling, which is chiefly in the hands
of the Chinese, though the Malays have particular games
fancied by the Javanese coolies. The most profitable way
to use land is to rent it to Japanese or Chinese for either
of these industries. There are games for all tastes and
pockets. Fantan is for those who have received their
wages. But you can gamble with the smallest sums.
To meet the requirements of schoolboys there is a set of
tickets price only threepence. The prizes for these vary
from sevenpence for one lucky daub to ten pounds for
the whole ten. The schoolboy who gets sixpence hurries
to his favorite “John” and buys from him two tickets.
He comes to school through “ Chinatown ” in the morn-
ing, places his money at investment, and at the mid-day
recess calls on “John” to see if a dividend has been
declared. More often than not there is none ; but the
boy does not draw all blanks. About twelve months ago
a little boy’s mother gave him a shilling on his birthday.
With this he bought four tickets, of which one gained 355.
Train up a child in the way he shall go.
* • * *
The public school is in Chinatown, so that the boys may
not have far to go to the gambling-den. Within a hun-
dred yards of the school fence are at least seven gambling-
houses on one side of the street. On the other is a perfect
labyrinth of gambling-banks, Japanese temples of Venus,
and Malay boarding-houses. It is no unusual thing to see
numbers of boys in this maze. The Malays, less expert
in the art of fraud than the Chinese and Japanese, play
games which seem to give the novice a fair chance.
Accordingly, some Europeans may often be seen playing
with them, and the boys come to look on, and to be
initiated into all the mysteries of Asia.
* • * •
Young white girls love to watch the athletic contests
of the Japanese, and they do not all come from the im-
mediate neighborhood of “Chinatown.” Many are led
thither by the Salvation Army. Soon after dinner on
Sunday the big drum beats near the Japanese quarters,
greatly to the discomfort of the gamblers. As there ig
no Protestant church in Geraldton. many mothers who
would not otherwise think of allowing their children near
the Asiatic quarter on Sundays (they cannot help their
going there to school during the rest of the week) permit
them to go out with the “ Army.”
* * * *
There is in Geraldton a newspaper called the World
and, from the number of races that inhabit the town, the
name is not altogether inappropriate. The eloquent
editor is never weary of vaunting the natural beauties of
the country round. Nature has furnished Geraldton with
two hills ; upon one is the R.O. church ; on the other the
Joss House. From the Joss House you can see a prospect
which you cannot get from Sydney Post Office tower.
Thick as holes in an ant-bed are a collection of hovels, out
of which hundreds of black, yellow, and brown people
swarm. The cabins are all low, built of rough shingles
and the boards from old cases. Only in the street are
they roofed with iron ; at the back they are covered
with wood or grass. There are no windows.
* » • *
These huts are built most thickly along the banks of a
creek. The “ sanitary accommodation ”is ! Close
to the street the person going into one hovel rubs
against the office i belonging to the next. Two or
three buildings at the junctions of two streets are cut
off from the rest. Here four saplings are stuck in the
ground ; the roof consists of sheets of bark and the walls
of pieces of tents and bags. This serves for a Japanese
temple, a boarding-house out of which twenty Coolies
may sometimes be seen to come, and a Chinese' “ bank.”
It is commonly believed (though the writer cannot vouch
for it) that some of the land on which the buildings are
crowded most closely together belongs to the Q. Govern-
ment, and is leased to residents who sublet it to its
present inhabitants. Why not? What else does Boodle-
wraith exist for but to fill its friends’ pockets.
PLAIN ENGLISH.
A Piece of Paper With Some Ink On It.
Premier John Porrest, of Westralia, and his
mentor and blind guide, Hackett, both laugh de-
risively at the goldfields’ demand for fair Parlia-
mentary representation and at the Goldfields’
Separation movement, just as equally dull persons
laughed at the possibility of a revolution in
France, or of a locomotive travelling on rails. In
fact, everything new moves some dull person to
laughter. Forrest and Hackett, and the other
dull people, have a childish faith in the defensive
virtues of a bit of paper with some ink on it. The
W.A. Constitution says the Queen may grant
separation at her pleasure. The Colonial Boun-
daries Act says that separation shall in no case be
granted without the consent of the local Legisla-
ture —the one which is stuffed by the representa-
tives of F orrest’s pocket-boroughs. And Forrest
and Hackett, being confident that the latter
Act overrules the former, are content. If
the piece of paper with ink on it decides
in their favor they feel satisfied and safe,
and they go on refusing the goldfields even
a fifth of their fair share of Parliamentary
representation with a light heart. Yet the British
Parliament, which passed the Colonial Boundaries
Act, may also repeal it, and if sufficient occasion
arose it probably would do so. That is one
danger of which the two dull persons take no
sufficient account. And there is another one of
which they take no account at all. Underneath
the constitutional movement for Separation there
already exist the beginnings of another move-
ment. There always is one movement under-
neath another in suoh cases. If the British Par
As It Was in the Beginning.
Ablaze the Heavens shone with light and glory,
the happy laughter of an angel throng
Rang out among the hills of Paradise, and echoed
down the tide an angel’s song.
But Lucifer, black-browed and lowly muttering,
curseth the music of the heavenly choir.
“ Curse on this ceaseless calm, these fadeless
flowers—all, God ! that I might follow my
desire! ”
Straightway the music ceased, the glory faded,
the stricken daisies drooped upon the sod,
And the black shadow of a mighty sorrow sat
brooding on the face of Heaven’s God.
“ And what is thy desire, oh, Discontented—
what lackest thou for thy delight in Heaven ?
Speak thy desire, and, by the love I bear thee,
that which thou seekest shall to thee be given.”
Then Satan spake—“ These fair and fadeless
flowers, the changeless glory and the radiant
throng,
Are gall to me, oh dead and changeless ages ! Ah,
Gop ! ah, God ! it is for this I long—
To fashion for myself a world where beings shall
- battle on through years of changing life,
The strong to match the strong ; my being thrills
to watch the wondrous chances of the strife.
“Ah, for the spirit of a being striving to rise, to
conquer, and to overcome,
Not like this throng of angel hosts about thee
content to circle round one mighty sun ;
But I would hold a world of mine own making
where though a being climbed to dizzy height,
There still should something be to lure him on-
ward, some greater battle yet for him to
fight.”
“ Have then thy wish ” —the voice of God was
speaking—“have then thy wish and since it
must be so,
Lucifer, most proud and most beloved, the eyes
of God shall follow as ye go.”
Then Satan turned him from the home of God
with all its glory loathsome to his sight,
And through the crystal gates he shame-faced
strode into the outer darkness of the night;
There mightily he wrought with all his power till
earth and sea to their completion ran,
Until his dream sprang to fulfilment and a world
laid bounty at the feet of man.
Then Satan cried aloud in ecstacy: “Strive ye
and strain in all the ways of strife—
’Tis thus alone ye tread the paths of Gods, ’tis
thus alone ye know the gift of life.”
And so men strove, the strong still rising higher,
the weaker in the dust were forced to lie,
Until with breaking hearts they called for succor,
and calling vainly gave it up to die ;
Then God called unto Satan: “See ye this—is
this thy great dream’s thrice accursed goal ?
Lo, unto these who suffer in my sight I give the
gift of an immortal soul.
These mortals who have suffered for thy sake
when I did weakly yield unto thy pride,
Shall come unto the glory that ye scorned—and
not in vain shall they have lived and died.
“Time and again I came into their life —suffered
with them in all their narrow ways,
Tasted with them the bitterness of strife, and
feared with them the coming of the days,
As Buddha did I wander through the East,
teaching of Love unto the sons of men—
And when Prometheus suffered on the rock thy
God in mortal body suffered then ;
The Galilean, He whom men called Christ, who
yielded up His life in bloody pain,
He was thy God, and in the name of Love I yet
may come again and yet again.”
C.P.Q.C.


liament does not grant Separation and the Forrest
crowd still refuses the Goldfields anything dimly
resembling fair representation, the men who are
organising this second movement propose to get
Separation by the simple process of separating.
This is a chance which so far no one appears to
have seriously considered. The Goldfields have a
considerable population, and it has the great
advantage of being a concentrated population.
They could probably raise a larger force of large,
muscular men in a day than all the rest of W.A.
could get together in a week. If they got
really angry they could separate in an informal,
off-hand, low-comedy sort of way at any time, and
if John Forrest sent all his available forces
against them, they could either buy over the forces
by offering them a higher salary and an extra plug
of tobacco, or else disarm them and pour “ shy-
poo ” over them in sign of friendship. The engi-
neers of this subterranean idea are already taking
stock of the fact that the W.A. Contingent, now
away in the Transvaal acquiring a large and cheer-
ful contempt for peaceful methods, and helping to
assert the principle that the Outlander should have
equal Parliamentary representation with the Boer,
consists mostly of Outlanders, and it considers
that they may be put to unexpected uses when
they come back if the grievances of the W.A.
goldfields are then still unredressed. And, for the
rest, they go on the fact that, even without them,
the Goldfields could turn out enough real men to
take peaceful possession of the country and the
Parliament, and to disfranchise Bunbury and
Perth, and stand Fremantle up on its ear. And,
after all, the principal force that the Forrest
crowd has to set against this is a piece of paper
with some ink on it.
Wanted: A Stringent Building Act.
Spruson, M.P., has written to the N.S. Wales
Health Department, recommending the destruc-
tion of certain filthy hovels in his own electorate
—Gipps Division, Sydney. Now, “destruction”
in the matter of architectural obliteration means
the measure of justice meted out to absolute
unsafety—to filth, very, very seldom, though the
threatenings of the rat plague may operate in
wholesome terrorism in this connection, and impel
the authorities to condemn houses on the ground
of unsafety which are filthy but substantial. Syd-
ney is one of some dozen world-spread cities
which have grown up in the last hundred years,
and it is about the worst built of them all. A
crying need is a stringent Building Act, which
would give the central authorities absolute power
over the architectural present and future of the
city. The three requirements are safety, sanita-
tion, and beauty. No one should be allowed to
build as he likes. The horribly monotonous repe-
tition of terrace upon terrace, rows of exaggerated
pill-boxes in which dwell pallid people, ministers
■distinctly to the spirit of ugliness. The man who
■erects a shanty insults and outrages his neighbour
as much as if he struck him a blow on the eye ;
and the law should be put in operation to curtail
his opportunities for wickedness. In any closely-
packed Sydney suburb you will see, in row after
row, suffocating little houses, breathing into each
other’s faces, standing in undrained and unchan-
nelled streets, with ghastly back-yards arear, while
in front of each little man-kennel is a feculent
pool full of fever germs. The country around is,
perhaps, bleak, bare, and sandy, but adown the
slope over which the drainage runs, when the
stagnant pools overflow, the ground is green with
the greenness that grows only upon the grave.
But the crying residential horror is the wooden
house, the weatherboard cottage, always evidencing
the ravages of Decay’s enclutehing fingers, worm-
eaten and inflammable. The wooden tenement
is a relic of old colonial days, and a relic easily and
profitably to be dispensed with. All wooden
buildings should be pulled down, and none should
be permitted to be erected in the future. Brick
and stone should be the only materials used in
building, with as little woodwork as possible. The
abominable paling fence should be replaced every-
where with stone or urick walls, and should never
be repaired—and thereby perpetuated. Suburban
Sydney is a rats’ nest of insanitary, eye-affronting
wooden shanties, perfect fire-traps and life-traps.
It must be fresh in almost anybody’s recollection
that the terrible Redfern fire was nearly a holo-
caust of an entire district, and it broke out, and
was fed, by closely-packed wooden buildings.
The ideal Building Act should be given an
absolutely free hand in dealing with that suburban
monstrosity, the terrace. JSo contractor should
be permitted to lay a brick before the design is
approved by a central authority whose dictum in
matters of architectural taste should be final.
“Gonmade the country, and Man made the town,”
has been said with some approach to correctness.
As far as Sydney is concerned it might be
with greater truth remarked, “ God made
the Harbor, and Man spoiled the site.”
The people of Sydney breathe the atmosphere of
the commonplace, the tawdry, and the banal.
Indeed they are sunk so low in an aesthetic sense
that it is hardly possible to sink to a lower depth.
Their school-houses look like prisons, and child-
hood is from the very first immersed in something
like a cross between a lock-up and a fortress. The
young man and the young women are reared in
the weatherboard-cottage with the galvanized-
iron roof, or in the red-brick pill-box that forms
one of a dozen of similar pattern. What chance
has any sense of Beauty to develope in such
an environment ? Hideousness, squalor and in-
convenience are everywhere rampant; the while
things are going into a rut of petrified iniquity.
A stringent Building Act, stringently adminis-
tered, is the one thing needful by way of remedy.

Tasmanian Great Western Syndicate.
Premier Lewis on Tasmania’s Great Western
Railway syndicate:—
To see a railway connection between the southern por-
tion of Tasmania and the western mineral fields is one of
our sincerest wishes. The Great Western syndicate re-
ceived the right to construct this railway as long ago as
1898, and, at the same time, obtained liberal concessions
of land and of water power. It was then promised that the
money was available, hut up to the present little has been
done, and the construction of the railway seems as far off
as ever, while a large area of land has remained locked up.
Last session, the representative of the syndicate returned
from London, asking Parliament for certain amendments
to their Act and further rights, promising that, if
obtained, the company would proceed with the
work forthwith. These the Parliament sanctioned,
the company getting the most liberal treatment.
But even then they were not satisfied, and approached
the Government to take up £200,000 worth of debentures
on the terms of a prospectus which virtually put into the
promoters’ pockets almost a million of money in shares
and cash. The Government, though in no way fearing to
risk the payment of interest on the sum asked for, could
be no party to any such transaction, which might have
been used to induce others in England to take up the
debentures and shares upon the faith of the Government
becoming in a way partners with them. The concession
will lapse next August, unless the promoters commence
the work in a substantial manner, and in the event of the
■concession lapsing the Government will be prepared to
submit proposals to assist to a limited extent any bona
fidfe company formed solely for the purpose of construct-
ing the railway.
Spj according to Premier Lewis, he proposes to
begin fooling with another land-grant railway
syndicate, and all the bitter experience of the last
one is wasted on him. Which induces this paper
to arise for the severalth time and put this plain
business proposition to the men who do these
strange muddles on an island off the Victorian
coast. If a syndicate, which will < almost
certainly have to pay 5 per cent, interest
on its borrowings, can build the Great Western
Railway profitably, provided it gets a grant
of a million acres or some other amount of land to
help out its revenue, couldn’t the Tasmanian Rail-
way Department, which can raise money at 31 per
cent., build it a great deal more profitably if it got
the same grant of land to help out its revenue—
and then the country would get the profit ? Has
the Tasmanian railway management ever been so
much as asked if it could undertake to make the
line on these terms ? If the railway department
wouldn’t undertake to build the line profitably
without a land-grant, and the co. undertook to
build it loithout a land-grant, there would be every
reason to let the co. have the job. But to let the
co. build it ivith a huge land-grant because the
department can’t do it loithout one is a foolish,
illogical, rule-of-thumb proceeding, and an out-
rage on common sense.

Poor Higgins.
Last week’s discomfiture of Higgins was about
the most contemptible experience that a Vic.
Assembly has ever enjoyed. Five days previously
Higgins sprung a surprise upon the House by
giving notice of a motion to the effect that the
M‘Lean Govt., not being a representative Liberal
Govt., is unfit to hold office now that “the near
approach of the Federal elections renders of
supreme importance the solidarity of the Liberal
party.” The dismal equity lawyer was under-
stood to be taking action by arrangement
with ex-Premier Turner, and for the next three
days the M'Lean Ministry looked likely to die
young. Nobody could give any patriotic reason
why Higgins, after furiously hurling Iris “ humble
negative” at the Federation Bill and joyfully
assisting to throw Premier Turner out, should be
anxious to put Turner back again in the
interests of Federation, but on the other hand
nobody saw any particular reason why M 'Lean
and co. should not be thrown out in their turn,
therefore the intervention of Higgins was treated
as a deserving joke. But on the fourth day the
Tory Argus upset the Liberal show by reprinting
a speech uttered by hasty Higgins in the early
days of Contingent discussion, when it wasn’t
quite high treason to have doubts about the abso-
lute justice of the British cause in South
Africa and everywhere else. Obviously, the
present jingo “ feeling of the country” would
not tolerate Higgins after that. The re-
publication of his forgotten speech served to
remind the Liberal party that it could not con-
sent to get saved by the lawyer member for Gee-
long. There was no help for it but to repudiate
any verbal promise of support that Turner
or Peacock might have given. The safest Liberal
policy, under the circumstances, was to leave
Higgins to his fate. So when lie rose to attack
the M ‘Lean Govt, lie started with a confession of
his own absurdity in the matter, and went on to
admit he had merely united the Liberal party in
a stern determination not to support Higgins.
Then he was proceeding to withdraw his motion,
but some one pointed out to him that lie had
never moved the motion, and it didn’t need with-
drawing. So the motion withdrew itself, an* 1 the
M‘Lean Government still lives.

The Bushmen’s Muddle.
The New South Wales Bush Contingent is fast
getting beyond the stage of a public joke. Even
in its earlier and more respectable days the best
that could be said for it was that it was a clumsy
and stupidly mismanaged affair. At the beginning
the business of selecting the bushmen lay with men
some of whom didn’t know a bushman when they
saw one, and the business of selecting the horses
lay with men some of whom knew a horse with
reasonable certainty from a dog. Then there was
the demand for “ references” from squatters, and
some squatters began to pay off old grudges
against good bushmen who were unionists by
refusing them the almost necessary reference, and
giving it to cheap blacklegs whose chief
qualification was their willingness to work
below union rates. There was the rushing
of the Bushmen’s ranks by city hard
cases who knew little more of the bush
than can be seen at Moore Park ; the selec-
tion of men who could neither ride nor shoot, and
who paid other men to ride and shoot for them ;
the unspeakable muddlement which allowed this
personation to pass undetected until the public
taught the management something of its business
by pointing out the swindle ; and the appointment
of city lawyers and such-like as officers. There
was frantic confusion about money matters;
the persons who have charge of the fund sub-
scribed by the public didn’t see their way to part
with the precious coin for along time, and Sydney
was pervaded by penniless, accepted Bushmen
who couldn’t get a copper to go on with. There
was the other muddle by which men were ex-
amined and accepted at country towns, and gave
up their billets or sold off their properties and
came down to Sydney to “join”—only to
find that the Contingent was full and that
they had been accepted by mistake. But
the last muddle and disgrace of all is that the
inflated persons who are disbursing other people’s
money over this business reserve to themselves the
right to throw out, without any explanation, men
who have passed all tests satisfactorily and been
accepted. It doesn’t only reserve the right to re-
ject men who got in through personation in the
riding and shooting tests —these men are presum-
ably discharged with a very sufficient reason given
—but to throw out anybody and everybody, and
to do it without stating any reason. One man re-
cently ejected had given away to friends and
relatives nearly £2OO worth of property because
he couldn’t take it with him, and he had neither
time nor facilities for selling it except at a great
loss. He gave away, in fact, nearly all his worldly
belongings in his misguided enthusiasm for shoot-
ing at complete strangers in Africa. And he is
by no means a solitary case. The men who are
“ fired ” without visible reason seem to be mostly
alike in one respect —they are real bushmen who
have made disrespectful comments on the manage-
ment of the alleged bushmen who run the show. It
seems to be a case of men who can ride and shoot
getting the “ run ” for making remarks about the
unspeakable system which has filled the ranks
with men who can’t shoot and who can only fall
off. The great initial mistake lay in allowing any
private persons to play at raising troops. The
Government is the only institution which should
be allowed to raise troops. However badly it may
do it private enterprise does it a vast deal worse.

Seven Days Shalt Thou Labor—
And do all that thy master requires of thee—at his
price. This is the new Commandment which the
Seakable Victorian House of 48 Rich Land-
proposed to embody in the Tramways Amend-
ing Bill. After the Assembly had passed a mea-
sure providing for a week’s work of 48 hours, with
payment at time-and-a-ouarter beyond that limit
up to GO hours, the stolid Four Dozen shelved it
by postponing the matter pending inquiry. Then
the co.—apparently suffering from an unaccus-
tomed spasm of prodigality—offered to concede the
matter of overtime, at the said rate, provided it
was distinctly and concisely stated in the Bill that a
week’s work meant seven days of eight hours each.
The brassy assurance of the awful co. will be fully
perceived when its position is carefully considered.
Section G 2 of the 1833 Act states that the “ days of
labor shall be eight hours . . . but any person
may work overtime for special payment ” up to GO
houra per week. Also, managing-director Clapp
and secretary Spkigg both admitted in their evi-
dence before the Royal Commission of Enquiry
that the Act required an eight-hour day, but they
claimed a seven-day week, making 5G hours in the
week. This wa3 in face of the fact that the
old A.ct of Charles 11., of blessed memory,
prohibiting Sunday - labor, excepting works of
necessity or charity, was in force in Victoria in
’B3, and so remains to this day. The Sunday
trams can hardly be considered necessary as they
only start at 1.30 p.m., and the whole of
the charity in the case that charity which
always begins at home —is monopolised by the
directors and shareholders. Moreover, even
accepting Clapp’s statement of the case—a large
order—the co. has repeatedly broken its agree-
ment, for the Act stipulates eight hours per day
and that overtime may be worked for special pay-
ment, yet the co. compels its men to work 10
hours per day one week, and 9 hours per
week-day with G on Sunday the next.
Furthermore, it has coolly and persistently
ignored the “ special payment ” proviso; for
every trip that every employe does is paid for
at the one ordinary fixed rate, no matter whether
it be over the 48-hour or the SG-hour limit or not.
There is no such thing as “special payment” in
the tramway service. In making this extrava-
gant offer the perfidious co. said no word as to
wages, which would presumably remain as
at present; so that, practically, the men
would still be worked GO hours. The gaudy
offer amounted to an increase of wages of
£1 13s. 4d. per cent., on condition that the men
bound themselves to work seven days a week,
any week and every week, Sundays and holidays,
without rest or respite ! The House of Landlords
enthusiastically accepted the company’s proposal,
and returned the Bill to the Assembly, which
body, being in its right mind at the time,
perceived the insidious attempt to place a
seven-day-a-week law on the Statute Book and
insisted on the 48-hour clause. Thereupon Messrs.
Levi, Embling and Co. again returned the Bill
with scorn and contumely, with the 7-day clause.
The Assembly again stood to its guns and so the
Bill was lost. Which, under the circumstances,
was the bast tiling that could happen. It is better
that the men should work for a while, under pro-
test, as it were, an illegal GO hours a week, than
that the 7-day week should be legalised and the
Day of Rest abolished by Act of Parliament.

The Australian J.P.’s sympathy with crimes of
brutish violence is apparently incurable. A more
than usually remarkable case is reported from
South Melbourne. Three young fiends broke, late
at night, into tne house of an elderly man —a com-
plete stranger—stunned him with an iron bar,
knocked down his aged wife and kicked her till she
was insensible, and brutally assaulted his son. _ If
they had broken in with the apparent intention
of stealing something it would have been called
burglary, and they would probably have got five
years. If they had been found in the house after
breaking in, and had run away on discovery, with-
out showing any desire to steal anything, it would
have been assumed that they intended stealing
something, and they would almost certainly have
got two years. If they had been found in the
house—having got in without any visible break-
age—they would at least have been a case of
“illegally on the premises”; and that, as a
possible offence against property, is worth three
months any day. But there was an extenuating
circumstance in their case. They had nearly mur-
dered two aged people out of sheer brutality.
And in consideration of this redeeming feature
they were let off for burgling a house, for smash-
ing property, and for being illegally on the
premises, and two of them were fined £5
each for plain assault, while the third was
ordered to pay £l. Also there was £1 2s.
cost. J’s.P. Thistleth waite and Smith,
who sat on the bench, went out of their way to
denounce the assault as “most brutal and unpro-
voked.” Yet the three get far less punishment
for breaking into a house at night and committing
three fiendish assaults than they would be liable
to for breaking into a house at night and not
assaulting anybody. The assault is actually, in
the view of the dreadful Australian J.P., a virtue
which helps to counterbalance the offence of
breaking in.

Evex the once-democratic Adelaide Quiz has got
hold of the stupid Tory catchword of the moment.
It lias doubts about the loyalty of Basedow, M.P.,
and says:—
This is a serious accusation against a member of the
Legislature who dram money for his services from the
Queen he indirectly reviles..
And yet the Queen never gave Basedow or any
other politician a penny for liis services ; it is the
Australian taxpayer from whom they draw their
money. And everybody knows she never
gave them a penny. And the Queen isn’t
such an inflated fool as to say that she pays
Basedow or any other politician or public
servant. Also, the lie and grovel does her
no good ; probably it doesn’t even please her.
But it makes a lot of sensible people feel disgusted
at their grovelsome fellow-creatures, and some of
the disgust that is felt for the Tory idolator
reacts on his idol. The unmanly grovel of the
Tory crowd is the real cause of “disloyalty.”
The Queen is herself an unobjectionable
and harmless personage ; it is the people who
abase themselves before her who are objectionable.
The only way to keep a large section of the com-
munity from unmanly abasement is to remove
what it grovels to, and that is the chief reason why
republicanism exists. Also, it is the chief danger
ahead of royalty. Some day sensible “ loyalists ”
will have to consider the necessity for suppressing
extreme loyalists for their disloyalty, on the
ground that their intense worship and degradation
makes a very large number of people feel sick, and
thus indirectly undermines the Throne.

A N.S.W. teacher’s letter to Sydney Evening
News (13/2/’00):
Sir.—Can you inform me what is to be done with the
Public School Teachers’ Patriotic Fund ? If the men for
whose relatives the fund is being raised return safe and
sound, is the money to be returned to the teachers by
whom it was donated? I ask this, as I know some
teachers who, if they died to-morrow, could not leave £1
a week to their wives or little ones. Teachers are com-
pelled by the departmental authorities to pay into this
fund. I was speaking to a teacher, who has an invalid
wife and six children, and he has to pay rent and for the
service of a help in his home. For such a man to be
compelled to contribute to the Patriotic Fund means that
he is simply robbing his family by paying monthly dona-
tions, so that the families of the men in South Africa can
have U a week, while his own wife and children could not
have tl a week if he cannot leave it to them. These
women for whom the fund is being raised have free
quarters, and their husbands’ pay. Teachers are as loyal
as any other branch of the community, and are willin'*- to
donate to the best of their means. But why should
teachers be coerced any more than any other body of citi-
zens f Mr. Perry tells them that he will receive any
communication, anonymous or otherwise, of their loyalty
or their disloyalty ! The I.ynb Ministry is passing some
good measures, but acts like those 6f Mr. Perry blast
them in their infancy. It is a monstrous and iniquitous
piece of coercion on a body of free and loyal citizens
Yours, etc., ONE OF THEM.
When N.S.W. Parliament meets it would be
highly advisable to appoint some Judge of the
bupreme Court as a commission to ascertain
exactly what measures were used by the Cow-
Shaking Government to raise money among its
employes for the Patriotic Fund. The prospec-
tive widow of the Australian who is killed in
Africa is already one of the best-provided-for
widows in Australia (unless somebody embezzles
the fund), and if the Cow-Shaking crowd has been
m any way coercing ill-paid employes to stint their
wivesandchildren, and further diminish thealready
scanty provision which they can make for their
own prospective widows, in order to further ex-
pand the Fund, this striking evidence of patriot-
ism ought to lie made known. Also, if the teachers
in Minister Perry's department have been prac-
tically forced to contribute out of their small
salaries,-it ought to lie made known that Minister
PthillY has contributed an equally large proportion
of bis big one. How much was Mr. Perky’s own
donation ?

“ Zip ” complains :
One of the most foolish practices in connection with
the contingents is that of putting non-riding volunteers
in amounted troop. It is a cruelly obvious fact that an
awful percentage of those forming the mounted corps of
all the provinces have done all the riding they ever did
in camp, and they came there as innocent of horseman-
ship as a dead policeman. Yet these men are gaily in-
cluded in a force that has to depend entirely on its
horsemanship for its efficiency and usefulness. The
Imperialist johnnies are wondering in a hazy sort of way
why it is that Australia has not been extensively used as
a recruiting ground for light mounted infantry, and they
prophecy that, in the near future, this class of troops will
be largely recruited from here. But their specula-
tion and prophesy would be squashed beyond recognition
if they were afforded a glimpse of the ordinary
flying Gilpin at riding exercise. To see an embryo
Roberts on a charger with his concertina trousers
divorced from his socks by several inches of calf, holding
manfully on to one rein and the crupper, and with a
fierce determination to sacrifice every inch of skin to the
saddle rather than fall off, is a sight guaranteed to
remove the aforesaid haziness. Putting this well-
meant apparition on a horse is simply adding in a
criminal manner to his chances of becoming food for a
Boer bullet, because a rifle is an entirely superfluous
article when all his energies and most of his available
arms and legs are occupied in frantically retaining his
seat on a bumping quadruped.

“ The war is causing a fierce war here,” writes
The Bulletin’s London correspondent, “and the
result, thank the gods of all creeds and races, will
surely be the reconstructing out of sight, and
memory even, of the fungi-grown, senilely-
doddering War-office—the red-tapeworm of Bri-
tish Government offices. Its maxim has been
during this campaign (1) conceal reverses, (2) tell
a portion well sub-edited, (3) and lie about the
rest. ’Tis certainly a blind ill-managed parent
that Australia is backing up with her men and
money, and even the most patriotic here are so
utterly disgusted that they do not know whom of
those in authority to rend first. Meanwhile the
theatres have slumped like dust before a ‘ brick-
fielder.’ The Savoy, Cecil, Carlton and other big
cafes and hotels are practically empty. The halls
with programmes full of patriotic numbers, are
trying to keep one hair on top of a depressed bead
above water; and the whole feminine world is knit-
ting soldiers’ night-caps and socks what time hus-
bands and others eat half-cooked meals to the tune
of * The Absent-Minded Beggar.’ ”

It apparently never occurs to Westralian Premier
Forkkst and his owner, Hackett, that while
their anti-Federal policy and their policy of
refusing anything like adequate Parliamentary
representation are driving the goldfields on to
desperate efforts at separation, they are them-
selves fooling with nothing less than absolute ruin.
A favorite theory of the anti-Federal party is that
the goldfields can’t separate, because, if they did,
the new province would be a province without an
adequate water-supply anywhere within its
borders. But, even then, its position would be
vastly better than the old province from which it
separated. Water can generally be found some-
where ; money, as a rule, can be found nowhere.
Such a vast proportion of Westralia’s borrowed
money has been, spent on works in the older parts
of W.A. that, if separation took place and the
debt was divided, the bit of W.A. that was left
would be utterly ruined. The revenue would
mostly go with the goldfields. The trade would
mostly go to new ports, and leave Perth and Fre-
mantle deserted. Perth would decay again to its
old status as an overgrown village mooning drearily
by the Swan River. The mint would be deserted.
The railways, of the old province would cease to
pay. Bunbury would perish, or nearly so.
Foil rest’s property and the bricks and mortar
of brother Alice would slump hideously. The
old Westralia would bo left with a huge debt and
practically no revenue. It would be the most
utterly ruined province in Australasia, if not the
most utterly ruined State in the Southern hemi-
sphere, with less resources than it had in the days
before gold was discovered, and about four times
more liabilities. Compared to its difficulties in
the way of finding money, the difficulties of the
new Westralia in the way of finding water would
be nothing, or less than nothing. The Forrest
crowd, in persistently kicking its only real asset,
the goldfields, is fooling with the worst kind of
ruin that ever threatened an Australian province.
And it does it gladly, cheerfully, out of pure blind
cussedness, with the fatuousness of that class
which rushes in where angels fear to tread, and
the happy smile of the drunk who smokes on 9
barrel of gunpowder.

SUNDRY SHOWS.
SYDNEY SHOWS FOR COMING WEEK.
Her Majesty’s “ Henry V."
Royal “ Trilby."
Lyceum “ It’s Never Too Late to Mend."
Criterion Rickards’ Co.
MELBOURNE SHOWS FOR COMING AVEEK.
Princess’s “ Little Red Riding' Hood."
Theatre Royal “ Fun on the Bristol.”
Bijou Rickards’ Variety Co.
Alexandra “ Briton and Boer.”
“Trilby” replaced “ Tess ” at Sydney
Royal last Saturday, owing, the programme says,
to circumstances which make the occasion an
extremely interesting one to playgoers. As there
is nothing in the performance about the Bush-
men’s Contingent this remark is less understand-
able than it might be. Trilby is the girl we are
all getting: used to who appears in Act I. fur-
nished with that necessary equipment for a
modem heroine a Past. Tess’s Past being
only a skimpy little affair she is repudiated
a good deal on all sides, but Trilby’s personal
charms being of a wider scope she has four
or five persons offering respectful marriage to
her ; likewise Little Billee, who is almost another
person. Which is a lesson to the rising woman to
go in for the thing properly as only a woman with
a good strong Past is capable of any Future. The
Girl With a Past is becoming so commonplace a
feature of modern drama that this scribe sees the
time approaching when the dramatist who intro-
duces a neroine without one will be hailed as a
brilliant and original genius, and people will dis-
cuss in newspapers whether Girls Without Pasts
are really like that and can possibly be as interest-
ing and agreeable as they are depicted on the
stage. Trilby, chewing bread and sausage and
waving white feet in the air, is a more natural
kind of character than Tess, but she is set in less
natural surroundings. Her picture-frame is a
certain atmosphere of virtuous immorality and
chaste vice peculiar to the Latin Quarter —as
known to fiction. The Latin Quarter as known in
real life is said to be a good deal more sordid.
* * • *
Most of the characters are mentioned in
the programme as first-timers, which probably
accounts for the joyous freshness of the grisette
dance in Act II. —the sort of dance which makes
young skittish maidens of 40 long to break from
the decorous d.c. and join in the furore. Tyrone
Power has the glittering eye and the dirt and the
sardonic laugh and the tree-top hair of Svengali all
right. His complexion boasts the waxy whiteness
of the girl who eats chalk to make herself look
nice; the girl you read about in those solemn
little pamplilets which tell girls not to do things
which they never dreamt of doing, but proceed to
do after they have been told not to. The villain-
ous laugh of Svengali is different from the villain-
ous laugh of Alec D’Urberville : the actor who can
sport several varieties of villainous laugh has a
great future just in front of him. Also, Tyrone P.
Svengali has a certain baleful vigor which makes
him really impressive. Miss Edith Crane gets
curiously away from the Trilby likeness in the last
acts, when she wears a white silk dress of such
length that she walks up and down it several
times after she has been reproved by Small Bill.
For the rest, she is realistic, and sports two nice
feet, and finally dies pleasantly by slow upheavals
on to her drawing-room sofa. If she had lived she
would have had to marry Little Billee. So she
died.
* • » •
Mr. Atholwbod, as Gecko, sends a distinct
wave of pathos across the footlights, and Cecil
Ward gives a neat representation of the stage
Frenchman who breaks out into “Tra-la-la” on
small provocation. Trilby’s voice—which is, this
time, Miss Maud Dalrymple—is a nice enough
organ, but when one considers that a woman who
could anyway represent the voice the Svengalised
Trilby is supposed to possess, wouldn’t be letting
it out eight times per night as the mere accessory
to a medium drama, there is a fair argument for
dropping the song out altogether, and letting it
be as inaudible as the songs of the other singers.
They perpetrate unheard music, and why Trilby
should be audible while they are not is a thing
wrapped in gloom. Besides the voice, this paper
advocates the heaving out of Little Billee. There
has never been a satisfactory Billee. The audi-
ence refuses to take Trilby’s fiancee seriously. In
fact, he is a character so difficult that there is
probably only one man in the world who can
play him really well, and that man hasn’t been
found yet. But The Bulletin would like to see
George Rignold try the part.
