Her Majesty’s (Sydney) is closed this week
but there is to be a great re-opening on Saturday.
On that occasion the oft-going-to-depart George
Rignold appears once more in a farewell season of
“ Henry V.” with Misses Linda Raymond and
Emily Hughes, also Howard Vernon, George
Laun, and other favorite artists, in the cast.
* * * -#
At Sydney Lyceum the management con-
tinues to put the right man rightfully into prison,
after which he retrieves the past by finding a
large and yellow nugget, which is so obviously on
the surface of the ground all the time that the
wonder is no one ever found it before. The sort
of nugget which lies about in this casual fashion is
liable to grave suspicion, as the nugget of real life
is of a more retiring disposition, and requires
a liberal amount of digging. There is this
reliable difference between the real and the drama-
tic heap of gold: the stage heap is there, and
the real one, as a rule, isn’t. The hero, besides
his easy find, is likewise to be congratulated on
having imported a large-size American log hut
into his Australian settlement. Why an Austra-
lian should build his hut on an American plan is
another mystery. Charles Reade’s story is
usually remembered by reason of an unclerical
clergyman, who tries the pi-isoner’s punishments
to see if they are bearable or not, and beguiles the
prisoner in the Dark Room by conversation
through the key-hole; but to an Australian
audience the play depends largely on the mopoke,
and the laughing jackass, and the large yellow
nugget aforesaid. These and various other details
will probably keep it going for some time yet.
** * *
At Sydney Criterion, Miss Rochester is
doing good work with a full, rich voice, and Phil.
Goer takes the place of Irving Sayles. The
Caddam Woods revels benefit by the change.
Charles Seel, the man with the loose jaw, seems
looser in the jaw than ever now. The Macarte
Sisters show great physical strength and molar
endurance, but the memory of the three pretty
Keziahs is still fresh, and the audience doesn’t
enthuse over the square-built sistern as it might
otherwise do.
** * m
The gentleman who makes more or less
lightning changes at the Criterion Theatre is as
toothless as a three-weeks’ child. Rumor says
that Ludwig voluntarily gave up a fine set of
teeth in order to facilitate change of facial expres-
sion. He also possesses a glittering glass eye, but
it is not stated whether that also was assumed in
the cause of his art, after the manner of the classic
philosopher who blinded himself in order to think
better. By the way, the Criterion audience is
getting quite mild in its loyalty. On Thursday
night, last week, it didn’t favor Ludwig’s Kruger
with a single hiss, while, at his representation of
Queen Victoria, only a fourth of the audience
tumbled to their feet, and half of these pretended
they got up to look for their hats or admire the
view. It was a pacific audience ; however, it hit
Harry Rickards with a penny after he had told it
not to, so that is something.
*# * *
“Briton and Boer,” adapted in some
places from Rider Haggard’s “Jess,” and partly
made up of recent war journalism and popular
British theories of cause and effect in Krugerland,
has come to fill a want at Melbourne Alexandra.
Seriously speaking, the one-sided want that Dam-
pier’s hotch-potch supplies is a deplorable develop-
ment of “ patriotism.” Audience ought to hunger
for truth and justice in stage dealings with the
Boer question—but they don’t. Moreover, they
would not recognise the truth if it were put before
them by a Dutchman or an archangel; nor is there
a Dutchman (or Britisher) living who could write
an unprejudiced drama on the question aforesaid.
Consequently, Dampier’s topical fiction, in five
acts and numerous discharges of firearms, is a
pardonable outrage committed from respectable
business motives. It will pay for its powder and
smoke, anyhow.
* * • •
The Boers at the Unlock include an abbre-
viated Paul Kruger, and a perky little Cronje—
Black Cronje on the bill—who makes himself
absurdly unpleasant, but often gets belted for his
pains. Also, there is a gory Boer-villain engaged
in capturing the enemy when the enemy is not
taking him prisoner, and the great ambition of
this sanguinary battler is to carry Jess’s younger
sister by storm. Meanwhile, Dam pier stutters
amidst the mystery in the kliarki character of a
sublime Bwitish idiot engaged to a widow
in the ambulance department, and when
lie discovwers that the lady is not suffer-
wing from a cork leg he loves her all the inoah,
doneherknuw. Somehow the war in South Africa
gets tangled up with the escape of Jess and her
Anglo-Australian brother-in-law-elect, owing to an
unnatural rising of the Tugela River at the insti-
gation of General Joubert, whereupon the wicked-
est of the Boer spies is shot by mistake, and the
relict, who hasn’t a cork leg, smites Cronje’s nose
with a rolling-pin. The last act but one was not
over before 11 o’clock on Saturday, so the adven-
tures of Jess and her sister’s young man have
probably been curtailed. As a matter of fact,
Jess makes the war go slower than one cou!d
wish. She seems to hamper the British advance on
Pretoria and delay the arrival of the Victorian
contingent, which is the signal for the Boer forces
to surrender. All the same, the gallery-patriots
are content to overlook the gloomy heroine for the
sake of British valor and the tumultuous foolish-
ness of proceedings in general. They feel that
they are getting full value for cash. Miss Lily
Dampier, who makes a rather startling re-appear-
ance as a plump, matronly Jess, is vaguely under-
stood to sacrifice her affections in the interests of
sister Rose. Mrs. D. is the widow with two
genuine legs, and Alfred Rolfe, F. Appleton, and
John Forde are frequently busy during the cam-
paign. Oom Ned Holloway perpetrates a pass-
able caricature of Kruger down to the waist, and
young Howard Smith is a clever-spirited libel on
Cronje. Also George Buller, as a Kaffir who
swears weird vengeance on his Boer boss, deserves
favorable mention in The Bulletin’s despatches.

Mrs. Andrews, interesting little advocate
of woman’s right to be worshipped by her fellow
man, will give another Dickens’ Recital at the
Athenaeum Hall on Monday (26th). Mrs. A. has
been keeping dark for quite a long time. It seems
years since she discussed Paul Dombey, William
Sikes and co. on the same platform, and got the
sweetest of notices for her highly-respectable
effort.
#* * *
Widow O’Brien, alias J. F. Sheridan, a
woman with a well-known Australian past, will
resume her old place in the rocking-chair at Melb.
Royal next Saturday. Sheridan disguised as a
man has been only a moderate success, and the
departure of “ The New Barmaid ” on Friday will
not agitate Melbourne to any extent. The frigid
respectability of the young person who sells drinks
across the bar is disappointing to a city which had
reckoned on getting a tow-haired Tottie of doubt-
ful character and alcoholic tendencies. As a
novice at the business, she is not altogether untrue
to nature, but the public would prefer a well-
seasoned siren, with a bright mechanical wink in
her other eye. An unsophisticated barmaid is
merely a sad mistake that soon profits by experi-
ence, and in comparison with her older and
tougher self she is not worth staging.

“ Little Red Riding Hood,” as mentioned
last week, is to appear at the Princess’s on Satur-
day (24th) under Williamsonian management.
Very few weeks back J. C. Williamson took a long
farewell of his old home, but Melbourne will feel
none the less glad to see his big show because it
was not expected. And the fact that it has been
running since Xmas in Sydney amounts to an
ample apology for the pantomime being late.

The Transvaal War pictures have come out clearer
since the first night, and the unpleasantness of
getting wounded and bandaged for the country of
one’s alleged ancestors, is now shown with con-
siderable effect on the screen.
“ Quex ” : Poor Lambie was so eager to
get on the warpath that refusal of his services
would have broken him up. The papers will pro-
bably find it necessary to impress upon their men
the necessity for self-preservation. A war-re-
porter’s first duty is to furnish “ copy ” for his
paper, and he can’t do that when he’s dead.
“ I d(Ri’t want an editor who is a thinker,
or a writer, but one who can put commonplace
ideas in pompous language.”— Proprietor. “To
begin at the beginning is not a good maxim in
journalism.”— Smalley. “ A journalist is a man
who prints ink on paper.”— Bismarck. “Journal-
ism is good if you can get out of it soon enough.”
—Thiers.
The Royal Agricultural Society of N.S.W.
is goiim to have a glorious show this year; and
Sec. W ebster has already grown several inches in
the chest through adequately describing the im-
mensity and splendor of the attractions he offers.
And the least thing that this country can do is to
gratefully attend in a body and pay, pay, pay
every day, for 10 days, at the gates ; or grab some
of the £4OOO to be given away in prizes. Cham-
pion horses and cattle will receive splendid prizes;
and the harmless but necessary pig, fowl, and dog
will receive the ribbons and medals where deserv-
ing. Also a wood-chopping contest is down for
those who believe in Gladstone’s motto—an axe at
any price.*
It’s sickening to hear the whoop now for
Tommy Atkins : he’s a hero, a little tin god. But
in the piping time of peace no respectable English-
man would be seen in his company. The finger of
every loafer is pointed at him as the fellow too lazy
to work. In his own country, pubs openly refuse
to serve him, and won’t allow him about the place
lest he, being an infected animal, should drive away
trade. Eating houses ditto. Certain parts of the
theatre are barred him. No respectable girl, if she
wishes to keep her place, dare be seen with him ; if
she marry him she is disgraced—far better to have
married a sweep. His pay barely keeps him, and
if he has the cheek to marry, his wife must work
(wash or char) to keep herself. And when this
war is over he will again be the pariah and moral
leper he was after every other war. The thin red
line is a fine thing in poetry, but in real life when
no war is on, the average Englishman wouldn't
touch it with a long pole.
Sydney Daily Telegraph’s San Francisco
correspondent writes that the South African war
occupies first place in American public attention
and that—
English Conservatives are deceiving themselves as to
the sentiments ot the American people. Undoubtedly in
circles close to the Administration, for special reasons,
and among the financial element, whose interests are
closely affected by the events in the Transvaal, sympathy
is felt with the British Government. There, however,
such sentiments cease. The resistance opposed by the
Boers has aroused the latent feeling in favor of the Repub-
licans, until it is now dominant throughout the country.
Within the last week the subject has come up in Congress,
and two resolutions—one antagonistic to Great Britain,
and the other favorable to the Boer cause—have
been adopted. Senator Hale, of Maine, was the author
of one of these resolutions, and, in supporting the second,
made a speech which has attracted general attention
throughout the States. Hale took direct issue with
Secretary Balfour that the South African war had knit
together every branch of the English-speaking race. “ I
deny, sir,” declared Hale, with great feeling, “ that the
American section of that race is in sympathy with Great
Britain in the South African war to stamp oiit the liberty
of a people. I deny that the American people are to be
tied to the chariot-wheels of war against the South
African Republics. And when the leader of the
Conservatives in the House of Commons asserts
that he should he met by some disclaimer from
this side of the Atlantic.” Hale, who had asserted his
belief that the war being waged by Great Britain was the
most fatal blow that had been struck at human liberty
in this century, went further than to deny American
sympathy with the war on the Transvaal, placing its
entire responsibility on Joseph Chamberlain and his re-
puted interested backers. “ I do not believe,” said
Senator Hale in conclusion, “ that the English people are
in favor of this war. I believe that the great Queen, on
bended knees, has prayed that the war might be averted.
I do not believe that the great Premier of England favored
the war. It was the act and movement of a sharp Cabinet
Minister, engaged with gold speculators, who favored the
war.” A pro-Boer meeting of large proportions was held in
Washington, at which many men prominent in the nation
were active or passive participators.
The WILD CAT
COLUMN
Owing to Absent-Minded Beggary and other
things several provinces are in desperate need of money
just now, and Maoriland has also sold £300,000 worth of
4 per cent. Treasury bills with a two years* currency in
London. But the strange thing is that Maoriland realised
£IOO 7s. 6d. per £IOO, whereas, for exactly similar securi-
ties with the same currency, New South Wales only got
£99 10s. and Westralia£9B. So either Maoriland managed
things much better or else the London market estimates
that \\ estralia’s credit is good enough to borrow at £5 per
cent., N.S.W.’s at £4 ss. 3d., and Maoriland’s—the best of
all—at about £3 16s. 3d. Yet Maoriland is evidently also
afflicted with the idea that the local investor is a poor,
mean, common animal who shouldn’t expect to get as
much interest on his money as the English one. When
the Maorilander has money to invest, the Government is
willing to sell him Consols bearing 3.1 per cent, interest,
and even of those it won’t sell more than £250,000 worth
in any one year. But when it wanted to raise money at
4 per cent, to aid the Bank of New Zealand it put the
loan on the London market, and now that it has a security
bringing in £3 16s. 3d. per cent, to dispose of that also
goes to London. The idea of letting the local outcast
have a chance to buj' the high-interest-bearing securities
never occurs to the Seddon Government. And it never
seems to occur to any other Government, either. In S.A.
Harassed Holder is offering 3 per cent, for 12 months
loans, and that weary local preacher man couldn’t get
them in London under 4 per cent.
» * * *
At the half-yearly meeting of the Queensland
National Bank, recently held in Brisbane, it was men-
tioned that next July or thereabouts the first repayment
of 6d. in the £ is to be made to private reconstructed
depositors. This won’t reduce the visible liability to
private depositors as shown in the balance-sheet; it is in
reduction of the ss. in the £ which was nominally written
off the old reconstructed private deposits to make the
accounts look a little more like square, but which is
to be repaid out of profits—the liability which isn’t put
among the liabilities in the balance-sheet, in consequence
of which the liabilities in the balance-sheet are not the
real liabilities or anything like them. It was also men-
tioned that the directors had voted on their own responsi-
bility £250 of the money which the widows of some
rained shareholders are in such need of to help the widows
of Queenslanders who may be killed shooting at perfect
strangers in South Africa. They didn’t deduct this from
their own fees apparently. And there was a small row
about London proxies. The London proxies, it seems,
are cabled out in cypher to the directors. They are not
submitted, like other proxies, to the scrutineers before
they are used in the election of directors, that the scruti-
neers may guarantee that they are all right. No under-
standable reason is assigned for the refusal to let them be
inspected. And these more or less concealed proxies ap-
parently make it possible for the directors, in almost
every case, to re-elect the retiring directors and appoint
the auditors and run the whole show on their own.
Which is a solemn state of things for an y depressed or
low-spirited or suspicious shareholder to think over. The
directors, of course, believe that there is no reason why
anyone should suspect their management of being in-
capable. Which is true. There was also no reason to
suspect the last management of being incapable till it was
too late.

The Sydney lan* 1 -dealing co. of Richardson and
Wrench, which sells j our house for you if it is at all sale-
able, reports a half-year’s profit to 31st December, 1899,
of £2071. A dividend at the rate of 5 per cent, per an-
num amounts to £ISOO ; another £SOO is written off good-
will, and the remaining £7l, added to £343 previously on
hand, leaves £4L4 to carry forward. When this concern
was floated into a company in 1889 with a capital of
£OO,OOO, all paid-up, almost its only visible asset was the
goodwill of the great land-dealing business carried on by
the old private firm of Richardson and Wrench. This ac-
counted for £57,5u0 of the £OO,OOO. It has now been re-
duced to £51,900, so £SOOO has been written off in 10J
years. As the paper asset diminishes and the visible
assets correspondingly increase (the co. has now £11,715)
accumulated cash), Richardson and Wrench, Limited,
should be an improving concern, unless something very
bad and unexpected happens to the land and house deal-
ing business.

A little insurance concern, not much known
outside the circle of its own business, is the Australasian
Plate-Glass Insurance Cos., (head office, Melbourne). The
paid-up capital is £SOOO, and there is a reserve-fund of
£2OOO, the result of 31 years careful and unobtrusive busi-
ness. For 1899 the receipts (including £372 from interest
on investments) were £2768. Losses were £745 and
expenses £IOOS, leaving a profit of £lOlB, equal to over 20
per cent, on the paid-up capital. The usual dividend of 8
per cent, is declared, absorbing £4OO. Altogether a solid
and prosperous little company.

A revival appears to be in store for the Mt.
Wills district of Victoria. The Maude and Homeward
Bound United Mine is developing well; so much so that
the management has decided to adopt crushing and con-
centrating appliances of its own. Their mill will comprise
ten head of stamps, classifiers and “Luhrig” Vanners
(supplied through VV. and J. Lempriere, of Melbourne, the
general agents for “ Luhrig ” Appliances). The mineral-
ised stone is rich ; recent concentrates averaging as high
as 5oz. gold per ton.*
• * * *
The Great Boulder Co., Kalgoorlie, have just
placed an order with the Austral Otis Engineering Co.,
Ltd., South Melbourne, to build a pair of compound con-
densing engines of 700 h.p., to drive their extensive mil-
ling plant. These engines will be of the latest type, with
Proell valve gear. The flywheel is 20ft. in diameter,
grooved for 16 ropes, and weighs upwards of 22 tons. The
total weight of engine will be 85 tons. The engines are
to be completed by the end of the year and will be the
finest and largest plant, on the field. Three engines under
construction at the Otis Works for the Kalgoorlie Gold-
fields have an aggregate of 1800 horse power.*

It is only necessary to read the reports to learn
what horrible cats the Victorian London-owned mines are.
Should London pull out no better with the Deep Lead
Mines —the Victoria Estates, etc. —it will soon be impos-
sible to float a Victorian mine in London, and the sooner it
becomes impossible the better. An enormous amount of
money is being spent on these alluvial properties in pump-
ing and other plant. The London and Globe Finance Co.
(Whittaker Wright’s concern) has taken a hand in them,
but even its failures are more numerous than its suc-
cesses. N.S.W. is very little better ; Myalls United is
working at a loss. Gallymont is a fairly good thing for
the tributors, but has been a terrible disappointment to
the co. Gibraltar, although turning out about a thousand
ounces a month, is making no money. The Wentworth
Mines seem to have pretty well petered out. There are a
number of others which never were any good from the
start, and are still in that unsatisfactory condition ; why
they continue working is a mystery.

Of £loo,ooo—and £1.112 brought forward makes IS,-36
available. Very few concerns in the precarious, wool,
cow, and pastoral property business can show returns
even distantly approaching this nowadays. A dividend
of 8 per cent, absorbs £BOO3 ; £4OOO is written off the
valuation of the co.’s freehold property, which makes a
total writing-down of £26,014, and reduces the paper
value of the property to £153,500; £2500 is added to
reserve fund, making it £60,000 ; and £4236 is carried
forward. This concern paid 10 per cent, from its incep-
tion in 1882 to 1885 ; 6 per cent, from 18S6 to 1804 ; 7 per
cent, in 1595, and 8 per cent, since then. The chief visible
reason for this prosperity and the absence of the usual
reconstruction troubles is that Harrison, Jones, and Dev-
lin had no debenture-holders to reconstruct; it does a
small and highly profitable business on its own capital
instead of a large and ruinous one with a million or two of
borrowed money. Its chief drawback seems to be that it
started with ail immense mortgage on its premises—as
late as 1593 this liability stood at £IIO,OOO ; and though it
has since been reduced out of profits to £87,000 it is a
healthy mortgage even now. And another curious feature
is the paper asset “Goodwill £40,000.” Certainly there
is a reserve-fund of £60,000 to set against it, but why the
co. doesn’t write off the foolish paper asset, and call its
reserve-fund £20,000, which it really is, passes under-
standing. Goodwill as an asset is one of the most unsatis-
factory things in business. A company seldom wants to
sell it till it is winding up, and the only time this asset is
really wanted it isn’t there.

The Carlton Brewery (Melbourne) showed a
profit for 1899 of £15,524 —equal to the splendid return of
almost 60 per cent, on the written-down capital of about
£27,000. The last three years’ profits have been :
1897 £3550 1899 £15,524
IS9B £5646
This is dating from the time when the Carlton had its
great cleaning-up. Since then the improvement has
been very rapid, and the almost-lost institution has been
rapidly getting on its feet again. The trouble ahead is
that the Carlton owes some £785,389 to debenture-holders
and mortgagees ; the debentures, which are the largest
part of this huge debt, mature in 1902 ; and the
co., by reason of the enormous depreciation in
the boom-burst days, hasn’t anything like £500,000
worth of assets just now. If the debenture-holders want
their money in 1902 it is dead certain they can't get it. If
they are content to renew for 29 years or so, anil take the
interest regularly in the interim, it is almost dead certain
that they will get their money. The accumulated profits
and the probable recovery in the value of the big co.’s
great mass of depreciated public-houses would put things
right by then, or some years later, or sometime. So far,
however, the debenture holders have proved themselves a
hard case to deal with.
The Carlton Brewery Co.’s present terribly top-
heavy position is as follows :
Liabilities. Assets.
Capital, paid-up .. £27.072 Cash, Plant, I’roper-
Debentures .. 680,084 ties, kc £458,716
Mortgages.. .. 104,705 Shortage .. .. 386,581
Sundry Debts .. 32,836
£845,297 £845.297
The co. makes its shortage much less on paper bv insert-
ing a paper asset— “ Goodwill, £150,000,” but. Ihe good-
will of a concern which is in such st raits is a thing about
which the less said the better. The real shortage is
£386,581. A year ago it was £402.105, and two years ago
it was about £408,000, so there is a steady and very
material improvement. If the co's creditors give it time it
can evidently work off the deficit by degrees ami pay
everybody, if they don’t give it time, and there is an
immediate realisation, they will prohahh get about 10s.
in the £, and they will lose much more than the share-
holders. whose capital is nearly ail lost already. It is
certainly a rather curious situation for a brewery which
is earning the splendid profit of nearly 60 per cent, to be
thus tottering on the verge of utter bankruptcy.

(Melbourne Age, by the way. in one of its wilder moments,

enough, though even they might be worse. The gross
profits were £61,477, and ordinary working expenses were
£62,581, so ends would very nearly have met had it not
been for the heavy burden of £22,500 forinterest at 4.) per
cent, on £500.000 of debentures. This debt was incurred
in 1888, when the concern was floated into a company
twice as large as the business warranted, if M’t'rai ken’s
had remained just the same humble concern it was it
would have been a paying affair even in most of the
recent bad years. Or if the shareholders had put up
£500,000 more capital instead of burdening themselves
with a huge liability which must be met in good times or
bad, their brewery would have paid its way and given a
small dividend occasionally, with a prospect of improve-
ment later on.
After ’9(5 it was decided to have a general clean-
ing-up, and £250,000 —half the paid -up capital—was
written off to provide for bad debts, losses, and deprecia-
tion. Since then another loss of £32,060 Itas accumu-
lated. The only apparent hope lies in paying off some of
the debentures, overdrafts and mortgages—4J per cent,
debenture-interest is a hopeless burden t hese times, and
presumably the overdraft and mortgages are at an even
heavier rate—.so the call of £250,000 has been made with
another call of £250,000 in prospective. The first call of
£250,000 has so far produced rather less than £BO,OOO. and
quite possibly it will not yield much more. A more or
less reliable Melbourne paper talks of the next call of
£250,000 yielding£so,ooo in all. There has also been talk
of raising £40,000 by an issue of preference shares, lint
the trouble is that all these devices are quite insufficient.
They aren’t likely to produce over £250,000, and over
£660,000 is wanted. The balance-sheet can be compressed
as follows:

During its life of a little over 20 years the
Bendigo branch of the Miners’ Association has returned
to its members £39,794 in accident pay; £10,384 has gone
to the widows and children of members who have died
from injuries received in mines ; and strikes have ab-
sorbed only £7988. A great part of the last-mentioned
has been in respect of troubles outside the district.
Star of the East (Sebastopol, Vic.) which paid
its last dividend, if writer remembers aright, in March
’95, now makes its 123rd call (3d.). Also it is reported :
“ Stone somewhat below the average.” Yet the average
has been low enough of late.
* * * *
There is trouble brewing around the well-
boomed Waverley (N.Q.) tin-mines. Companies only
employing enough men to hold ground. No sinking
is being done, for an obvious and rather painful reason :
the lodes apparently don’t go down, and what there is on
the surface has been much overestimated both as regards
quality and quantity. Locally it is commonly believed
that the machinery will never be erected, and the bottom
must drop out of the show soon. Writer knows the
ground ever since the prospectors found it, years ago, and
barring a few rich surface patches, there never was any-
thing to warrant large expenditure, especially as the in-
credible roughness of the country and the out-of-the-way
position of the mines makes working extremely costly.
For instance, carriage front nearest railway-station £8 per
ton ; only water about 800 feet below mines in a creek ;
no grass for miles around (even now in the wet), and no
timber handy. Hene Tekel 1 Also Upharsin 1 Likewise
Ichabod 1

Latest Q. gold rush is 25 miles south of
Coen, and C'oeti is somewhere up Cape York Penin-
sula. Specimens vary from 2ozs. to 1000 alleged-
very much alleged. Find supposed to be identical with
“ Lead Man's Secret.” for which various parties have been
searching for the last eight years. Name arose, or said to
arise, thus: A sick miner reached Cook town Hospital in
the moderately long ago, and in return for wardsman’s
kindness showed latter swag of rich specimens and
endeavored to draw a map of the locality, but died before
he could finish it. Since then hundreds have wandered
after the Eldorado. Hamilton, M.L.A. for the district,
was one of them. The present find is probably no relation
to the Secret, for Queensland is a large hot place, and
strange myths grow there as well as alligators.

Federation Cos., Croydon, a Bull concern, is now
in liquidation. Usual cause—ornamental and incom-
petent management. Local Mining News consistently
condemned manner of working, and was, as usual, con-
demned in turn for “ trying to injure the field.” At
length. London directors sent an experienced mining
manager from the Towers, to inspect and report. Result:
Discovery that nearly £6OOO had been wasted, co. £2OOO
in debt, and only alternative liquidation.

Lee Block, and a good prospecting show, Golden Gate
(Croydon) Cos., consisting of three 25-acre leases, upon

are putting down a deep shaft. Also, diamond drill is

This will be a great test time for Croydon.

* * * *
Mt. Lvell last month treated 23,051 tons of ore.
The average copper value was only 2’49 per cent; silyer
2'63 ; gold '099. This lower grade copper the manager
attributes to the fact that the systematic working of the
excavations required the extension of the open cuts to-
wards the north end of the ore bodies where the ore is of
lower grade than the average. Since then it is alleged the
grade has risen. It is plain to anyone looking at the Mt.
Lyell huge deposit that the ore lying against the hematite
body is much heyond the average value. It is in the
immediate vicinity of this hematite body that the richer
sulphides are worked and the gold and silver contents are
also greater. A portion of the hematite is also worked
for its gold contents.
* v • •
Dundas (Tas.) is a good example of the
“busted” mining town. When the West Coast had
its silver boom a few years back Dundas was exploited,
the badgers shooed out of their holes, pubs, built, and
things tvere “locomotive.” The trifling fact that the
mines produced no silver didn’t matter —at boom time.
But the boom went the way of all booms, and Dundas
slumped. Now it is a place where the few inhabitants
make a living by borrowing a pick and shovel to look at a
lode. Its streets are thickly peopled with goats and the
used-up horses of Zeehnn. Now and again a horse dies in
the main street, whereupon all the male portion of the
population who happen to be awake hitch ropes on to the
carcase and drag it to the nearest abandoned shaft.

Adelaide Register prints in parallel columns
some gold returns of hake View Consols mine as made to

returns made to the Government are presumably the
correct ones; they have to be sworn to and there is a
heavy penalty if they are incorrect. Those to the share-
holders are not sworn to and can be shifted about in any
direction without any risk.

The Financial Times (London) attributes the
great collapse in Westralians to the want of highly cap-
able mining managers and metallurgists, such as the big
mines of the Rand have long commanded, heavy taxa-
tion, severe railway rates, and incompetent control in
London, which control had always more regard to the
market than to the mines. Lovely to read this after the
slobbering encomiums passed on Kalgoorlie managers and
metallurgists when things were booming beyond their
worth.
The Sulphide Reduction new process claims
to have solved the sulphide problem in a practi-
cal way' and heyond all dispute. The Ashcroft
people did likewise, but found a terrible fly in the
ointment after wasting about £500,000. The new process
has treated a consignment of Mt. Reid ore, base because
of a high percentage of zinc. It will be remembered in
sorrow and anger that the same thing was claimed to
have been done in England by Ashcroft with a large con-
signment of Broken Hill sulphides. Anyhow, this com-
pany is selling its rights like hot cakes, and it is now
proposed to am ilgamate with the French co. with a
capital of £500,000, which will be turned into £1,000,0 )0
when occasion warrants. Shares are selling at a premium,
and everyone went away from the meeting in London
recently with the assurance that the sulphide problem
was absolutely solved at last.

H. C. Callahan, general manager of the Lake
View Consols and consulting engineer to the Ivanhoe
Gold Corporation, arrived in Sydney by the Moana on
Sunday and is now on his way to Kalgoorlie. It now
transpires that he took a San Francisco steamer to Hono-
lulu intending there to pick up the Canadian Pacific
steamer, but on its arrival it refused to take on any
passengers in consequence of the plague. 11. C. C. had
then to return to ’Frisco and put in quarantine-time
with the result that instead of being ori the’field at the
end of December, he cannot get there till the end of
February. During his enforced and unexplained absence
all sorts of y'arns have been spread about him, including
one that he was not likely to return at all. All on
account of the slump in the share-market, for which the
manager, under any circumstances, is always made re-
sponsible, so far as that great asinine public is concerned.
Lake Views fell in two or three months from £26 to £ll,
They'have, during the last few weeks, risen from £ll to
£ls, and should they rise another £5 Callahan will again
be encircled with a halo of public admiration.

“The earliest information” complaint by Aus-
tralian speculators is entirely justified. Why should
London directors of several W.A. mines be in possession
of yields and all such information weeks and months before
the facts are allowed to leak out in Sydney, Melbourne,
and Adelaide ? The London men have generally finished
their manipulation of the markets before we know the
causes of the rise and fall of stocks.

Since the London Critic, Hess’s paper, changed
to a purely financial journal, its most distinguishing
feature is the “ Directory of Guinea Pigs.” Up to date it
has pilloried over 200 and given the details of over 2000
cats which they directed, and so far in alphabetical order
only the letter G has been reached. The list is already
out in book form, and when the alphabet is complete the
volume will be as big as a family Bible, while its informa-
tion should be invaluable. The writer can imagine
thousands of people looking up the Directory' of Guinea
Pigs when names are emblazoned on prospectuses.
Concerning recent remarks that miners have an
idea—“ superstition ” somebody calls it—that accidents
are most common underground in the early morning.
Where there is a night shift they certainly' are. It is
doubtful, though, whether atmospheric changes have
anything to do with them, though that is what many miners
ascribe them to. The cause, more likely, is in Diemen them-
selves. In summer especially the miner who lives in a
galvanised iron humpy, with seven youngsters crying
most of the time, only sleeps in patches ; and if he isn’t
bright in the early morning when he ought to be asleep
(and it’s a pretty drowsy atmosphere in the 1000ft. stopes)
it is no miracle. Yet the miner who can’t keep bright
while working among “ soapy-heads ” or running faces is
pretty sure to be taken out some morning in sections.
Mayor Billson, of Albury (N.S.W.) de-
siring another year in the chair, took the occasion
of the aldermanic election the other day to obtain
what may be called a mayoral referendum. The
names of the three would-be mayors were submitted
to a vote of the ratepayers, Billson heading the
poll with an almost 2 to 1 majority. This is the
first time such a referendum has been held in
N.S.W. ; the law makes no provision for it and
the result binds no one. But, as Billson says :
“Such an expression of the ratepayers’ opinion
will powerfully hint to the aldermen whom they
should elect.” Once more—why not let the rate-
payers elect the mayor ? In many country towns
the ratepayers have very little rational amuse-
ment, and it is a sin to deny them the harmless
joy of electing their own Billson. All mayors
should bp named Billson.

SOCIETY
A wealthy Australian merchant and pillar
of the Y.M.C.A. recently received much ap-
plause for a magnificent donation to the
war fund. Simultaneously the wages of the poor
women making coats in his big factory were re-
duced from 2/1 to 1/9 per coat. Despite all efforts
the philanthropist could only be induced to com-
promise at 1/10, and the sweated unfortunates have
resumed work to make good the amount (and more)
of that “munificent donation.”
* * * *
Some Melb. shops now have for sale the
“patriotic handkerchief”—a linen or silk detailed
map of S. Africa, with pictures of Kruger and
Chamberlain in corners. This useful and instruc-
tive article makes it possible for anyone to run the
war on his little own, bombard any place, and
insult Paul or Joseph till further orders. Present
writer, being a cosmopolitan, sadly wipes liis
neutral brow in the Indian Ocean.
IN THE AGE OF THE NEW WOMAN.
1ST NEW MAX: “My dear, who is that extra-
ordinary creature on the sofa talking to
Georyina?”
2ND NEW MAN: “Oh! a recent arrival. He's a
bundle of affectations, too athletic, rational, and
all that. Quite too dreadfully effeminate for
me, dear."
One Q. contingenter had curious luck.
The Commandant had decided that married
men with more than two children should
not be allowed to go to the war. When the would-
be soldier of the Queen left home he was the father
of only two children. He was duly accepted and
passed all the tests. On the morning of em-
barkation he received a telegram from Ins wife to
the effect that he was the father of three
children, and therefore his trip was off. The
authorities had also been apprised of the increase,
and just before the troops fell in the happy but
enraged father was called aside by the officer and
informed that he must be discharged.
A feeling has arisen in Melbourne that the
Post Office clock is tainted with disloyal opinions.
That implement plays a large assortment of tunes
including' “Gorsave,” “Rule Britannia,” “Home
Sweet Home,” “ Last Rose of Summer,” &c. The
jingo crowd is pleased with “Gorsave” and
“Rule Britannia,” but it wants the other music
expelled to make room for “ Soldiers of the
Queen,” “Sons of the Sea,” the dreadful jagged
lilt of the “ A.-M.-Beggar,” and the other weary
horrors that shriek in the music halls in these loud
days of gore and foolishness. And if this matter
isn’t attended to there may be trouble, for P.M.G.
Watt is the responsible man, and Watt voted
against the first contingent, and only painted him-
self bright red when he got a portfolio. Therefore
Watt is subject to suspicion, and needs to sing
the “ A.-M.-Beggar” at least every hour to clear
his character.
• * • •
The Australian small boy improves. A
nine-year-old, whose parents are on the land near
Colac (Victoria), packed his handkerchief and
went off to join the Bushmen’s Contingent the
other day. Subsequently his irate sire pursued
him through the bush on a horse, whereupon the
youngster took to the hills, and made for a gully
impassable to a horseman. The father dismounted,
tied up his nag, and continued the chase; but
again the rising generation was too many for him.
The kiddy doubled on the pursuer, and, securing
the horse, rode off, and was not heard of for
several days. When the dear child was discovered
he had sold the horse at Panmure, and with the
proceeds was painting the town red as high up as
he could reach by standing on a chair. He was
arrested for horse-stealing (the father had bor-
rowed the nag in the first place), and when last
heard from young hopeful was out on bail, and
the proudest kid in all Christendom.

Letter from an affectionate wife to Mayor
of Christchurch (M.L.) concerning another “Ab-
sent-minded Beggar”:—
Do not put my husband’s name down for the third con-
tingent ; lie has a house full of little ones, ami if he goes
he must take them with him. lie is in his fifty-fourth
year, had his leg broken when drunk, cannot read with-
out specs., and has his head sewn up. He gets fellows to
tell and write lies for him. l’lease do not accept his
name ; he gets cramp, and is only making a fool of him-
self.

A fair proportion of the Vic. Bushman’s
contingent have had enough country experience to
justify their inclusion, but the acceptance of about
a quarter of the men in it is a howling farce. The
officers are nearly all of the Collins-street variety.
One of them has fluctuated between the occupa-
tions of draper"and stock-jobber, and others have
no bush knowledge and no military experience
except in the matter of charging Gs. Sd.

Some of the so-called evidence collected
by the Creswell-Tichborne Commission is the
merest foolishness. For example, Mrs. Bridget
Wines, widow, stated that in 18G9
she was courted by a sort of a
foreigner who bore “most, if not
all, of the marks” which
the eal Sir Roger had
concealed about his person,
and who told her lie was
si nobleman’s son, having
run away to sea from Stony-
hurst College when he was
14, after which he visited
Valparaiso, and was aboard
the Bella when the (un-
authenticated) mutiny oc-
curred, &c., &e. The real
Roger Tichbome never
saw Stony hurst till he was
17, neither did he ever run
away to sea. He started to
travel for the benefit of his
aristocratic health at the
age of 24, and next year lie
booked a passage by the
Bella, bound for New Fork,
and the Lord knows what
happened to the Bella. And as
for real Roger’s alleged tattoo
marks, it is quite probable that tlie
Ticlibome family witnesses invented them for the
purpose of confounding the impostor Orton at the
famous trial. Other witnesses swore they had
never seen, nor heard of, such marks.
* • * *
“ C.M.G.” : Was in the baths at South
Melbourne the other day when a sixteen-stone
citizen taking aquatic exercise in the hope of
reducing his wilful waist suddenly lost his
equilibrium, and began to roll about in the water
like a barrel. He was too fat to sink had he had
sense enough to keep his head shut, but he com-
menced the usual absurd effort to drink the bay,
and was fast drowning when a perky boy of about
eight years seized on him and began to haul
towards the steps, looking for all the world like a
sugar-ant pulling an omnibus. Then three or four
men went in, and the job was taken off the
youngster’s hands. The stout one was taken out,
and pressed and drained. Presently he sat up
and began to take notice, and then interest was
diverted from him to the amazing urchin, who was
sitting near, mother-naked, and snivelling miser-
ably. “What’s the matter, my lad ?” asked one
of the rescue party. “Ye dirty lot o’ sneaks 1 ”
wailed the little hero, “ what id yer take him from
me for when I was gettin’ along orl right ? P’r’aps
he’d’a’gi’me a bob.” Even the very small boy
knows the average size of a stout man’s gratitude.
Rockhampton (Q.) Record published two
telegrams side by side, the other day, from the
little ink-spot town of Barcaldine. One was to
the effect that the local Patriotic Fund had
reached £l5O. The other stated that the local
hospital, which last year treated 1289 patients, had
been so much neglected of late that the total
funds were only £B. It is the same everywhere.
The money subscribed to the Patriotic Fund is
mostly robbed from charities, and the real
widow who is with us now is deprived of her little
dole to keep the possible prospective contingent
widow, who is already about the best-provided-for
kind of widow in Australia.
The perennial National Guard case has
been re-opened in a Sydney Police Court by old
George Republican Dibbs summoning ex-full-and-
complete private Byrne for neglecting to give up
trappings valued at £5 odd after having been sum-
marily discharged. Byrne says he wasn’t lawfully
discharged according to the muddled Volunteer
Act of the province, and he refuses to give up the
property until he is so discharged. He has now
been fined ss. “ with the option,” his objections
being over-ruled by the Stipendiary. The case
turns on G. Dibbs’s right, or want of right, to
summarily and verbally dismiss a N.S.W. soldier
without bringing him before the commandant on
some definite charge of “misconduct,” and giving
him a chance to answer it. The question, in
short, is whether the National Guard belongs to
the man who publicly cursed the Commonwealth
Bill or to the people of N.S. W., who have paid for
its arms, its belts, and its finery? Stipendiary
Mair says, in effect, that it belongs to the eminent
Bill-curser.

* • • •
The usual leper was unearthed the other
day in a Queensland sugar port. Not that that
matters much. In the same town seven or eight
supposed lepers “ batch ” in some dens down a
by-street. They are only “supposed,” because
they allow no one in their hovels, and they only
come out in a soft, noiseless way on moonless
nights, and no Government official calls round to
inspect. The rent is thrown out of the window—
how it is earned no one knows, but it is thrown
out right enough.
• * • *
The Melb. tram-conductor, when he got a
living wage, kept himself clean and neat. Now
his uniform consists mainly of long hair, dirty
linen, and patches. The other day several ladies
of uncertain age got a severe shock when a con-
ductor walked the car with a considerable-sized
flag of distress flying from a rent in his exhausted
pants. If the bosses won’t pay a living wage,
then for shame’s sake they might supply their
employes with a large bag apiece to wear over
their other clo tlies.
* * * *
A divorce-court audience has no soul to
appreciate true heroism in a respondent. At
Melbourne last week, the mother of eight children
applied for severance of the marriage-tie on the
ground that her husband, after 20 years of
domestic happiness—as happiness goes—had left
her for a much newer girl. And the fickle fugitive’s
letters to his wife were read in court. He told her
she had always been a good woman, and he still
loved her, in the sense that he looked forward to
meeting her in Heaven—where marriage doesn’t
count. But he also loved his new girl Kate, and
intended to stick to her, because she was otherwise
friendless and alone, and all that sort of thing.
A lot of laughter,” “loud laughter,” and “con-
tinued merriment ” greeted the reading of these
honest epistles. Here was a man who could give up
a good woman and eigh t children in response to a
natural desire, and write “Dearest wife, —Get it
through as fast as you can and as cheap as you
can, and the court roared as though he were a
low comedian saying “Damn it!” Orthodox
LOVE’S CLIMAX.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The stars were lamps to light your path ;
The trees arched proud above you
When lowly at your feet I knelt,
With, “ Dearest heart, I love you."
Whisper—Do kisses weary you ?
Do tender words seem hollow ?
Is there no climax to “ I love ’’ ?
Is there nought else to follow f
There comes a fairer moment, dear;
How does this climax strike you ?
Clasp hand in mine ; I stand, not kneel,
With « My true friend, I like you.”
Sydney, 1899.

MY IDEAL.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
When the sunlight Alters through
Dim green branches, I remind me
When the pain is all behind me
And Life’s splendid gift was—You.
When at night the moonlight streaming
Steeped our hearts in Love’s young dreaming
W r hen your eyes on me were beaming
Doubts were few.
When the curlew’s plaintive song
Came to warn that Life had sorrow,
What to me meant Love’s to-morrow,
W r hat to me that Love was wrong.
Thro’ the boughs the wind was sighing,
Sadder came the curlews crying,
We but knew Time’s wings were dying—
Youth is strong.
What to me that grief is real,
What to me that she has left me,
What to me joy is bereft me.
Now I know I could not steal
All that made her worth the winning.
I could not have loved her—sinning.
Slowly round God's wheel is spinning
My Ideal.
HELEN B.

A thrilling shark incident happened last
week at Coogee (Sydney). The fish was dozing
peacefully in about 4ft. of water when a lady
bather took a header and landed on his back.
They were both surprised. A screaming female
whirled her wet white limbs up on the rocks, and
an alarmed fish got up steam and put out to sea.

Tasmanian item. A unique lady telegraph-
operator is alleged to exist in the Isle of Absent-
minded Sleepers. Someone having complained of
inattention at the office window, she coldly
remarked that any person wishing to send a wire
should give her notice a day beforehand.

Court case at Ballarat, last week, who had appa-
rently seduced and abandoned a young woman
after deluding her by a mock marriage, was
characterised by Judge A’Beokett as “ utterly un-
worthy of belief and an absolute liar.” Next day
a horse which had apparently heard of the case
kicked him so violently on the leg that he had to
be taken to the hospital, a very much damaged
ndividual.
Baron Brassey was the only Victorian
Governor who didn’t sign official documents.
“Uncle Tom’s” hand grew so unsteady from
hitting the ground when he fell off things that he
had a rubber-stamp made, so that one good punch
would accomplish the desired effect. Even with
this assistance, many of the papers on which le
Government of the country hinges bear the
stamped signature at all angles. A few even pre-
sent it upside-down, and there are one or two rare
documents where the signature occurs in the
middle of the verbiage, and trails m a long wavy
smudge, extending thence to the proper spot.
“ Uncle Tom ” was a bad shot, and if he had been
bombarding a town instead of a document he
would never have hit anything, save the hospital
now and then.
* * * *
The society of good people who poke their
noses into things is again on the war-path in Mel-
bourne. In couples the good, long-nosed people
scour the gardens and parks in the dusk, and just
as ’Arry is telling his Mary Han what a regular
devil he is, two pairs of gig-lamps are fixed on him
and a voice like a rat-tail file says : Young
people, will you take a tract ? ” Unless driven off
the kopje there and then, they deliver a lecture
with an unwholesome moral, snort a bit, and glide
away. The substance of the tract in general is the
Bad Man, the Once-Virtuous Girl, and the Baby.
Every week these gig-lamps report progress to a
company of other hard-faced people with similar
green spectacles, and tell the Horrid Things they
have seen. Then all the thin, hard-faced people
rise up and polish their green spectacles with a
trembling hand, and say how glad they are that
they are pure and virtuous, and not like the un-
couth but comparatively harmless ’Arry—who,
after all, was only telling Mary Han a fairy tale of
valor about an alleged person that he licked in Col-
lingwood. * * •

On Mrs. Sydney Burdekin’s grounds on
the Hawkesbury was celebrated, the other day, the
98th anniversary of Ebenezer, the oldest Presby-
terian church in N.S.W.—in fact, in Australia.
Ebenezer was the first church of any denomina-
tion in Australia built wholly by voluntary sub
scriptions. It is still in good repair, and promises
to last another century.

Launceston (Tas.) Examiner tells a story
about a Tasmanian J.P. who, hearing that a
certain inhabitant wasn’t quite convinced about
the justice of the Transvaal war, paid a, local
ruffian a sovereign to assault him, and promised to
acquit the ruffian immediately if the case came into
court. It may possibly be true. The Tasmanian
J.P. does many remarkable things, and public
opinion is so brutalised and debased just now by
jingo violence that the average jingo paper would
applaud a little thing like that.
* * * *
What ought to have been a strong tragic
situation was spoiled the other day by bad stage
management. A young Sydney woman and her
husband had quarrelled and separated. She grew
despondent over the matter. One day she got
into a cab and told the driver to take her to her
old home, and when he got there to call her hus-
band out. Then she took poison, and if she had
taken the right kind of poison, and other things
had fitted in, her dead face would have stared at
her husband when he put his live one into the cab,
and it would have been a notable episode. But,
after the manner of her kind, she speculated in
carbolic acid, and her cries in the cab made the
driver abandon all previous arrangements and
drive furiously to the hospital, where the patient
died. And even if she had gone home her hus-
band wasn’t in at the time so they wouldn’t have
stared at each other across the cab door after all.
This is the true story of the prize absent-
minded man of a south Sydney suburb. Many pre-
vious instances of mental abstraction are charged
against him, but the latest is a record. About five
weeks ago his wife died, yet the other night a be-
lated neighbour, who takes the same size in hiccup
as Blank, was surprised to see that gentleman
stealing in at his own gate in the small hours and
crawling cautiously on the verandah. There he
removed his boots, imploring himself at intervals
to keep quiet. Then lie opened the door with the
loud and clumsy caution of a belated bear, left it
open, crawled upstairs dropping his boots all oyer
the place and calling out to himself to keep quiet
and not waken the wife, fell down once or twice,
set fire to his tall hat at a gas jet, and vanished
with intense care into the bedroom. He had for-
gotten that his wife was dead.
* * « *
Even now it is very seldom that a week
passes without a rumor going round Queensland
that “important discoveries” have been made in
the matter of the Gatton murders. Nothing ever
is discovered, but the good old rumor totters per -
sistently on the mental horizon, and even at this
late date strangers occasionally go into the fatal
paddock and stare at the grass, as if they expected
the earth to open and a clue to rise, like Lazarus
from the dead.
A case of just retribution was witnessed
the other day at Manly, near Sydney. A young
lady and a young man were strolling towards the
boat. They were both affluent-looking ; the lady in
particular was a curled and bangled darling with a
great mass of soft, fluffy brown hair. The sun
lingered in these brown tresses, and the girl
looked like a daughter of the gods. Behind them
a stout, middle-aged, motherly-looking woman
was trotting, also boat wards, with a large parcel.
Just as she caught up to the pair the girl’s glorious
mass of brown hair fell off and bounced on the
pavement. The girl strolled on unconscious, with
a short, scanty tail sticking out underneath her
hat—only that and nothing more. The motherly
one picked up the hair, concealed it in her cloak
and ranged alongside. In her kind-hearted way
she meant returning it surreptitiously, so as not to
shame the owner by drawing her companion’s
attention, but the girl blighted her before
she had whispered two words: “I don’t know
you,” she said, haughtily; “Please, do not in-
trude.” The motherly one still tried to speak.
“ Really,” said the bangled one, cutting her short,
“You are a most intrusive and obnoxious person.
George, this woman is begging or something.
Send her away.” Then the old lady blazed up
and threw opippunction to the winds, and held
JOHN BULL , THE GOOD SAMARITAN.
out the mass of curls publicly at arm’s length. “ I
was not begging,” she said, in a voice that rang
half-way down the Corso; “I only desired to
RETURN YOUR LOST BACK-HAIR. HERE IS YOUR
hair.” George turned ghastly as his eye fell on
the skimpy tail at the back of his best girl’s head,
and then at the mass of brown tresses in her hand.
It was evident that an illusion had vanished.
After waiting a few seconds he spoke in hard
tones: “I think, Julia,” he said, “that you for-
got to thank this lady for her kindness in return-
ing your back-hair.” And Julia, with the hair in
her hand, said something in a broken voice—
possibly to the effect that she wished the old lady
was dead.
TWO MELODIES!
Brisbane.
Two melodies my lady plays,
Though one ye never hear ;
The first she strikes from yonder lute
In silver notes and clear ;
The other comes from tender string*
That tremble at her will ;
And I— I know whose bosom throb*
To bursting when they thrill.
Such magic in her music lies,
Such deep impassioned call,
That both her songs are blent as one
To hold us here in thrall ;
With one she sways the list’ning throng
That lingers gently near,
And me she bends beneath the spell
Of one ye never hear 1
P. LUFFIG.
HE KNEW
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
She wax the Ball-room Belle,
said all —
And there reeve many there
to see —
At that Vice-regal festival
Of grace and chivalry.
The men admired her, young and old ,
And wise and foolish, fresh and stale;
But she was as an iceberg cold,
And as an iceberg pale.
With scornful glance and queenly air,
She gazed upon the glowing scene,
As if a queen indeed she were
Arid men her subjects mean.
One man alone of all the throng
Kept far aloof from her the while ;
And, as she proudly swept away,
Surveyed her with a smile.
Had he wdh hopeless love gone mad?
Or was his cynicism forced?
Not so—not nearly so; they had
Been married—and divorced.
And so, while conquests marked her
track,
He merely smiled to think that she
Had two large warts upon her back
And was bandy in one knee.
CREEVE ROE.
A MAN’S FORGIVENESS.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
You ask me to forgive you, sweet,
You kiss me through a mist of tears,
While even now the bitter words
Ring in my ears.
Do I forget the yielding mouth
Can utter words with serpent’s sting.
The dear wet eyes look scorn and hate
And misery bring.
Ah, clinging lips 1 ah, loving eyes !
Of course I kiss away your tears,
While all the time the bitter words
Ring in my ears.
Vic.
JOHN WARD.

A WOMAN’S LETTER.
Sydney, February 20, 1900.
Mr Dear Mvee, —
At the Bushmen s racos on Friday Society ro-
gathored amicably after a long absence. In the
V R lx>x MrsDarley and .Mrs. Mackenzie sat
side by side like two big sisters, in dark blue
foulards figured in white ; and the Misses Darley
shivered m white drill suits and black hats The
newest bride, Mrs. Stanley Kyrie (w. e Miss Maud
Mackenzie), was the brightest figure of the Acting-
V. R. set, in hergoing-away suitof navy doth with
sailor collar of pale blue, and big black hat with a
touch of blue among the feathers. Among other
dressers, I noticed many pretty country girls who
scored heavily by knowing the Mush Contingent
better than the town girl ; Mrs. MaoArtlmr Ons-
low, a tall figure in white-flowered silk with aeorn-
flower sash and the big black hat without which
we are nothing ; pretty Miss Maggie Cox, in a
striped black-and-white tailor-made ; Miss Inez
Bensusan, in a red toque and short jacket and
blue-and-white skirt ; Mayoress Harris, in a novel
cream-laco jacket over a black frock.
Women showed an inclination to break through
conventionalities and hug the gentlemen in kliarki
without an introduction. Several women who
believed they were being carried away by patriot-
ism cornered some of the best-looking busbies.
1 heir conversations always opened with, “ Are
you glad you’re going to the war ? ” The Bushies
looked as if they thought they were. This question
is put to them by every second woman who visits
the camp, and is apt to bring oil that tired
feeling.
A useful little worker for the Absent-Minded
Cause has been Dorothy Vane. She sang very
nearly £3OO out of local pockets through the Pan-
tomime season. In town on Saturday morning
she was looking quite done-up in a paio-grev suit
and sailor-hat. In one way, at any rate, she is
one ot the least disappointing actresses going, for
she has only been out of tlio bill twice in upwards
ot two years opera.
, I TORfl sQme verses somewhere the other dav
about Those Who Stay At Home.” Charming
verses, insinuating that those who stav behind
have just as grievous fighting to do. That’s easy
to understand But it’s hard to distinguish
between the rights of those who cut and kill for
England s sake away in the Transvaal, and those
who stay at home to do it. Two men in Victoria
quarre led over the war last week, and one killed
the other. A carer home, a little son of Colonel
Maul, of Soudan distinction, fought for the British
in the back-yard last week, and blew his eye out
with a tiny cannon. All the little boys at

whother they won’t go and try to do likewise is a

the widders and h’orphans.” he said. “Think of
the widders and h’orphans.” A little further on
And all those widders and fatherless h’or-

space was broken with another touching hurst
about the fatherless children and the poor, un-
provided-for widders.” As everybody feels sorry

unmarried men.

It is alleged that Lord Hopetoun is likely to be
Federal Governor, and Victorian women are pro-
testing that if they had known Federation would
mean Lady Hopetoun coming amongst them again
they would have fought against the great union.
The lady was unpopularity personified and her
husband’s hardest work was trying to atone for his
wife’s undisguised contempt for tho people
amongst whom she had come very reluctantly. It
may he that site has learnt a good deal in the past
live years, for she never had a London season till
she quitted Victoria. She was a verv raw
Governoress. It was as a “freezer ” that she shone
most markedly. She would sit on the dais at
balls, alone, or with women whose chairs were
placed well out of the ice-belt, perhaps as a dis-
couragement to chat. If addressed, she would
reply with a cold smile, or a monosyllable.
Persons who attended a certain Highland ball
during the Hopetoun regime, have not forgotten
the Governor's dancing of a national dance with a
girl who was the champion dancer of the High-
land clan, lie danced with enthusiasm, snapping
his fingers and kicking high—regularly enjoving
himself. But when he went back to the dais"and
his wife, what a change came over his Beauchamp-
like face (he was then very like Little Billee).
The consort was talking in a low tone ; her fine
eyes were snapping. If she was not giving it to
Adrian Louis for “making an exhibition of him-
self,” then the onlookers were much mistaken.
He danced no more that night.
Perhaps if Lady Hopetoun had some centuries
of pedigree behind her the Victorians would liavo
stood her Vere de Vere attitude more patiently.
But ennoblement and the evolution of De Molcyns
from Mullens are incidents of this century.
“ Trilby ” at the Royal on Saturday, showed its
title clear to being called a popular play. A seeth-
ing house, crowded everywhere, gathered to
admire. It seems like last century smeo we had

with diamonds and roses about the corsage. Mrs.
Hugh Dixson was also in blue. Mr. John Storm
Kingston, who would surely have made such a de-
lightful little Billee, sat next to Mrs. Kingston, nee
Miss de Lara (sister of Isidore de Lara, the com-
poser), a brunette in a smart long grey overall
cloak, with turned-over white fur collar. Mrs.
Bethune, in black, sported a glorious diamond
necklet and pendant; and a handsome young
artist brought things back to their daily level by
walking about under a white helmet. He was
mistaken for a bush-brigader, but observed that
he was not going to the war, because he had to
stay at home and fight the rumor that ho was
going to be married.

they nearly “upsplit” her death scene. At the

In Iby was too indignant to come before the
curtain.

Which reminds me that one is invariably disap-
pointed when a play talks a great deal about
somebody’s wonderful beauty, or wonderful voice,

If ever your youth returns to .you, Mvee, and you
break into play-writing, do write a play in which
the hero—Cromwell, for instance—never appears.
Let us hear of him, and feel him, and just miss
seeing him all the way through, blit for art’s sake,
and common sense’s sake, and sentiment’s sake,
don’t bring him on the stage. Besides, think of
all the actors you might put into such parts.
No woman can live to-day without a white lace
veil. Surely the embroidery on some of tho
fashionable ones has been suggested—as to design
—by Maori tattooing. A lady who has just
returned from Maoriland, »vturs a veil—the em-
broidery of which exactly follows the lines and
curves of tattooing on the chins and jaws of chiefs
of high rank. All the same, the veil was neither
boug.it nor made in the land of the Moa and white
Heron. It came from Paris.
Rumored that Kipling is running an involuntary
zoo of himself. Another consequence of his men-
dicant ditty. When the people round his place
have an hour and a half-crown to spare, they give
the latter to a cabby, carter, or other vehicle-man
and instruct him to drive slowly round Rudyard's
garden walls till the value of the money is ex-
hausted. The cabbies and carters down Rudyard’s
way are getting into the millionaire stage, and the
sig.it of a number of heads barely visible above the
walls slowly circling round and round, happy
when they catch the gleam of Rudyard’s spectacles
or the echo of some gentle swear-word, is said to be
a beautiful and instructive sight.

ment, and carriage are worth more than rubies to a

When we knew her, the sometime Hon. Sybil de
vere Capel was worn, sallow, grey-haired, with
teeth hopelessly out of drawing. Nevertheless,

“ Poor Mr. Lambie, the first Victorian killed in
the war. was a “ ladies’ man,” in the sense that
women liked him and appreciated his gentle genial
manner. There was something attractive, too, in
the pallid, wistful, old-young face and slight boyish
figure, in juvenile short jacket and incongruous
tall hat. Africa must have seemed a scrubby,
grubby spot to Lambie, for the home lie left
behind him in a Melb. suburb is a dainty nook—
a picturesque cottage mantled with creepers and
surrounded by flower-beds and home-made
ferneries,, the pet hobbies of its departed owner,
who also found time to play melodiously on the
flute.

PERSONAL ITEMS.
The commander of the Vic. Bushmen’s
Corps is Captain Dobbin. Astounding to learn that
there can be a Capt. Dobbin outside of Thackeray’s
“ Vanity Fair.” But the name seems to fit the
billet. Gee up !
By, the recent death in London of Francis
Lewis Shaw Mere wether N.S.W. Treasury bene-
fits to the extent of £9OO a year. Mr. Merewether
was N.S.W. Auditor-General for a term and car-
ried a £9OO pension into private life.
Mrs. Kruger is chiefly described as being
an over-fed and adipose virago. Nearly every
British paper has published screeds from various
lady globe-trotters describing Mrs. Kreer as the
worst-tempered and most hostile lady it had ever
been their lot to meet. Still, Mrs. Kreedjher
seems to have some gentle forethought about her,
for when President Krugger’s statue was designed
Mrs. Kri-her suggested that the top of his bronze
belltopper should be hollowed out and kept filled
with clean water for the sake of the little dicky-
birds.
Tim MTnerney, the Victorian captain,
wounded and taken prisoner at Rendsburg, South
Africa, is a solicitor in private life and a partner
of his brother, Dr. Tay Pay MTnerney, warden
of the Melb. University. He was the first to take
advantage of the Act qualifying a managing clerk
of 10 years’ standing as a solicitor. Also he has
been a more or less military character since 1890.
Tim’s capture is about the best thing that could
happen to him, for he is over 6ft. high, and built
in such proportions that any respectable Boer
marksman could kill him to a dead certainty at
lialf-a-mile.
“The German Emperor during his recent
private visit here, ” writes The Bulletin’s London
correspondent “ removed many nasty impressions
concerning his hungry desire for ‘ sending wires ’
and the moral effect he created improved the
pound-a-week city German clerk’s position fully
£1 19s. a year. William is certainly full of life
and humor, even in face of the perpetually
gloved left hand on the end of his withered
arm, and that unpleasant, actively-eruptive ear.
He is a ‘ boss ’ in the ‘ bossiest ’ meaning of the
word, and punctual as the ‘eight o’clock rush.’
But his and your punctuality are one and the same,
he waits for none and none have to wait for him.
P’raps when all is considered it is his fun which
saves him from being a consummate prig, for
wherever he is oscillating there is genuine laughter
all round. Bill speaks purer English than Albert
Edward and the ‘ th’ baulks him but intheslightest.
He shoots with his right arm only, does it well,
and carries his useless limb in a masterly, un-
obtrusive manner.”
Papua’s Bishop Stone-Wigg who, notwith-
standing considerably shattered health, plans out
further hard work among the dusky islanders,
proved himself a man of courage during his
first year in Brisbane. Brisbane’s least
respectable quarter was the property of /
the former Bishop, Tuffnell, a wealthy
old absentee who employed ex-
Premier Dickson to collect the
rent.s The Rev. Stone-Wigg
examined the Bishop’s slums
and alleys, and, after many
expressions of horror, started
a society to “convert Bishop
Tuffnell’s tenants.” As the
tenants were piebald and
cosmopolitan, he employed
kanaka missionaries, as well
as men and women of his
own color, to attack them
with prayers and brass-
bands. But most of the
tenants were hard cases,
and the work didn’t prosper.
Tuffnell died fabulously
wealthy.
Last year much was
heard all over the earth of one
Hooley, who might have dropped
out of public notice but for the
Bankruptcy Trustee. When Hoo-
ley broke, he publicly declared
that he was going to pay 20s. in
the pound, that his estate would
show a surplus of half-a-million,
and that when everybody had
been paid he was going into the
House of Commons. It now
seems that the claims against
the estate amount to over a
million-and-a-quarter, more than
double the amount estimated by
Hooley; so far the amount
realised is considerably under
£lOO,OOO. It is doubtful if the
estate will ever pay more than
3s. in the pound. If Hooley ad-
heres to his resolution to be in
the general election for next year,
it will be necessary for him to find
over £600,000 in the meantime. How
long is it since the advertising meteor pre-
sented that service of gold plate to St. Paul’s
Cathedral, for the faithful to take the Hooley
communion out of ?
Sydney journalist Cyril H. Sandham, who
last week died suddenly of heart failure, was in
several respects a remarkable man. Originally a
naval officer, and afterwards a Queensland racing-
official, Sandham—like so many men who, so to
speak, gravitate into journalism—combined great
literary versatility with a comprehensive general
knowledge, and was always relied upon by the
paper with which he was principally connected
for satisfactory copy at the shortest of notice upon
almost any subject short of Chinese metaphysics.
Slightly Mephistophelean of aspect and somewhat
cynical of philosophy, Sandham was nevertheless
at bottom essentially a man, and will be missed
by many who, like the present writer, knew him
too intimately to take the husk for the kernel.
Oscar de would seem from some
recent files to be still going strong in London on
the agricultural and pastoral outlook of Australia.
When he lived in Queensland there was no stop-
ping him on that double-barrelled theme. He was
M.L.A. for a Northern and afterwards for a
Western constituency, and the same speech ap-
peared to serve him all the time. With Morehead,
then a giddy young thing, and Lumley Hill he at
one period ambitioned the overthrow*of both
Griffith and Mcllwraith. He was the first to
extensively settle the Diamantina country and he
still owns station property out there.
“ Paradis ” : Major Macready, son of the
tragedian, is referred to in the war news from
South Africa. Macready married for the third
time very old, and sets down in his journal his
rapture at the birth of this little major. Barry
Sullivan, in Melbourne, had a Macready in his
company, under that name, said to be a son of
the tragedian, and like him in the face, but with
no talent. He only acted “John, a servant,” or
something like that, not even ascending to the
dignity of “Charles, his friend.” Barry Sullivan’s
Macready could not be accounted for in the bio-
graphy. He seemed to come from nowhere.
The economic tendency in Australia is ever
downwards. There are places in Sydney where
you can get a decent meal with wine for 9d. nowa-
days, and in accordance with this almighty
tendency the big dogs of the bar are glad to reach
out and seize even such small things as District
Court Judgeships. Twelve months ago Rogers,
Q.C., saw a vacant seat on the District Court
Bench and took it with all his might. And now
Mr. C. G. Heydon does the same thing. And
yet, there was a time when those gentlemen and
much smaller gentlemen would have met the offer
of such a position with a cry of wounded dignity.
Captain Machattie, who goes to Africa
with the Bushmen’s Contingent,, if it ever gets
away, has been 20 years practising medicine in
Bathurst, where his father also practised for 50
years. Captain Machattie was President of the
Federal Convention held in Bathurst four years
ago. It was a challenge thrown out by his father
whioh determined the bushrangers Ben Hall,
Bourke, Vane and Gilbert to raid Bathurst in the
early 60’s. This led up to the attack on Keight-
ley’s at Dunn’s Plains, on which episode was built
the myth of Mrs. Keightley’s ride for the £5OO
ransom. And out of that again grew a drama
from which Australia is not likely to recover for
many years.

N.S.W. Ex-Premier Reid will be 55 years
old on Sunday next, February 25 ; 48 years of
which time he has spent in Australia, mostly say-
ing Yes-No.

C. J. Roberts, N.S.W. Leg. Councillor,
one-time Postmaster-General, but best known as
boss publican, goes to London for a season. When
in the Lower House he was a devoted Parkesite,
and often, going to a Cabinet meeting, put his
cheque-book in his pocket. As a politician he
was unobtrusive, even when Minister, and regu-
larly went asleep on the cushions of the Assembly
at 11 p.m. He was the youngest and most lavish
Mayor Sydney has had.
Dear Bulletin.— Leichhardt was a Dutch-
man or German, or something, and consequently
“ unpatriotic.” What the devil is the meaning
of this latest expedition to look for the dis-
loyal person’s bones ? If they are found I
vote that they be burned by the common
hangman. Likewise that Von Mueller’s
bones be dug up and sent away to
Europe in disgust. Also, it would be a
fair thing if the Sydney suburb which
is named after the disloyal explorer—
who doubtless went out to Cooper’s
Creek as a Boer spy—should change
its name to Victoria.—A.G.
The kanaka has a head
quite as thick as the
aboriginals. AtMul-
grave (N.Q.) a tram-
line engine ran into
a kanaka, caught him
fair on the cranium,
knocked him rods, and
left him with a buzzing
brain-box and a broken thigh.
Such an accident would mean certain
death to a white man, but Billy Mallicolo re-
covered rapidly.
Matthew Davies last week made his first
Melbourne re-appearance as a representative per-
son and a social shining-light. He was elected
president of the Philharmonic Society.
The father of James Carroll, the new
Maoriland Minister for Native Affairs, was a
Parramatta man, Carroll’s grandfather having
come to Botany Bay as a military guard. Minister
Carroll’s mother belonged to the Wairoa tribe.

Some of the
Australian Congrega-
tional people, back
from the interna-
tional conference
in Boston, tell of
meeting “ Dr.” J.
A. Dowie, formerly
of Melbourne, who now
runs a thriving faith-healing
business in the cultured city.
Dowie admits that he is making money—says he
has got well into the five figures already—and he
drives as good a pair-horse team and carriage as
his city sees. Yet Boston is supposed to be a place
of intense mental elevation, where everybody
wears spectacles, and has a forehead that bulges
out like an awning, and carries the Encyclopaedia
Britannica in 24 volumes under his arm.
“ A-theos ” puts in his little speak about
a rather unimportant person who died -lately a
long way off: —
There is another and more presentable side of the Mar-
quis of Queensberry than that which you sum up in the
words : “ One way and another Q. contrived to be a
highly disreputable personality.” He at least had the
courage of his opinions, and it was because he dared to
avow his sympathy with agnosticism by becoming the
head of a sister Freethought organisation to that of which
Charles Bradlaugh was the chief, and not because he
had been divorced (under Scotch law, by the way) from
his first wife or knew the rules of boxing, that the
hypocritical peers of Scotland refused to re-elect
him a representative peer. It is customary enough for
religious journals to make accusations against Free-
thinkers for trade purposes, but we may well look with
surprise when the stories originate in the secular press.
Though personally unacquainted with the private life of
the late Marquis, I venture to affirm the belief that the
great agnostic leaders in England would never have pre-
ferred him to the position he held had there been any
suspicions as to his character, as they well know that the
Church organisations would be only too quick to make
use of any scandal as a weapon of attack. They could find
no occasion against Bradlaugh or Ingersoll, and the fact
that they never attempted to use Queensberry’s name in
this way either is sufficient to show that they had no
evidence to go on.
Robert Colquhoun, one of the fnost despe-
rate criminals in Australia, escaped from Yatala
(S.A.) prison lately. Colquhoun was, years
ago, a prosperous Melbourne tradesman, in a
shop of his own, but be took to owning property
that didn’t belong to him, and, after a few
sentences, became one of the famous gang of safe-
breakers, headed by Williams and Montgomery.
When these two men of enterprise were hanged
at Sydney, the leadership fell to Billy O’Neill, a
big, masterful man, who successfully planned a
certain famous steal of £3OOO worth of tobacco.
The tobacco was traced, and Colquhoun completed
the chain of evidence by squealing vigorously.
O’Neill got seven years, and the informer himself
was sentenced to four years, and was told by the
J udge that his conduct only aggravated his offence.
He was, however, released within a week, and
made for W.A., where he soon got seven years for
burglary. He had served only a few months when
he broke gaol, and went to Adelaide, to fall in
with a ten-year sentence there. He had completed
about 18 months of this term when he got away.
Colquhoun is commonly supposed to be the man
who put a large consignment of bullets into the
neck of Melb. detective Me-rcer, though the charge
was never proved.
