Aboriginalities.
“Malle”: Came across a ghost of the
past recently in the shape of a red-shirted digger
rocking a cradle in a creek. The old boy re-
counted how he and his mate once came across a
Chinaman in the Lambing Flat locality, who had
just wound up a bucket of wash-dirt. ' They
wanted to inspect dirt, but the Chinaman elbowed
them away and said, “Nosavee.” Whereat the
mate, “ who was a bit hot-tempered,” threw the
heathen down the 40-foot shaft on top of another
yellow delver, and hove the nail-keg of dirt down
after him, and then left, leaving the rope on the
brace. “I’ve often wondered,” soliloquised the
ancient, “ how them blessed Chows got out.”
• • * *
“Luck ” : Re luck of drunks and children.
Once trained a black mare for a bush publican in
north-eastern Victoria. Was absolutely the most
vicious animal I ever struck, and I didn’t strike
her often. It wasn’t safe. I was the only one
that could handle her at all, and in nine months I
got kicked twice and bitten half-a-dozen times.
Couldn’t put a horse in the next loose-box or she’d
kick the partition out. Would even kick if a
stranger came within three yards of her stall.
One day a lot of threshing-machine men got on
the spree at the pub. I left the door of the
stable open for a few moments and when
I returned, heard someone in the stable say-
ing, “Whoa (hie), ol’ gal.” Looked in, and
there was a drunk stroking her all over, and she
feeding away and taking no notice of him what-
ever. _ Told man to come out, and he came. Next
morning mare kicked me, with her usual affa-
bility, and broke two ribs. I think vicious horses
like to have beer breathed on their hind-quarters.
• * » *
“ W.M.F.” : Here is something new to
me. Other evening rode to a dam to give my
horse a drink. Was surprised to see scores of
bees buzzing round wildly and helplessly on the
surface of the water. Looked for trie cause and
found each bee held down by a little black water-
beetle. When the bee is floating on the water,
drinking, up darts a water-beetle, seizes him by
the leg close to the body, and hangs on. Bee
buzzes round and round trying to get his sting to
work. Beetle knows his business and simply
hangs on till bee is drowned. Then he sets to work
to feed on as much of the bee as is not pirated by
other beetles. Similar performance to that of the
devil-devil bug, which makes a circular, funnel-
shaped hole in the sand or dust for ants to fall in,
and then hangs on indefinitely. But with the bug
his capture is private property.

“ Trotter ” writes from Gunnedah
(N.S.W.)
Six dry summers, and seven months without a shower
is the order of things here. The land is as dry as a pair
of boots in Sheol. Country all around as devoid of
grass as a Sydney tram-motor. The average temperature
for the past few days have been 100 in the shade, and at
the time of writing 112. Furniture is too hot to sit down
on, and I have stood up for six days. A man on the
Plains lost 400 head of cattle in one night; they got into
a paddock of thistles which they devoured eagerly and
died. They had been eating wooden fences for weeks
previously and food broke them up. The Namoi is
practically dry, and you can hear the smell of it a mile
away. Water is undrinkable—except boiled—and the
tanks are teeming with strange beasts. You swallow 18
beasts in an average drink. A man is thirsty all day long.
Local option man Boyce ought to come up here for a
month or so, and I guess he would soon change his
opinion. Yes! they drink all right here and getting
drunk is the chief pastime. lam a nomad and par-
ticularly averse to sameness, and when you hear
“warm!” “hot!” “very hot!” “devilish hot!” “hot
as h I ” all day long as the day progresses, the weari-
ness of it settles on your soul. There is no shade on the
plains except when you conceal yourself behind a
mosquito.
• * * #
“W.”: Apropos several things. As an
infant I was devoted to my nurse—a coal-black
Dyraaba gin. Used to clasp her round the neck
and kiss her; follow her to camp, squat at the
fire and gnaw a ’possum bone, or a ’guana tail,
with much satisfaction. Yet —to-day the sight of
a gin makes me sniff involuntarily and deviate.
The Unseen Member of the Trilby Co., at Sydney
Theatre Royal. Miss MAUD DALRYMPLE,
who sings “ Ben Bolt ” behind the scenes.
Photo., Grouzelle.
Again, my father loved drink, which in the end
so requited him that, in trying to avoid a hole
that wasn’t there, he fell into one that was, and
was killed. Yet I could never drink even beei’,
and absolutely loathe spirits. Have never been
drunk, nor even “merry” ; and xny constitution,
on doctors’ evidence, is exceptionally strong.
Which upsets one theory of the temperance advo-
cate.
» » * *
Strange no one rises to protest against
that most hideous of all bush curses—the horse-
bell. Strapping a bell on a horse’s neck on
letting him go at night is a habit contracted by
the Australian before the era of fences. Ii these
times most horses are fed, and therefore loaf about
the camp, but the bell is dangled on them just the
same. Even when camping about a town the
Australian hangs the blastiferous bell on his moke,
quite indifferent to the fact that he is thereby
depriving dozens of people of their sleep. If you
drive the bell-ringing brute away from you the
owner considers it a personal affront, almost call-
ing for violence. His belief in the absolute right
of his accursed beast to ring a big dinner-bell in
your ear at any hour of the night is something
pathetic.
No use howling against the camel.
Boulia (N.Q.) has been drought-stricken
for months, and of late famine-stricken
also. Neither horse nor bullock team
could relieve it. T’other day, the arrival
of a camel-team saved the place from
sheer starvation. Curse the Afghan driver
if you like, but, till the flying-machine
arrives, it’s folly execrating the camel.
* * * *
More about the small ironies of life.
At Morwell, Gippsland, the Mechanics’
Institute serves many purposes. It is
even a Court of Justice on occasions.
Then magistrates and lawyers occupy
the platform, whilst below, immediately
faced by a 6in. lettered “Welcome,”
stand the various drunks of the district.

“ Xerxes ” : Can any Ab. reader
give us particulars regarding a quadruped
called the bilby? As far as I know its
habitat is Central Division of N.S.W.,
and nowhere else. Plentiful along Lower
Lachlan River. Color, blueish-grey. Ap-
pearance, size, and mode of progression,
like a kangaroo-rat. It burrows and has
fur like a rabbit, and many bushmen
swear that it is a cross between the rabbit
and the ’roo-rat. Query—Did it exist beiore the
rabbit came ?

“AlexM.”: I can believe “Yam” (B.
17/2/’00) about that vet’s system of canine
therapeutics; because, as a lad, I used for several
years to earn a quarterly half-sov. by “ curing” a
maiden-aunt’s pug of the effects of too much
banquet. Used to be handed over to me, a bloated
monstrosity, hardly able to waddle ; whereupon,
to begin with, I would give him every day for a
week (on the quiet) a. “scrap” with a diminutive
hero of an Irish terrier, and the way the combined
funk and exertion used to take the blubber off
puggy was a caution. During this week of war
and woe I fed the patient sparingly ; but, when he
began to show some ability to keep his end up, I
knew it was time for about a week of nearly abso-
lute starvation. Then a third week of moderate
diet would put the final polish on him, and I’d
send him home in light marching order and as
frisky as a kitten.
* • • •
Long ago, before the war, there was a dis-
tinctly famous Absent-Minded Beggar in Two-
wharves. He was a young three-quarter-caste
named Tommie Pipeful. The timbergetters called
him “the cove without a memory.” He lived at
the blacks’ camp ; and his English vocabulary
was very poor save in swear-words. There it was
as plentiful as rabbits. Because of his grotesquely
defective memory, he was the worst messenger in
all the Big Scrub. He would repeat the pith of a
message over and over again—and forget it while
so repeating. One day he was sent by Willie
Jimmie Jimmie (the blacks called the latter for
short “ Jimmie-two times-twice ”) to “Ruggy”
Ned’s mill for a cross cut saw. Tommie went off
repeating vigorously “ Karose-cut-sore,” etc., etc.
When he saw “Ruggy” Ned, he said, “Willie
Chimmie Chimmie bin wanta lenda dat pfella—er
dat pfella—oh, baal, mine tink of it bimeby.”
“Haxe?” “No.” “Hadze?” “No.” “Maul-
unwedgez?” “No not dat pfella.” “Oh,
well, what does he want, Tommie?” “Baal,
mine fogit hiss plurry name. You know dat pfella
got plurry sharp teeth. Him—him—van an’ come
back agin like dis,” and Tommie Pipeful ran to
and fro on the floor making dismal noises as if he
required to be oiled. “Oh, Hogan’s ghost ! He
wants th’ crorss-cutsaw, Tommie.” “ Yowi, dat’s
da pfella.” Tommie carried the saw down to his
gun yah ; but by the time he got t' e r he forget
for whom he had borrowed it, and w. ile he was
trying to remember this he forgot that he had bor-
rowed anything.
“Eller”: The squatters around Narrabri
are very dissatisfied with the Govt, for not making
a start with the rabbit-proof fences to Yarraldool.
They state rabbits are increasing rapidly. If the
Govt, will take my humble advice it wiil waste no
more money this way. A rabbit-proof fence
makes little or no difference to bunny. He. climbs
them like a cat and moves north—rabbits always
have a tendency to move north— until the next
fence is met. Then he goes over that.

“E.K.”: We camped down in Snake
Valley, me and Ginger, alongside a water-hole.
Ginger played his mouth-organ ; I smoked. After
a time heard a queer hissing and rustling, looked
round, and, by gad, there was a whole army of
snakes,_ up on their hind legs, bobbing up and
down, in and out, dancing to Ginger’s music. 1

WATTLE FLAT.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
When I was digging in the hills—’way up on Wattle Flat,
A parson came to straighten us—a little one at that.
He told us we should sling the cards, and give the liquor
best—
And oh ! ’twas grand to hear the way he’d chuck it off his
chest!
Said he : “ My friends, you’re going to hell—damnation’s
very near.
You are a blanky Godless lot —you wretched slaves of
beer!
Give up your Sunday football now—avoid the flaming
pub—
And let’s improve, our minds and start a parlymsnt’ry
club.”
We reckoned that he’d struck a patch —if none would act
the goat;
And met the follering Friday to decide “ Should Women
Vote?”
The chaps rolled up to see the fun—and girls! Each
brought his own.
A bit of skirt, the parson said, would give the thing a
tone.
He wouldn't take the chair—he thought ’twas best for
one of us.
So we elected Ratty Bill—who took it with a cuss.
He always sunk a duffer, when he tried to talk— but, still,
He’d stoush a blooming bullock, so we all respected Bill.
And then the parson pitched it strong about our sisters’
rights.
But Bli-me Joe, he reckoned only them should vote as
fights.
“That bars you, then!” was my remark —which ter-
minated Joe’s.
It ain’t the chaps as flash their dukes that flglit the
willing goes 1

“ You mustn’t swear,” the Speaker said—“ You’ll break
the blanky rule.”
“When I’m wanting infermation,” said Mick, “of any
sort—
Of course, I’ll take it from a man that’s got a shingle
short.”
“ I’m boss,” said Bill; “ they’ve put me here to carry out
the law—
Sit down, and put yer flute away—or else I’ll break yer
jaw.”
Mick started poking it again—but ere he’d said it all—
The pair of them, in willing holts, were rastling for the
fall.
It was a lively argument, and, long before its close,
A dozen keen debaters were a-dressing ayes and noes.
The little devil-dodger was a-yellin’ for the p’lice ;
But we were holding down the trap to let ’em fight in
peace.
There’s whips of self-improvement in a parlyment, no
doubt,
But members find it rough when half the House is counted
out.
We drifted into sin again—being all inclined to think,
Debating far more dangerous than football, cards, or
drink 1

CECIL POOLE.
A BUSHMAN.
Now he’s with the Bushmen’s Corps.
Out back for a month or two
He went, seeking bushies’ lore,
Helping Evans in the store,
Playing work in clobber new—
Just a bounding jackaroo !
Once I saw this jaunty bloke
Up the hollow of a tree,
Puffing curls of scented smoke —
Pooled by someone for a joke.
Clinging to his shotgun he
Puffed away excitedlee.
“Hello ! ” said I, “ what’s up, mate?”
And the corrugated lout
Whispered softly : “ Hang it, wait —
Don’t spoil sport at any rate.
Here’s a ’possum up this spout;
See me smoke the beggar out! ”
S.S.

A Well-Known Railway Worker.
Mr. Harry Gibson, a well-known railway official,
ever takes an earnest if quiet interest in all that
pertains to the welfare of the state or individual.
Thus, so popular is he amongst his fellov.
workers that when he was found to be missing
from their ranks for some considerable time, his
absence occasioned great conjecture.
A reporter was sent to elucidate the mystery,
and this is what Mr. Gibson told him.
‘'Missed me from my old haunts, have you?”
said he. “Well, it has been this way. Until
lately I have not been able to get about as I used,
for my joints swelled to a great extent and became
very stiff. If I moved at all they gave me the
greatest pain. Every bone in my body ached with
rheumatism, but my shoulders, arms and legs were
the worst. At night the pain kept me awake,
liven in fine weather I suffered agonies, which
increased when it rained. As the dull, wearying
ache gave place to sharp twinges upon the slightest
movement, I did not get about much.
“ Acting upon a friend’s advice, I tried Dr.
Williams’ pink pills. The first box relieved me
and four boxes cured me. The stiffness in my
joints completely subsided, and I am active and
strong and can get about as well as ever. It is
nine months since I have used Dr. Williams’ pink
pills, and I went through last winter without one
twinge.”
The last statement shows that Mr. Gibson has
been cured permanently (otherwise the pains would
have recurred before this). Dr. Williams’ pink
pills cure permanently because they act on the
cause of disease, not on the mere signs and symp-
toms. As a blood-builder and nerve tonic they
are unequalled ; the numerous cures they have
performed prove this. These include paralysis,
dysentery, consumption, bronchitis, weak heart,
chronic dyspepsia, insomnia, ladies’ ailments,
debility, anaemia, etc. Obtainable from Dr.
Williams’ Medicine Co., Queen’s Place, Sydney,
two and nine per box, six boxes fifteen and three,
and from chemists and storekeepers. But mind
you ask for Dr. Williams’, for these are the kind
which Mr. Gibson used. *

“PENNY IN THE SLOT.”
Some of our singers might save themselves frow
injury (whilst singing the “Absent-minded
Beggar”) by adopting this idea.
an accommodating sort of man, Quinn took up a
large blue-gum shell, lying on the road amongst
the trees, and made it his home. He fenced it in
with boughs, stopped up one end with a few slabs,
and he and his wife and his brood lived in the log
for over twelve months in perfect content. At the
end of that time Quinn waited upon the manager
of the Packers’ Find, who had given him a few
jobs on the surface, and said: “ That’s a fine
roomy log bey ant there by the dam, sor.” “What
of it?” “Well, sorr, if ye have no use for the
same, an’ it bein’ near to the stiddy job what ye’ll
he givin’ me maybe, I was hopin’ ye’d let me shift
in. - ’ “Why,” laughed the boss, “isn’t the log
you’re living in snug enough ? ” “Yes, sorr, but it
ain’t roomy enough for lodgers, sorr.” “Lodgers?”
yelled the manager. “ Yes, sorr, Tim the braceman
was thinkin’ o’ lodgin’ wid us.” Tim shifted into
the new log next day.

Bill my lad beware your life is running short unless you
do as I want you to do now what you have to do don’t
smoke or d m’t drink brandy or ale and drink nothing for
four months but Hoarhound boiled and kemp and eat all
the onions you can but be sure to eat two onions every
night after you are in bed raw mind they must be large
ones now d m’t think 1 am saying this without cause I se
you and I know the same that carried your brother off
will soon have you you think 1 don’t know because I had
a drink or 2 But I can tell better don’t smoke or drink
anything but rum and bitters and beware to eat at least
two onions just as you get into bed and mind they are
large d m’t use any salt eat as many as you can in the d ay
either fried or boiled mind get the other stuff I mentioned
first if you cannot get it I will get it for you and be sure
to eat two large onions every night when you go to bed
Mrs. Brumby.
* * * *
Cattle in the West in drought times seem
to develope on camel lines, and to get an enor-
inous carrying capacity—in the water
line. Before starting off to forage for
food they will fill themselves with
water until they resemble a huge
barrel, except as regards several
obtrusive bony projections. They
then sally out to gorge themselves
with gum-leaves, bark, and fence
rails.

The ill-spelt pathos of those who

“ Dareel ” : I know a slieep-man who
won’t endorse E. J. Brady’s plea for the crow. He
lias a personal grievance. Left off pulling out
bogged sheep to have tucker, and for some reason
or other took out his gold-plated front teeth. Had
to leave hastily to catch his horse, which pulled
away and cleared out. On the way back he saw a
crow rise from the camp with something that
glittered in the sunlight. Tumbling to his loss he
galloped after the “parson,” waving his arms
about like flails and objurgating in a gummy and
profane manner in the hope that he might scare it
into dropping the set. Casual swaggies who
pas ;od during the chase had the monotony of the
track brightened by the sight, and were satisfied
they had seen a rabid crow-crank who would kill
a good horse in running after a single bird. A
wire-fence beat the horseman, and the bird flew
on to plant the seeds of another Taranganba or a
second mountain mystery in the ridges. Now the
sheep-man seeks election on the Stock Board in
order to have dentists proclaimed noxious animals.
THE AUSTRALIAN GEM TRADE.
H. Newman, the well-known jeweller of 175
Elizab“th-street, Melbourne, is the largest buyer
of opal in Australia, and opal miners will find it
to their advantage to send their opal direct to
him. The firm has a reputation for straight deal-
ing extending over a period of 40 years, which is
a sufficient guarantee to consignors that they will
be honorably treated. H. Newman is also a
bnver of all other Australian gems.*
The Royal Tar, Paraguay Lane’s early ark,
now lies routing in the Yarra River, Melbourne.
Business, Robbery, etc.
Imagination goes a long way. This from one in
the know. A cigarette factory which manufactures several
brands of cigarettes puts exactly the same tobacco in all
the different kinds. Yet you will hear someone cracking
up “ Kill me deads ” while another swears they are too
strong, whereas No. 10’s are nice and light, besides sundry
other remarks. Yet the label is the only difference.
* * * *
The sewering of Smellbourne seems to pan out
very satisfactorily for those house-property owners who
live by taking in tenants. When a landlord is charged
(say) £5O for the sewerage “ connection ” of a six or seven
room villa, he goes round to the occupant with a tear in
his eye, and feelingly adds half'-a-crown a week to the rent.
That is to say, he increases tlie value of his proputty by
£5O, and charges the other person 12) per cent, interest
on the money. Then he writes a letter to the Argus com-
plaining that a new burden of taxation has been dumped
upon his shoulders, and wanting to know why he doesn’t
get an extra vote for Thrift.
• » » •
In one town in the Woolly West there have been
recently seven fires in rapid succesiion, with a loss of
£20,000 and one human life. A spark from a shooting
star set fire to one place, and in another some rats got
drunk on kerosene and then had a snack of wax matches
—and the rest was easy. Again, at a drapery bargain-sale
the goods were going off so fast that the heat thus gener-
ated ignited the premises ; and in yet another case a
burning ember is supposed to have floated 500 miles from
a fire in Brisbane and set light to a grindstone, which in
turn caused the destruction of the establishment*.
Stimulated to active measures, the police sallied forth
and vigorously arrested three harmless citizens who were
out looking at the eclipse of the moon, and got them fined
for “ righteous ” behaviour —after which the foorce felt
better.

“C.E.” : The struggle for a crust. In a George-
street (Sydney) pub. recently I saw a dead-beat when un-
observed cautiously draw an empty pint-pot in front of
him. Then he “ interviewed ” the luncheon, washing it
down with gulps of imaginary beer from the pot.
* • * »
A Napier (M.L.) bankrupt met his creditors
recently with a tale of misplaced confidence. There had
long been a legend in the family about an enormously-
wealthy relative in America whose money would come to
them some day, and when the Bloated One really died
and left them his money, bankrupt went into debt for
various luxuries to the extent of £lBOO. Then his share
of the noble fortune proved to be £9 14s.
* • * »
There’s no fire-brigade in Suva, Fiji, and when
a blaze occurs it is great joy to see the natives rushing
around with axes, hewing down the wrong verandah-
posts, hacking at everything within a mile of the fire,
breaking windows, jumping wildly on top of perishable
goods, heaving brittle things into the road, and stealing
all the tobacco they can. A fire generally means ruin
over there, as insurance offices don’t doat on Fiji risks.

* * * *
“S.”: Have heard recently of a modest wild
cat scheme that came off. Jones was the proprietor of a
small house on a patch of land littered with hunks of
glassy, hungry-looking quartz. One day he gave it out
casually that he had found a fine specimen amongst these
stones. A little later a friend of his showed a specimen
that he had found. Jones was going up to Bendigo for a
week, but before departing let it be understood that he
had an idea of floating his property. lie went. When he
came back there was not a bit of quartz to be discovered
within a hundred yards of his house. Then Jones made
his garden.
* » * *
Hawking, to evade unpleasant police scrutiny,
is a favorite trick of the bookie-spieler now-a-days.
Bailed up, he produces license (mystery is how he gets it)
to show that he is a man with a bona-fide occupation.
One gent., intercepted lately, who said he was a hawker,
but declined to say what he hawked, was forced to disclose
contents of the bag which contained his stock-in-trade.
Contents wore a hunk of dry bread and meat and three
dice.
In one of the King-street (Sydney) sixpenny
bars lately the landlady was bemoaning the cost of the
counter-lunch. “In meat-pies alone we get through 60
dozen a week in this house, and this exclusive of sausage-
rolls, &0., and such things as bread, cold meat, hot
corned beof, and so on. The people look for them, and if
they don’t get them they won’t como.” While the lady
was talking one customer had three good cuts at a pig’s
cheek, two sausages, bread, tomatoes and onions—and
then she left for fear the spectacle would prove too much
for her good nature.

* * * *
Fitzgibbon, the autocrat of Melbourne Board of
Works, lias been badly defeated by a Chow idolator. The
worshipper of graven images runs a garden on the banks
of the Yarra, in which the asparagus is urged on its wild
career and the tomato springs up like the devices of the
ungodly. From time unknown the pagan has irrigated
his garden free of cost, and it was only recently that the
official brain “dropped” to the fact and fixed a water-
meter upon the premises. But the yellow man ingen-
iously tinkered with the indicator so that the quantity of
water passing through the meter was not registered. And
the garden flourished. After much deep thought the
Board detected the ruse, and a metal liood was placed to
fit tightly over the meter. This was held in position by
iron chains, the couplings being taped and sealed so that
any manipulation might be readily detected. But the
cabbage cultivator was equal to even this emergency. He
secured a stamp whose impression so exactly resembled
the board's seals that for a time the officers failed to dis-
cover that the Asiatic was regularly breaking the seals,
removing the chains, lifting the metal hood, blocking the
indicator, and surreptitiously irrigating his carrot
patches. When Fitzgibbon heard of this latest iniquity
the plumbers went forth and removed the water service
from the garden altogether. The resourceful Mongolian
now pumps water from the Yarra on to the melons and
asparagus beds. The board is quite cross over the matter,
and has worn the office law-book to rags hunting up
statutes to compel the pig-tailed one to pay for irrigation,
but so far the Chinaman is well ahead.

W.A. Premier Forrest is the newest kind of
mining expert, as witness this extract from a recent W.A.
Hansard :
The Premier : A reef could be dredged just in the same
way as soft stuff, and work of the kind could be seen at
Fremantle.
Mr. Kingsmill: But the reef would have to be blasted
first.
The Premier :No ; without blasting at all. Dredges
were so strong now that a great deal was being done in
this way at Fremantle without blasting.
Mr. Gregory : It would be a most inconvenient way to
work a reef.
The Premier : It appeared to be a capital way.
Mr. Gregory : How deep would a dredge be able to go ?
The Premier : A dredge would follow the reef to a
depth of 30ft. probably.

Four youths recently sued in Melbourne
District Court for wages and arrears
owing to them by a firm of elec-
trical engineers. Some months ago
the parents of the boys paid a premium
of £5O each for them to acquire a
knowledge of electrical engineering. J!o>
the lads allege that they were kept in blissful ignorance of
the business a,nd spent their time doing plumbing work
and odd jobs in the bell-hanging line. They played cards
or amused themselves anyhow they could when' business
was slack and it was a long time between bell-hangings.
Query: Isn’t it about time the premium business was
abolished? The firm which yearns after “youth with
premium to be taught the business ” is mostly the firm
which has very little business to teach the youth.
Artist A. H. Fullwood.
Artist A. H. Fullwood has turned his studio in
Paling’s Buildings into an exhibition of oil and
water-color paintings, black-and-white work,
pencil drawings and etchings—in all about 150
subjects. A catalogue has been prepared, the
pictures have been docketed, and the public is
invited to drop in and buy, for Mr. Fullwood has
definitely decided to leave Australia for fresh
fields and paddocks new. Albert Henry Fullwood
is 35 years of age, and was born in Birmingham.
As soon as he could wield a pencil he "went
to the local art-schools, and was a contemporary
student with W. J. Wainwright, R.W.S., Walter
Langley, R. 1., and several other well-known men
in the world of art. While still in his teens, or
barely out of them, he exhibited in many of the
principal exhibitions in England. When'2l years
of age Mr. Fullwood came to Australia,' and
worked for the local press. After a year of this
experience, “ The Picturesque Atlas ” was laid
upon the stocks, and he was attached to the staff
of that publication, and worked over the greater
part of what is recognised as settled Australia for
three years. Mr. Fullwood has also represented
the London Graphic for about eleven years,
during which time many of his drawings of
Australian current events have appeared. He has
from time to time contributed to the pages of The
Bulletin. Amoung the pictures now being ex-
hibited are several sketches of “Old Sydney”
Mr. A. H. FULLWOOD, a well-known Sydney
artist.
Photo., Hubert Newman.
“bits” of the city that have disappeared and are
disappearing—representations of scenery from
Maoriland, Tasmania, Sydney Harbour, “Up-
country,” etc., and a capital etching of the “Black
Horse at Richmond —a celebrated honey-moon
resort in the early days. There are several
pictures of historic interest, too, notably a large
water-color of a now defunct erection—to wit,
the old bridge at Mosman—an old whaling
station ; a black-and-white of the great Federal
meeting in the Town Hall, Sydney—the original
drawing of the picture reproduced in the London
Graphic; and the “Royal Tar” leaving Sydney
for Paraguay—another Graphic original, repre-
sentative of the New Australia movement. Two
large pictures of merit are a view of the Hawkes-
bury River, and a big interior entitled “ Bad
news from the Seat of War.”

A VOICE FROM THE “PUSH."
BRUMMY BILL: “ Why can't then send the bloomin
police to the war and give a bloke a charnst? ”
They’ve horsemen without number,
Like the bloodhounds in the slips
Their arsenals forge thunder,
Their dockyards vomit ships ;
They and their offspring allies,
Wh )’JI kill at their command,
Will cover all your valleys,
And darken all your land.
Old man, what are you doing ?
What’s now your great intent ?
Whan were such hosts on ruin
E'er by the devil sent ?
Think as the tempest gathers
Round your little ones and wives,
Stout Dutchmen saved their fathers
Their freedom and their lives !
Think when the shells are falling
Thicker than summer rain,
Your memory recalling
The locust hordes of Spain,
More foes than could be reckoned
Darkened their hollow land,
For not one single second
Did God withhold His hand !

Sleep When Work is Done.
Dining' the late summer (189 G) I passed several
weeks in a foreign city nearly 700 miles further
1 i than London. We had about ten days
n Cense heat, and for six nights when it was worst,
a ao not think I slept as many hours altogether,
xne resulting nervous collapse was simply awful,
j ~ Oram reeled and I con’d neither understand nor
a° anything rightly. I walked, as the good Book
111 a vain show. ” What a blessed thing is
Meep ; how destructive, how killing to lose it.
in healthy sleep the nervous system is inactive
tOe brain and spinal cord.
a >e body lies quiet, the muscles relaxed, the pulse
tower than when we are awake, and the breathing
, us ' s >'«quent but deeper. Then nature —wise
'eepcr of the House of Life—proceeds to renew
tbe energies expended through the day. To miss
tins renewal, even in part, is to tread the edge of
insanity and look into the mouth of death.
-Methought I heard a voice cry, ‘Sleep no
VI Macbeth doth murder sleep!’” So said
- lacUoth himself; but there is a power which
murders more sleep than ever did Duncan’s
assassin ; and to that Mrs. Beattie alludes when
she says of her daughter, “ She got no sleep.”
T *■;!* May, 1891,” writes the lady, “my daughter
Aie.itia fell into a low, weak condition, and could
not get up her strength. She had no appetite,
and what little food she took gave her great pain
at the chest and side. She got no sleep at night,
and became very nervous and low spirited.
As time passed by she grew weaker and more
aVi’ an<l vve t'D'Ught she would never get better.
-A. doctor attended her but none of his medicines
seemed to suit her case, and for two years she
continued to suffer.
“ One day she read in a book about Mother
oeigel s Curative Syrup and what it had done for
others. She got a bottle from Mr. Nicholls,
chemist, Donegal Place, and after taking it began
to improve, being able to eat, and her food
agreeing with her. After having used this medi-
cine a short time she was strong and well as ever,
and has since been in good health. For a long
t ime 1 myself suffered from weakness and indiges-
tion, and seeing what good Mother Seigel’s Syrup
had done I took it and was completely restored to
sound health, You may use this statement as
you like. (Signed) (Mrs.) Maijy Beattie, 23,
Inmty Square, Belfast, August 14th, 1896.”
•‘ln the early part of 1894,” writes another, “I
began to suffer from indigestion. I had a poor
appetite, and all food gave me pain at my chest
and a gnawing feeling at my stomach. No
matter how light the food I took I had paiii and
sick headache,
‘bur over a year I was tormented like this,
when Mr. John Weir, living in this place, told me
how he had been cured by Mother Seigel’s Syrup,
and recommended me to try it. I did so, and
soon all pain left me, and I have since enjoyed
good health. I know many persons residing in
this district who have often been benefited by
taking the same medicine. Often in my shop I
hear persons say what it has done for them. You
arc a.t liberty to publish this letter. (Signed) Alex
\v ilson, General Merchant, Castlewellan, New-
castle, Co. Down, Ireland, Aug. 15th, 1896.”
Indigestion or dyspepsia destroys the power to
sleep by starving, and thus weakening the nervous
system. Indeed the whole body starves, and is
thrown into profound disorder. Food may be
taken into the stomach, but. as people constantly
say, “It does no good. ” No ; but in a sense it
does harm. Fermented, soured, undigested it
develops poisons which act upon the system some-
what as a pestilence does upon a community,
flesh is lost, strength gives away to trembling
weakness, courage is supplanted by fear, and life
is dreary and desolate. That Mother Seigel's
Syrup should be able to cure a disease so common
and so terrible is reason for gratitude to us all.
For the healthy work and sleep.
“ With a little more experience, Bovill
will be the worst Judge on the Benoh.” — Ballan-
tine.
Septimus A. Stephen, whose London lec-
ture on “Sydney—Past and Present” is going
the rounds, was seventh in the family of the late
Chief-Justice, C.-J. Stephen was father of 18
children—nine boys and nine girls. Nine were
bom to him when a barrister, and nine when a
Judge. He had nine by his first wife and nine
by the second. Nine were bom in Tasmania and
nine in N.S.W. Five sons and four daughte.s
were born in Hobart, and four sons and five
daughters in Sydney. Two of the boys were
twins, and two of the girls. By the way, why
does not “Seppy” Stephen allow his father’s
autobiography to see the light. That would be
Sydney—“ Past ” at any rate.
“ Old Jack ” gossips concerning some old-
time N.S.W. litterateurs:—
“ Kendall’s letter to Harpur deals with Stenhouse as a
critic; his memorial verses deal with him as a man."
Thus “ Red Page ”of 28th Oct. But those who suppose
that poets—especially of Kendall's build—write memorial
verses, or critical sneers, because they are saturated with
conviction of the fidelity of their motives, want to go to
school. Kendall, of all men, could work up a spurious or
artificial enthusiasm, which would take this shade or that
ae«)rdin»-to his humor. When his humor hadn't been
sufficiently stimulated it was inclined to rail at the hand
that gave. Indeed, that was the poor devil’s prevailing
humor. Weak, vain, childishly sensitive, and without
the capacity of being contented with his gifts, but afflicted
with an itch of envy of those who possessed higher, Ken-
dall was always eating his own heart. By the way, when
present writer first met him he was eating pork pies and
drinking rum arid water, in the Currency Lass tavern.
Also he was writing a “poem” called “Manasseh ” —a
noble apostrophe, which he asked me to read and then
presented to me. The grease-marks of his fingers linger
still on the paper. I once found Kendall in pawn in a
Waterloo pub. Had stuck up drinks, and Mr. Public
House had collared his boots and hat as collateral se-
curity. “If even I were to write his epitaph, dear boy,”
said the poet, tearfully, 11 he wouldn’t take it in exchange
for my boots." “ What’s an epitaph ?” demanded Bun<'.
“ A lie on a gravestone,” said Kendall. “If you give me
my hat and boots I’ll tell the biggest lie about you that
imagination could devise." “ You’re a rum un,” said the
publican. “ Here you are; ” and he handed over the hat
and boots. By the way, the “boots” were slippers.
Poor K. had “ got out” by the back gate

“ H.” : The case in which Edward Gibbon
Wakefield, afterwards so illustrious as a coloniser
in South Australia, Maoriland, and Canada,
served three years’ imprisonment for abducting
an heiress, is worth some detail, being almost
unknown. It was in 1826, Wakefield being a
young widower. His first marriage had been to a
supposed heiress, but it appears he was taken in,
and his heiress had no money. He lived in Paris,
where his young step-mother came on a pleasure-
trip. She told him she had found him a real
heiress, sweet Ellen Turner, 15 years old, only
child of the Cheshire sheriff, who resided on his
Estate of Shrigley. Wakefield had never seen or
heard of this girl. Nevertheless the abduction
was at once planned, the stepmother being the
brain of the whole affair. Miss Turner was cap-
tivating and described as intelligent, notwith-
standing the stupidity with which she acted.
Wakefield had no money, but the indispen-
sable step-mother went to a money-lender on
a Sunday, and borrowed £l5O, representing that
it was to liberate a relative arrested for debt.
The first thing Wakefield had to do was to extract
Miss Turner from the four Miss Daulbys. These
gorgons kept the Liverpool boarding school at
which Miss Turner was. By an incredible con-
catenation of successful lying about family cir-
cumstances of the Turners, Wakefield persuaded
the eldest and presumably most discreet Miss
Daulby to relinquish custody of the girl. The
next step was to persuade Miss Turner that she
must marry Wakefield, to secure payment cf
£60,000 to her father, who was alleged to be in
dire monetary straits. All this reads like the plot
of a bad novel, but is absolute fact. They went
off to Gretna Green, and were married.
Wakefield stopped, according to the evidence,
at the mere ceremonial, but had the girl
in his possession for a week. They went
next to Calais (France) where the explosion took
place. Miss Turner’s two uncles and the family
solicitor had got on the scent. They saw the
pair looking into shop windows and laughing.
The next step was tracking. them to Madame
Quillac’s hotel. Here an infuriated uncle burst in
upon Wakefield, Miss Turner not being present.
After much violent language Wakefield brought
her in. She clung to him at first, but hearing of
the deception, and the agony of her father and
mother, she rushed to her uncle’s protection,
vowing she would never look on Wakefield again.
Apparently she never . did, except in their
encounter at the Lancashire assizes, where he was
convicted and sentenced, together with his brother
William as an accomplice. The stepmother
escaped punishment, though also convicted. In
Newgate Wakefield studied up colonial questions.
Thus the carcase yielded honey, or at least
history.
Ex-M.L.A. Tommy Walker, whom Syd-
ney, Melbourne and other Australian centres
remember with mixed feelings, orates Sunday
nights in Perth on the virtues of John Forrest,
horrest in his recent tour frequently referred to
lommy as a remarkably eloquent and convincing
orator “the finest speaker I have heard any-
W j r -o” If Tomm y gets into the W.A. House
and Big John lasts, Westralia may yet be bossed
by the mutually admiring pair.

POLITICAL
POINTS
A. J. Wilson, of the Investors’ Review,
estimates that the war is costing Great Britain
£2,000,000 per week.
“ Now is the time for the long-talked-of
morning Protectionist paper in Sydney,” was said
to a member of the Lyne Ministry the other day.
“ The Daily Telegbaph is doing very well,” was
the reply.
Vic. A.N.A. at Tts annual congress is to
deal with a proposal to admit Britishers-born of
five years’ Australian standing to membership,
and to change the name to The Australian
National Association. Thus does the new Ass
rise from the old one’s grave.
Five of the W.A. Transvaal contingent
came to grief the other day—one killed and four
wounded. They included two Victorians, one
Cornstalk, one Englishman and a West Indian—
not a Sandgroper amongst them. About the
scarcest individual in the Sandgroping contingent
is the Sandgroper himself.
The Vic. Education authorities have been
holding an indignant inquiry into the case of a
female teacher, who, it seems, expressed her sor-
row that there should be any war. The results
are not quite certain yet, but it is expected that
the lady will be let off with a warning to walk the
chalk line of strict loyalty in future, and say she
is Sarned glad of the war.
Ex-Premier Dickson left Brisbane the
other day for England, bearing letters of in-
troduction to Newcastle-on-Tyne people from
Thomas Glassey. The anxiety displayed by the
latter politician to be in some way identified with
every movement bearing the Q. Govt, brand is
amusing. As for stump orations and organising
missions (as in the days of yore)—T.G. knows them
no longer.
Another effort will be Ifiade in M.L. Par-
liament next session to remove the embargo from
sweeps. It is considered that Bill will have a better
chance this time in Lower House, at any rate.
Previously it has been sat upon in only a gentle
sort of fashion. The amount of bullion sent from
the province to- Australia since prohibition of
these little gambles would about buy both Islands.
But if sweeps are allowed, why shouldn’t the
State run its own sweeps ?
“Vic.”: The fund for'the widow and
orphans of John Hancock, M.L. A., could do with
more than a little assistance from other provinces.
It may be just my private delusion, but I hold
that Hancock would have proved second in value
to no member of the Australian Labor-party.
Anyhow, he served this country with all his might
while he lived, and now he is quite as dead as any
soldier who can possibly be killed ~ out Boer-
hunting, and quite as worthy.
Official utterances on same day of last
week: Premier Lyne—“N.S.W. Agent-General
will not be a commercial man, but will be assisted
by a commercial man”; “Minister for Mines
Began—“ Our next Agent-General must be a com-
mercial man.” But what does N.S.W. want any
Agent-General for ? In a few months more there
will be one A.G. for all the five federated pro-
vinces. To appoint a local A.G. now is simply to
give some friend or hanger-on a possible claim for
compensation.
The unexpected thing which was named
Higgins didn’t really happen in Victorian politics
after all. The Turner party decided not to sup-
port Higgins’s motion of censure on M‘Lean, and
the bald one, having no party of his own, didn’t
move the vote after all. The spectacle of the anti-
Federal Higgins wanting to throw out the anti-
Federal M‘Lean so as to help Federation, didn’t
seem to appeal to anybody. So Higgins darkened
the subject by a long, obscure explanation and
then dropped it.
The International Labor Conference
which met at Sydney to draw up a Federal pro-
gramme decided, among other things, that it
believed in the principle of old-age pensions. But
when a rash member proposed that the necessary
expense should be defrayed out of revenue the
conference wouldn’t hear of it. It evidently
wasn’t quite sure that even old-age pensions
shouldn’t come out of loans and be called a “ per-
manent and reproductive work,” and it declined
to commit itself.
Victorian P.M.Gr. Watt upended himself
and said that he was hostile to the despatch of the
first crowd of Boer-hunters (he was then only Watt,
Nothing), but he regarded his share in the des-
patch of the second one as the proudest
act of his life (he was then Hon. Watt, P.M.G,)
In the present state of popular frenzy Watt, if he
hadn’t approved of the second contingent, would
have certainly gone out of the Ministry on his
bright young ear. So he wisely hung on to his
portfolio and approved.
The voice of Ahab, who was a wise king in
Israel years ago :
Let not him that girdeth on his harness boast himself as
he that putteth it off.
And the voice of Hassall, M.L.A., who is an un-
wise member of a Cow-Shaking Government in
New South Wales :
The Australian troops . . . had taken the Boers in
hand, and the Boers would find to their cost who they had
to deal with before the Australians were done with them.
He only wished he could go right into the thick of the
campaign with a good strong force of Australian bush
riders behind him. If so, he would guarantee that the
result would be a revelation, not only to the Boers, but to
all the world. The first thing he would do would be not
to charge them in the front in the regular army style of
doing that kind of thing, but would make a raid into the
country behind entrenchments and cut off their supplies.
With a sufficient number of the right kind of Australians
he would undertake to shake every horse, cow, calf, and
sheep within 100 miles of wherever the Boers might collect
their forces.
ALL THE DIFFERENCE.
RECRUIT: “I hear, sir, you
want recruits?”
CAPTAIN: “ Yes; hut
did you not yet your
discharge about a year
ago?”
R.: “ Yes, sir; but I was engaged to
my girl, and the regiment was going to England."
C.: “ It’s going to the Transvaal now.”
R.: “ I know, sir; but I’m married now, sir.”
“ H.G.” : The further the war progresses
the further the British War Office exposes either
its hopeless incompetence or its utter corruption.
The latter seems to be the more likely. An Aus-
tralian inventor, who left for England some
months ago with a model of a new slaughter
machine and £5OO cash, returned sorrowfully the
other day, as his means wouldn’t suffice to square
even the office-boy of the department. The way
that Ashton’s magazine-rifle has hung fire in Eng-
land, after the exhaustive and very satisfactory
trials, is another instance—to say nothing of the
refusal to look at Ashton’s high explosive at any
price, the prolonged silence about Alcock’s
position-finder, the value of which has been
mathematically proved, and the shelving of Body’s
submarine torpedo-boat. These things indicate
that somebody is making a nice little bit out of
the British taxpayer.
G. H. Reid, having returned to Sydney,
has laid down his fighting platform so far as he
(being always in the provisional state) ever lays
down any tiling, except himself when he is going
to sleep. He claims most of the Lyne legislation
and finance as his; but he won’t own the Early-
Closing Act at any price. He says:
Such of the Sydney papers as I have seen suggest that
the expected has happened, and that what might have
been an unmixed blessing—for the object of the Act is
one of the noblest —is being tainted by its injustice, and
discredited by its imperfection. I suspend my final
opinion upon the working of the measure until I can
judge for myself.
In other words, Reid hasn’t had time to decide
yet whether the small shopkeepers who are agitat-
ing against the Act have a majority behind them
or not, and he is once more waiting to see how the
cat jumps. G. Reid has had one eye on the cat
and the other on his victuals all the days of his
life.
One or two members of the N.S.W.
Cabinet are in favor of meeting the impending
war-bill by increasing the tax on incomes derived
from property from 6d. to 9d. in the £. Such a
tax, if maintained for seven or eight years, should
about cover the bill, provided the war doesn’t last
very much longer. But a multitude of the people
who helped to make the war will be dead before
eight years have passed, and a multitude who had
no voice in making it will be liable to taxation
before then. Moreover, if the present military
and loyal fervor keeps up, Australia may have
another war or two on hand long before it has
finished paying for this one. The short way out,
and the honest way, is to put the income-tax up
to Is. in the £ and get rid of the lamentable bill
quickly. If the people are patriotic enough to yell
and not patriotic enough to pay, their patriotism
isn’t worth much.
EDITOR (to applicant for the place as descriptive reporter): “Have you (jot a fragrant fancy
and boundless imagination, young man? ”
APPLICANT: “ For twelve months I have written up interviews with persons who were cured by
Boiled Eggs for Exasperated People. ’
EDITOR: “ Consider yourself engaged, young man, at your own terms.”
An intelligent and honest kind of jingo—
the town clerk of Hawthorn—has suggested that
every local government body in Victoria should
levy an extra rate of id. or Id. in the £ in aid of
the war fund. Considering that such a large
section of the Victorian people throws up its joint
and several hat—not to speak of the rest of its
clothes - for the war, this business-like proposal to
start paying for it even in a small way ought to
meet with the approval of everybody. Yet
strangely enough it doesn’t seem to have met with
the cordial support of anybody.
The Sandgroper Government sends Parker,
Q.C., to England at large expense to “watch the
passage of the Commonwealth Bill.” As Westra-
lia has absolutely nothing to do with the Common-
wealth Bill, Parker, Q.C., will presumably stand
outside Parliament House, Westminster, and look
up at the windows while the Bill is going through.
It is about the only way he can watch it. Still, he
may be John Forrest’s 42nd or 27th cousin, and
he, perhaps, requires change of air at the public
expense. All the influential people in W.A.
require occasional change of air at the public ex-
pense.
There is a tendency for wages to go up in
Victoria owing to the departure of so many men for
the war. This fact was the subject of remark the
other day by an alarmed member of the Legisla-
tive Council. “But surely,” he exclaimed, “for
reasons of patriotism, men will never ask for a rise
in wages at a time like this ! The man who asks
for a rise because of the shortness of labor at this
time is a damned traitor to his country ! ” “ What
of the man who reduced wages because of the
abundance of labor ? ” said the other. Then the
M.L.C. got angry, and called the other a Boer spy,
or something to that effect.
A few days ago ex-Preinier Reid made this
apparently innocent remark :
The civil servant who honestly believes that England is
engaged in an immoral campaign should no more be
silenced than t, who conscientiously believe that the war
was absolutely necessary.
It seemed a perfectly innocent observation in
defence of the most elementary principles of
liberty and free speech. Yet the grovelsome
Australian Star was roused to frenzy, and
yelled in great headlines “ Treason in the Service,”
“ Applauded by Mr. Reid.” So freedom of
thought, which was all Reid contended for, is
Treason nowadays, and the doctrines of the Inqui-
sition are in full-blast once more, or in as full-
blast as the Cow-Shaking Ministry and its organ
can manage.
Tasmania’s new Premier tells a great
truth:—
Renewals and repairs of roads, bridp-es and jetties have,
in spite of many protests, been from time to time charged
to loans. This is, in my opinion, an improper proceeding.
Once a work, of whatsoever nature, has been charged to
loans, any renewal should be made chargeable to revenue.
The principle we shall seek to adopt is to maintain out of
revenue, to the full value of the amount expended on it,
such assets as have been constructed by loan money.
It is a good, honest idea, only it has taken a very
long time to get as far as these first rudiments of
honesty. And, having got thus far, is Premier
Lewis prepared to pass an Act making it illegal
and punishable for any Government to charge
patches and repairs to loans ? Having found out,
in however small a way, what is right, is he
courageous enough to so arrange things that the
right policy shall be the permanent fixed policy of
the country ? When a Government finds out to
its great surprise that burglary is wrong it makes
burglary illegal. When it finds out that the mis-
appropriation of loan moneys is wrong, why not
make that illegal also ?
“Clipper” Paton, the democratic can-
didate for ensuing Hobart election, has just had
a lesson from what P.O. Fysh used to call “the
Tasmanian mob.” Paton was Holman’s chairman
when that eloquent anti-jingo was mobbed by a
crowd of “ patriotic ” hoodlums in Hobart recently,
and his dreadful sin has found him out. Attempted
to address the electors on the democratic platform
at the local Town Hall and was howled down. He
stuck to Lis task bravely for half an hour, but as
he couldn’t even hear himself speak, he de-
cided to give up the attempt. Men-o’-wars-
men from the Squadron were present in force,
but the cowardly unfairness of the proceed-
ings appealed to the soberest of them so
strongly that some half-dozen brawny tars formed
a body-guard and probably protected Paton from
personal violence. When Hobart’s large en-
lightened free citizens have to take a lesson in
decency from men-o’-warsmen, the “loyalty” of
that dreary village must be something too dread-
ful for respectable people to associate with. Still
“British fair-play” came on top that time. It
was British fair-play that kept Tasmanian hood-
lumism at bay.

Melb. Herald is struggling to popularise
barley water, and points out that Lord Brassey
had it prepared for his use daily. Barley water
the reader is assured, is extremely nourishing.
How explain Lord Thomas’s falling off, then?
In Brunswick (Victoria), the town whore
the Orange and Green shindies are, a local
householder recently painted a verandah-post
yellow. He meant to make it a bronze green
(paint green over yellow and partly rub off), but
when he was still in the first stage of the work an
indignant navvy with straws m his hair and three
pints of beer in his head interposed with a cry of
rage : “ What the ‘L d’ye mane by puttin on that
bl ash ted color?” he demanded warmly. the
artist explained that he was going to put green on
top of the yellow. “Is that it ? ” said the mollified
warrior. Begorra, come an av a drink, ino
bhoy or a dozen if ye want them. Come on. But
wait till I spit on the post.” And he expectorated.
“ E.K.” : Been reading a lot of the latest
books, newest style—all creeping things: hospitals,
gaols, madhouses, and so on. But not one on the
bone-yard. Could not some of your good! men
spend a night in one and write? Call it—“ Tomb-
stones that talk in the night.” (N.B.—“ Ships that
pass in the Night” is written on a consumptive
hospital—a very funnv book.) The tall, thin stone
could tell a story of how she lingered and wasted
away, and her dear children ditto. Then
the little fat stone that laughed and told inquest
stories. The Humor of the Post Mortem. Also,
the stone cut off in his prime could be made chip
in, with some witty remarks on sudden deaths
he d seen, and dear little Arthur (in stone —age 5)
might moralise and let off a hymn and a pious
reflection. The public don’t want to laugh—they
want to creep. The above is new—the idea—not
the corpses. Also, it is creepy—not to say crawly.
The Fearful Summer Heat
m the Australian Back Country
ENERVATES THE STRONGEST MEN AND WOMEN.
An Interesting Case Illustrating the Virtues of Clements Tonic*
THE CASE OF MBS. ANNIE LEESE.
(Reported for “ Redfern Times.”)
'Not far from the Exhibition Building our
reporter found No. 37 George Street,
Redfern, the residence of Mrs. Annie Leese,
a well known fashionable dress maker.
The lady chanced to be at home and
received her visitor with every courtesy.
“I have called on you, Mrs. Leese,” said
the journalist, “ for confirmation of a state-
ment that has reached me :—That fpr a
long time you were a great sufferer from in-
digestion or dyspepsia and made quite a sen-
sational recovery by taking Clement’s Tonic.”
oat is quite true,” replied Mrs. Leese.
“ '' ould you be good enough to give me
the details ? ” asked the reporter.
_ “ Certainly; and you may be sure the
circumstances are such that I am never
likely to forget them. We were living at
Nyngan at the time, a town with a fearful
summer heat and most uninteresting
surroundings. I think these circumstances
combined to start my malady. First the
great heat, next, the want of exercise, for
there was no inducement to go driving or
walking in that bare, monotonous place.
And I am quite aware that in my sedentary
profession somephysicalexerciseisabsolutely
y to preserve good health. My
Bickness came on gradually, and for some
time quite unperceived, and for a good time
after the first symptom of trouble I gave
it no thought or attention—l have always
been careless of myself in that way. At
last the insidious trouble could be no longer
I recognised that I was in a ;
very bad way. My whole digestive system
seemed to be thoroughly disorgan'sed and
run down. After eating, or trying to eat,
I experienced all over niy body a fearful
sense of fulness hard to describe in words.
It was as though up to the throat I was
distended to the point of bursting, a stifling,
straining sensation with pain in the neck.
And on my chest there was a feeling of
great weight as though au immense load
lay there trying to suffocate me. At my
back, between my shoulder blades, there
was a constant dull aching pain, and lower
down there was another intolerable pain ia
the right side below the ribs. But this was
not enough—my cup of misery had to be
filled to the brim ; my head constantly
ached and to my disordered vision all
outward objects seemed to go round and
round in endless revolution. My days and
nights were passed in misery and despair.
Then I would long for food with the desire
of great appetite ; but when it was brought
and waiting for me I could not eat it.
And how I longed for rest, for a good sound
sleep once again, and escape, if only for a
little time, from my pains and miseries ;
but the sleep would not come. Daily I got
steadily worse and worse, my mind filled
with gloom and dreadful forebodings, I
came to that pass as to utterly despair of
myself and the future.”
“ What remedies did"' you seek, Mrs.
Leese ?” encpiired the reporter.
fy“ A doctor treated me for some time,
trying first one thing and then another, but
none of his medicines gave me any
permanent * relief, my malady seeming
altogether beyond his skill. -Then I tried a
variety of remedies so numerous that now I
could not count them. It appeared I was
doomed, that in the whole world nothing
would cure, that my state was beyond
human aid. It chanced one day that my
husband had a fortunate inspiration—he said
to me:—‘Why don’t you try Clements
Tonic? you have heard what a splendid
medicine it is, and what wonderful cures it
has effected; perhaps it would cure you.’ ”
“ And did you take Mr. Leese's advice ? ”
enquired the reporter. , *
“Yes, and thankful I am to say I did,
for when I had purchased a large bottle and
toon after I experienced a wonderful relief
from all my suffering, and a change so
sudden for the better that was hardly
credible to anyone who had not experienced
it. And a 3 I continued taking Clements
Tonic iny serious troubles diminished.
The terrible feeling of inflation, that had
almost stifled me, subsided and troubled me
no more ; the dull pain between my shoulder
blades passed away. My appetite returned
to its normal condition and I could eat and
drink with relish. About the same time
my headache left me, also the disordered
vision that caused all objects in my range
of vision to appear whirling round. Before
taking Clements Tonic the slightest worry
was disti’essing to me, causing violent
headache, arising, without doubt, from my
shattered nervous system ; hut now I was
so braced and strengthened that this
symptom passed with all my other troubles,
and I was once more my old self again.”
“Then I may count you, Mrs. Leese,”
enquired the reporter, “as one of the large"
majority who swear by Clements Tonic,? ”t
¥ “ You may. I know what it can do, and
what it has done. I have constantly
recommended Clements Tonic to my friends
and customers in all cases I thought it
would benefit.”
“ “ May I make this testimony public, Mrs.
Leese? ”
“You are very welcome, and you may
add that I shall be pleased to confirm my
wordstoanyperson whomayread what I have
said, ayd who will take the trouble to call
at my residence.”
STATUTORY DECLARATION.
I, Annie Leese, of 37 George Street, Redfern,
Sydney, in the Colony of New South Wales, do
solemnly and sincerely declare that 1 have carefully
read the annexed document, consisting of four folios
and consecutively numbered from one to four,
and that it contains and ie a true and faithful
account of my illness and cure by Clements Tonic,
and also contains my full permission to publish the
same in any way ; aDd I make this solemn declaration
conscientiously believing the same to be true, and by
virtue of the provisions of an Act madeand passed in
the ninth year of the reign of her present Alajosty,
intituled “ An Act for the more effectual abolition of
Oaths and Admirations taken and made in the
various Departments of the Government of New
South Wales, and to substitute Declarations in lieu
thereof, and for the suppression of voluntary and
extra-judicial Oaths and Affidavits.”
Declared at Redfern this fifth day of July, one
thousand eight hundred and ninety-nine, before me,
W. J. MacDONNELL, J.P.

Red Bird Crank Bracket.

Red Birds.
•As illustrated, the complete bearing is in a steel barrel, which holds the cups
in perfect alignment. Thus the bottom bracket bolts cannot distort the bearing
cups and cause them to run tight. The cups are designed so the axis of the balls
and axle are parallel, and the balls run in a continuous oil bath.
The cranks are secured to axle by means of a hexagon taper, which is further
secured by means of a cap screw.
The cranks cannot possibly work loose as with other forms of attachment.
This is another part where the Red Bird is years in advance.

SPORTING NOTIONS.
Interprovincial bantam - scrap, Wal. M‘Kay
(Vic.) against Bert Weekes (N'.S.W.) at Golden Gate A.C.
(Sydney), Saturday night, proved a hashing, hanging,
crashing bout. It lasted 80min. (20 rounds), and was full
of fire the whole time. From the beginning till the fifth
of the term had expired it looked a guinea to a goose-
berry on one, and he the Smellbourne iittle-’un. Bert,
however, came along here with a clatter. Then there
were two in it for a while, and, by-and-bye, only one
again, hut t’other chap now. They stood toe to toe and
poured out thumps heavy and fast. Wal. rushed and
VVeekes uppercut him fiercely and often. Both men
reeled from the force of the punches, but came again
chockful of vim. They got together, hooked, ripped
and swung till the animal in the hundreds of excited
onlookers fairly howled. M’Kay’s optics gave pal-
pable evidence of the wear-and-tear of the fray.
Half-a-dozen times he hung out signals of distress
and seemed on the verge of going under, but grit and
ring-cunning of the highest quality saved him, and he
weathered the storm long enough to recuperate and
make Weekes wince some more. The last round was
fierce ; each made for a knock-out. Close, hot fighting,
clinches and quick breaks occurred a dozen times till the
end, when the referee gave his decision to Weekes, and
the crowd endorsed it.
* • * •
“Old Timer”: Had a look at second match
between Australian top-rung bantams Bert Weekes and
Wal M‘Kay on Saturday night. M‘Kay’s hard old mug
and stockv-built frame were in striking contrast with
Weekes’ tall, lean, fragile-looking figure and boyish face.
On appearances it was any odds against the latter. M‘Kay
hadn’t the condition he had before ; Weekes was better.
M‘Kay was a known quantity to Weekes this time, and
the influence of his long list of 70 battles without a knock
out did not weigh worth a cent, consequently Weekes got
to it charged to the brim with confidence. The gruel
served out on both sides was of the hottest and thickest.
Streaky-formed Bert’s capacity for taking punishment
came as a revelation. Vicious short-arm smashes in the
ribs did little more than shake the attenuated object of
impact up momentarily and leave a big red blot on the
clear white skin. Weekes has the knack of hitting, he hits
straight and quick and consequently hits effectively every
time. He would have been the champion in his division
ten years ago when we had champions here; what must
be said of him now compared with the very poor material
in evidence 1 Worth mentioning that both were weighed
at noon on the day of the fight and each brought the beam
to 7.9 exactly.
« • • *
Champion 9-stoner Tim Hegarty, and cyclist-
trainer Billy Williams, are matched to fight in Sydney at
9st. 61b. on March 19. Saw Billy have a willing and cor-
dial spar with Otto Cribb at Seale’s Gymnasium on Mon-
day afternoon. Place was filled with sports. Old-man
Billy’s condition was a long way below requirements, but
he shaped well; still it looked as if the flag hail only to go
up and Otto would have outed him. “ Cribb’s too husky
and heavy for me,” said Billy afterwards ; “ but I’ll settle
Hegarty—see if 1 don’t.”
» * * *
Sydney Bocks crack light-weight “Cocker”
Tweedie, he of the American figures of speech and the
nose-twang, gave a taste of his quality at Melbourne last
week. Willing Jock M'lvor was the subject operated
upon. Jock set a cyclonic gait from “ go.” Brain-
wrecking left jabs in the neighborhood of the breathing
apparatus (during round two) started the gore on both
sides with a lively flow. After that, though Mac kept
coming strong and forcing matters (apparently insensible
to Cocker’s battering) the Sydney man did all the effective
work and scored points till referee (Tut llyan) gave up
reckoning, knowing that nothing but a knock-out would
win for the Victorian bruiser. Jock was spread-eagled
three times in the tenth round, but came up so full of
energy that he managed to lay Tweedie out once. The
uproar here was paralysing. Mac. caught it so hot and
strong in the eleventh round that his back bumped the
boards with a resounding bump thrice successively. Then
the crowd screeched “ Take him away ! ” but he wasn’t
going to be taken away. The gritty Scot was allowed to
stagger, lurch and sprawl till Tweedie got the come-hither
home and laid his man out limp. Ned Cullen will be
Cocker’s next vis-a-vis, and then Tim Hegarty, if Tim is
not otherwise engaged and will waive that weight stipula-
tion.
Yankee mail Moana brings details of the fight
in New York last month between Son-of-Ham Geo. Dixon
and Terry M‘Govern for the bantam-weight champion-
ship of the world. The attendance was enormous.
Money had to be refused. Said that gate reached 24,000
dols., of which sum winner got 9000 and loser 3000.
Promoters, managers and other hangers-on came in for
the balance. M'Govern won in eight rounds. He went
for the body at the outset, sending both hands home with
telling effect. Dixon adopted Englishman Pedlar
Palmer’s methods and played into M‘Govern’s hands by
rushing. The pace was terrific for four rounds.
M‘Govern proved absolutely irresistible ; he bore in
with cyclonic force as strong as a bull and full of
fight. Dixon stood an awful rain of thumps. In the
eighth 3mins. he was a horizontal nigger eight times and
limp, bordering upon lifeless. His manager then skied
the sponge amid a scene of intense excitement.
JPGovern’s latest battle supplies a true line on his go with
the English wonder. Pedlar Palmer, last year. British
sports argued that M'Govern’s win was one of the luckiest
flukes any Yankee ever had. Outcome of the fight, with
the colored phenom. gives matters a different complexion.
Dixon ranked among the cleverest pugs, the world has
seen. He is now in his 30th year, and he has been on the
top rung since 18, while M'Govern won’t be 21 till next
month. Last-named is probably as good already as ever
the black was.

Letters by Monday’s American mail tell that
whilom Australian pug.-marvel and world’s premier
feather, Griffo, has come down with a crash again.
After taking Griff from the lunatic asylum “ Gentle-
man ” George Dawson (also from this quarter)
tended him like a mother, togged his charge in gay
apparel, and sent him away to the hot springs to build
up. Griff met two crack Yanks —Lewis and Kenny—in
quick succession and gave them no hope. Both were
limited-round goes. Frequent flashes of the Sydney
Rocks winder’s old-time brilliancy brought the house
down. As before he didn’t seem capable of landing a
stiff punch on the man he was fighting, but it is equally
true that the man he was fighting couldn’t land any sort
of a punch on him. Later, Griffo got a fat screw' for a
week’s showing in a Chicago theatre, but the adulation of
the push settled him, and, less than a month since, he
was reported in a chronic condition of drunk and alto-
gether beyond pulling round.

The big guns of pugdom — Jim J effries and Jim
Corbett—fight for the world’s supremacy on 15th prox.
First-named says he’ll give sailor Sharkey another chance
a fortnight later, and will take Bob Fitzsimmons on four
weeks afterwards. A huge order this to execute in
12 months —and Jim Jeff talks of doing it in two. Odds
are that Sharkey won’t get another opportunity to maul
and wrench the carcase of the Los Angeles giant, nor will
elongated, auburn-haired Maoriland Bob be allowed to
reverse that very fishy-smelling result of a year ago if
Jeffries can help it, and he’s master of the situation.
* * » *
“ Griff ”: One who knows present bantam
champion pug. of the globe (M'Govern) says he’s a
thinker—he fights his own way, body-blows are his forte,
he is tireless, swift as a tiger, and crafty, and prefers to
shoot a volley of short-arm punches over the heart
in a mix-up. When he dons his verdant togs and
faces an opponent, the modest smile fades into a look
of seriousness which, later, gives way to one of grim
determination. No time or energy is expended in upper-
cutting the atmosphere or pounding the wind ; it’s all
real right down biz. from the jump. He goes for his
man straightway and bangs like a lost soul pounding on
the door of Paradise. Mac. is intuitively a financier.
“ Since I started fighting 2£ years ago,” he said recently,
“ I have made 25,000d01., and am worth 18,000dol. to-day.
Out of my winnings I’ve provided a home for my mother
and my two brothers, and one in Brooklyn for the wife.
Much of the money has been made backing myself. I’ve
never smoked, drunk, or chewed.” The Yankee freak
will probably tackle the feather division after this.
Cheap and nasty novice tourneys are surfeiting
Sydney patrons of the pug. game. Metropolitan and
Golden Gate Associations are running one each now for
the 8.7 section, and giving away 80 sovs. prize-money.
The fighting, as a rule, is of the crudest. Seventy-
five per cent, of the competitors are so unfit that they lie
down, dead to this sphere for the time being, from the
effects of their own futile efforts, rather than the stoush
dealt out by the other man, who, in many cases has no
stoush concealed about him. Few heats extend over two
rounds —most end inside one, and many fail to last half-a-
minute. Clues (only such in name) find novice affairs
exceedingly profitable biz. Money given is trifling as
compared with “gate” realised, for the scrapping sees
several nights through. Worst of it, and what palls more
than aught else, is the fact that the same old adamantine
chivvies and unsocked and dirt-encrusted extremities are
in evidence tourney after tourney, and the same old
ancient odour arises from the cognoscenti always present
on 35., ‘2s. and Is. nights. The prawn-like ones are
away at the “two-up” school, when the tariff is lifted
to 55., 3s. and 2s. for something bearing the imprint
“ extra special.”

started first favorite and pulled up behind the whole

out of the way to find So much support. Parthian is now

will hump 8.7, and looks unusually good. Five to one is

winner of two-year-old race at Rosehill, Sydney, Saturday),

Pilgrim’s Progress—Zeph colt Palmer’s win of
rich Futurity Slakes (200.0 sovs.) at Caulfield, on Saturday,
was about as big a reversal of form as has been sprung
upon the longsuffering punter lately. He ran almost last
in Oakleigh Plate a week before, when Isaac Mo would have
allowed you to name your own odds about him. He
might not have given you the odds after you named them
for I. Mo has reserves of caution, but he would have let
you name them right enough. Seven to 1 was the best
available on Saturday—a ton of money going on. St.
Albans 2-year old Kinglike, by Wallace—Bonnie Rosetta
went out favorite but could get no nearer than fourth
—Olaf filling second and Songbird third positions. Present
owner, young Jimmy Wilson, bred Palmer and trained
him privately at Queenscliff, that quarter noted for dark
’uns. Melbourne Cup winner, Merriwee, frinstans. Which

“ leaked out ” that Merriwee’s Australian Cup chance is

shouldn’t he scared —nothing “ leaks out’’from Queens-
cliff that isn’t wanted to leak.

“Tote”: He Bulletin par, 10/2/’OO, concerning
Saraswati-The Watchdog biz at Warwick Farm (N.S.W.)
previous Saturday and consequent greasing of that
already very fat sow, the shop double-layer. Something
similar happened at Bushmen’s Contingent Races (Rand-
wick) Friday last; also, same two nags and owner were
involved. This time the double—The Watchdog and War
God—had been backed in shops ; not Saraswati and War
God as at Warwick Farm. Saraswati and The Watchdog
were let loose in Contingent Handicap. The Watchdog was
heavily wagered on at fives, while Saraswati went to tne
post a rank outsider (Tucka Tucka Smith is said to have
won 200 to 10 about him), yet Saraswati finished half a
neck in front of his stable chum who came as if fired from
a catapult, but came just too late. If time (1.14) may be
accepted, The Watchdog’s Newmarket chance looks par-
ticularly rosy, as he was one of the last to get away and,
at the end, shot out from almost nowhere. Saturday at
Rosehill (Sydney) races Saraswati, humpingOlbs. less than
it won with on Friday, started a red-hot 6 to 4 favorite for
Sprinters’ Handicap over same distance as Contingent
Handicap and finished about last in a field of nine and in
2sec. slower time than that put up the day before.
The bookmaker is a hard-faced person, with a
business jaw. The average backer, when he has a
win, opens out and plunges, but the more a booky
wins the less he is inclined to take risks. At Rose-
hill, on Saturday, after four consecutive red-hot favorites
had gone down—in two cases to unbacked outsiders—the
books' opened out on the February Handicap and reck-
lessly volunteered to “ take ” 5 to 4 about St. Clare, or
bet 4 to 1 bar one. The public backed St. Clare at this
price, and Bombshell, an outsider, straightway went out
and won the event amidst much joyous yell from the
Ring. Then the books cheerfully cried—“ I’ll pay this
winner 1 ” (they always loudly offer to “ pay this winner ”
when there’s no paying to speak of to do—otherwise
they keep quiet) and offered open-heartedly to lay 3 to 1
bar two for the Final Welter.
But the most surprising case of “remarkable im-
provement ” in a horse Sydney has seen for months is
that of Aberdare, winner of the hurdle events at Rand-
wick and Rosehill on Friday and Saturday last. About a
month ago Aberdare started in a hurdle race at Canter-
bury and labored in a long last, Tiie Bulletin at the time
referring to him as an “ indescribable quadruped,” and
commenting on his feebleness. In that short month,
however, he has built up into, despite his fiddle-head, a
really good stamp of a horse, and has developed a whirl-
wind.finish, his end-up when he downed old John Rid at
Randwick being brilliant. Aberdare won’t want to im-
prove at this rate much longer to get near Grand National
form.

The Boer who catches Uproar, the winner of
the Bushmen’s Contingent double at Randwick on Friday
last, will need to be mounted on a good bit of stuff.
Uproar won the first race, carrying list., in the excellent
time of lmin. 20sec. ; and in the second event, carrying
the steadier of 12st. 101 b., he ran his six furlongs in
lmin. 21sec. A. Battye, the old-time crack horseman
who goes with the contingent, had the mounts on Wan-
garah and Wyalong West, both red-hot favorites, but he
could only run second in each instance to the speedy
Uproar.
• * • *
Uproar is rather well-known to Sydney pony-
race followers, who invested on him on Friday
to a man. Uproar promised big things amongst the
dog - sized horses until the Kensington stewards
decided to give him a spell. The spectacle of a
disqualified pony winning a “ superiah ” A.J.O. double is
interesting. Apropos of aforesaid Bushmen’s events, the
horsemanship of the common privates was noticeably
superior to that of the officers. Uproar was beautifully
handled in the first event by Private Walker, and there
was nothing wrong with Sydney poloist Lieut. “ Billy ”
Cope’s steering of him to the van in the Commandeer
Plate, but the efforts of the spangled ofHcers-in-general
were not brilliant. One of them, on a good sort of nag,
had a go at Uproar opposite the stand, but stopped
flogging to fix his cap on straight.

“Tassy”: A steeplechase run at Chudleigh
(Tas.) lately produced some old-timers. Winner was 14
years old, second (who has been racing for something like
10 years) was 16, while the third horee was aged 14. It is
remarkable that “ leppers” last so long on the turf.
Horses which, in any other walk of life, would have gravi-
tated to the bone-heap years ago still prance gaily over
the hurdles and defy Death to gather them in.

“Ranji ”: Owner of whilom Australian nag
Cherry, winner of latest Viceroy’s Cup, in India, struts
under the appellation and titles of Fargaud-i-Khas-Paulat-
i-Inglisha Mansoor-i-Zaman Amir-ul-IJmara Mnharaja-i-
Rajgan-Rajishwer Sri Maharaja-i-Rajgan—which, short-
ened up, means his Highness the Maharaja of Patiala.
When this potentate travels his initials are brought after
him by freight train. He is 28, keen as the keenest on
sport, and a reported top-rate horseman. He owns an
immense stable of racers, hacks and hunters, and is
a mighty pig-sticker before the Lord. ’Twas Patiala
that recently presented a high-class Arab charger to Lord
Bobs. In addition to his other qualifications, he is
almost as goo * a cricketer as Ranji.

* • * •
“Scrip” : Reason why so many small owners
don’t know where next week’s oats are to come from is
that their gallopers are only mediums, and they’re not
clever enough to “ place ” them properly. Also, some so-
called trainers should be scavenging instead of handling
frisky thoroughbreds. Know a man who served me with
milk nine months ago—been in the cow business all his
life —now set up as a public trainer. He says : “ Send
’em round,” and round they go ; the boy jockey does it
all. Owners are satisfied apparently.
M.L. punters who operate with the irrepressible
“ walking tote ” do so when two horses
belonging to one owner are bracketed on the legalised
machine. Latter pays out on both ; the walking gent on
the winner only.
* • • *
A very dressy individual of affluent appearance
sauntered up to a fashionable jockey on an Australian
course the other day, and asked about his mount’s chance
in next race. Jockey winked his blind eye, and inquired
if he would “ stand in ”if he gave a good tip. Agreed to.
Told inquirer to back, say, Nobbier for all he was worth.
“My moke,” he said, effusively, “is stiff.” The good
thing came off with satisfactory financial results. But
that jock never rode his own mount again ; it happened
to belong to the dressy person, who raced under the rose,
and who also changed his trainer forthwith.
A good field went out for the chief event (15
bags) at a Maori oats-meeting a while back, a noted brown
man’s horse being a hot favorite. It got beaten by
a neck, and the Maori judge said, “ Nummer seven win.”
But the owner of the g’ood thing, a six-footer of ferocious
aspect, yelled—“ No fear ! My horse win ” ; and the
crowd capsized the cart which did duty for a “ box.”
Then the judge admitted he was wrong. But that night,
when the brown men were drinking, the owner of No. 7
stole the oats and sold them to an adjacent fanner, and
there was so much trouble that it has been decided to
strike the 15-bag' event off the programme in future.
