In Sydney “club” cricket on Saturday, Pad-
dington put up a good performance by getting rid of
Central Cumberland for 106, but C.O. put up a better one
by gathering in 6 Paddington wickets for 72. Trumper,
Noble, and Mackenzie, for the latter club, only knocked
up 23 among them. “Hopeless Alick’s” share being-
nil. Mary Ann Noble, however, collected 6 C.O. wickets
for 39, and Trumper snared 4 for 58. It isn’t often that

cracks, North Sydney ; W. Camphin, a sturdy bat, getting

Among the accepted volunteers for the Vic.
Bushman’s Contingent are the James brothers, a year or
two ago the crack oarsmen of the province. As a repre-
sentative of the Melb. Rowing Club, now defunct, Fred
James stroked one interprovincial eight, and both he and
his brother sat in several others. Both are men of
splendid phy 7 sique and have seen something of the bush.
Their cycle ride from Mount Magnet to Melb., a couple of
years ago, had a lot of bush experience in it.

“Bias”: Sydney Club bowling competitions
are in their final stages. Petersham got put out of both
pennant and “ B.” matches last Saturday. To-day
(Saturday 7) City will meet Waverley, and wants one win
to secure the premiership flag again this season. Waver-
ley must come out atop twice to achieve that distinction.
Waverley and Ashfield are the only two left in “ B.”
tourney. Whichever side wins this afternoon gathers in
the laurels, and wears these brow-vegetables for this
season.

Five - hundred yards swimming supremacy of
the Cornstalk province, at Bronte (Sy 7 dney), Saturday
went to boy Dick Cavill, who swam superbly, and
touched the finish with three yards of the fluid you mix
with your whisky between himself and Craig (220yds.
champion of the continent). Craig was eight yards away
from Bond (owner of the J-mile anrl 1-mile premiership of
N.S.W. and Victoria respectively). Time (0. 52 1-5) is
a new best for this section. Freddy 7 Lane’s 6. 59 2-5 pre-
viously held pride of place. For purposes of comparison
may be mentioned that the world’s amateur fastest is
6.45, and professional 6.28). Cavill could have travelled a
bit quicker had it been necessary, but it wasn’t. All
three men placed finished inside standard—7.s.

contests ended Monday 7 night. A big crowd rolled up.
Jackson (Vic.), from scratch, got away with the final of
the Bushmen’s wheel race, with Rowlands (S.A.), 100yds.,

only separated the trio. M.L. champion Sutherland
annexed “The Chenhall ” (named after a Sydney solicitor
who dabbles a bit in the game) mile from scratch, bril-
liantly 7 beating Bob Walne (Q.), also on the same mark,
by 7 20yds. Cobar (N.S.W.) Larry Corbett (75yds.) was a
good third. “Muskett” 10-miles scratch race—the big

Champion cyclist E. Reynolds, picked to repre-
sent M.L. at Paris, walked off with the one, three, and
five-mile cash events at League of Maoriland’s champion-
ship meeting t’other day ; while brother W. Rey nolds
secured the three and ten mile amateur championships.
Reynolds the elder is in great form just now.
The much-boomed pedalling game has decayed
greatly in M.L. The admixture of pro. and lily-white
amateur racing is suggested as the cause. Taking them
all round, too, the pros, are a comparatively poor lot,
and the public won’t pay to see “ arranged ” races. Last
championship meeting was a sick affair, both from a
racing and attendance view point.
* * * *
A by-law passed by Christchurch (M.L.) Coun-
cil has elicited some very breezy observations. C.vclists
must toll bell when meeting or overtaking another cyclist,
or a pedestrian, horse, or vehicle, and when crossing
streets. They mustn’t “ ride without handles ” —a knock
at conceited prigs who bowl along with hands off handle-
bar —and short-cuts round corners are prohibited, a
graceful curve being demanded. Apologies for lights
will be severely sat on ; real good lighthouse affairs are
wanted. The Cathedral City 7 is now a tinkling hell, and
action is to be taken against demonstrative bikists whose
clanging of large dinner-bells has brought them under
the category of public nuisances.
* * # *
“Reo” writes: “C.M.G.” (B. 10/2/’OO) won-
ders why cyclists don't get killed by their fa.ls when
racing. Answer—condition. Same with jockeys. Let
an average man fall from a horse and he spends a term in
bed, but a jockey can have a horse roll over him and
bruise him from head to foot, and, bar a broken bone, he
is right in two or three days.
•*" * »
“Melb.” : At the A.N.A. fete, I stepped up to
the Exhibition dome by night. Perhaps the only 7 cities
with a more central view-point of elevation are Boston,
from the gilded cupola of the State House, up to wliiph
everything slopes, and Milan, where the cathedral is such
another hub, similarly visible from all points of the com-
pass. At night the Exhibition dome, buried in air, was
above me, while all around was the ocean of lights, and
the strains of music welled up from beneath. How sur-
passingly beautiful was the view of the Coliseum, so to
speak, below, electric-lighted, with its thousands of spec-
tators, and a dozen cyclists, in jockey colors, whizzing
round in a cluster. Tyres on the asphalt sound at that
distance rather like the patter of the hoofs of racers on
the turf, though more subdued.

• • * *
The Committee of the Druids’ Gala (Melbourne),
recognising that motor-paced contests are popular with
the public, have inserted a 3 Miles Paced Tournament in
their programme of sports, to be held .at the Melbourne
Exhibition building at Easter time. The best of our
cracks will be invited, and the fact that a big prize will be
given to the winner will produce a struggle between
Messrs. Walne, Martin, Jackson, Beauchamp, Sutherland
and Mutton that should be worth travelling miles to see.
The tournament will be paced by the Dunlop motors,
which gave such an interesting exhibition at the recent
A.N.A. Meeting.

“ The Pelican ” sends a sporting item : Saw some
unique sports t’other day at Golden Lyre Hotel. The
tournament was snakes versus men. First event was a
flat race, straight course, 80 links long. Combo, an
“even time” nigger of the district, was favorite, though a
fine specimen of tiger snake almost shared the honor.

* » • *
Will any Australian ever repeat Geo. Giffen’s
great feat of heading both batting and bowling on an
English tour? The most likely emulator is Noble, as
Crumble is getting old, and his muscles must be stiffen-
ing. Vet even Noble’s chance isn’t promising, as he is an
erratic bowler, and the Britishers can puli his slow stuff.
All this shows that the Giffen gap in Australian cricket
hasn’t yet been filled. It is only when a champion retires
that his value can be adequately estimated.
The next English team will probably be without
some of the men lovers of the game hoped to see. Major
Poore, who headed last season's averages, and C. B. Fry,
who performed so well against Darling and Co., have gone
to South Africa, the latter with the Imperial Yeomen ;
Capt. Wynyard is under orders ; Fox, the Oxford wicket-
keeper, has been bitten with the fever, and enlisted
in the 6th Dragoons ; Lord Harris has also volunteered,
and, it is to be hoped, will be satisfied with Kruger’s
umpiring. Likewise, it is cabled that, owing to the war,
the proposed tour is off for the present.

The Britishers and their institutions die hard..
Mr. Arthur Coventry, the English starter, is still sceptical
about the value of the starting barrier, which is to be
tried with the two-year-olds. Under these circumstances,
Mr. Coventry may not prove so good a hand at the
machine as the inventor would desire, as few men are
anxious to prove themselves unwise prophets.

With the holiday season coming on, and num-
bers of people either going themselves or sending their
families to the mountains, the enterprising burglars’ har-
vest has begun. Provident people, however, are provid-
ing against his visits by storing their valuables—plate,
jewellery, papers, &c\—at the Sydney Safe Deposit, Ash-
street (hack of Paling’s), and business there is proportion-
ately brisk. We would recommend Bullktin readers to
call and inspect the massive vaults, and ascertain terms
for storage. Safes may now be rented upon reduced
terms, and boxes are received for storage at very moder-
ate rales.*

At Poverty Point.
At an evening entertainment once in a small Lanark-
shire (Scotland) town a very mild amateur tenor made a
wild attempt at “My Pretty Jane.” An old farmer was
asked what he thought of it. “No bad,” he replied,
“ but a h’ard ane Sims Reeves sing it, doou in Glesca, an’
he wiz fully as entertainin’.”
When the Italian Opera Co., which the late Martin
Simonsen brought here about the beginning of ’B7, was
voyaging from Sydney to Melbourne, the steamer, on
rounding Wilson’s Promontory, shipped one or two seas.
The tenor Lazzarini was observed standing at the saloon
companion, looking very pale and forlorn. Someone
asked him if he was sick. “ No,” he replied, “I no seek.
My vife seek. I frightened..”
Charles Arnold when last heard from was about to play
“ What Happened to Jones ” in Capetown, and had
announced his intention to take the co. to Kimberley
before leaving for Australia. Probably the next announce-
ment will be that Arnold has decided not to play in
Kimberley owing to the prevalence of the Boer plague
north of the Modder River.
Thus a Poverty Pointer to a Bum.etix man:—“Of
course, actors, not having ‘ stiddy jobs ’as a rule, and
being an improvident crowd that stays out late at night,
frequently falls into arrears at hotels'and places, and give
Mrs. Landlord worry and sleepless nights. But I particu-
larly object to the continual assumption that the landlady
of the actor’s hash-entrenchment is a tough case with a
heart of old red sandstone, who locks up "the backward
artist’s trousers, or holds his person pending settlements.
As a matter of fact, I have found them all the other wav.
Here is a case in point. I was staying at a house in Carl-
ton last year, and was soon a month behind, and promis-
ing to increase the delay indefinitely. Did my landlady
lock me up, or take steps to hold my belongings ? Not a
bit of it. Four nights running I noticed a ladder reared
against the wall under my window. On the fifth night I
took the hint. Now, the juxtaposition of that ladder and
my window was no accident; it was a polite intimation,
and if I ever have a fortune left me by ,1 11 pay that
woman, if only as a reward for her exquisite delicacy.”
Barry Sullivan, the tragedian, held himself in high
esteem. A super, in the old Theatre Roval, Melbourne,
having offended him, was dismissed. The wretched man
fell on his knees before the theatrical magnate and begged
for mercy. “Kneel not to me,” said the lordly actor,
“ but to one ” (pointing to the heavens) “ who, perhaps,
is mightier even than I.”
When Louise Pomeroy, the American actress, was per-
forming at Melbourne Royal, she was greatlv smitten
with the charms of curly-pated Herbert Flemming.
One night she fainted between the acts. Some friends
gathered round her and anxiously debated as to whether
they should send for a doctor. The fair tragedienne
instantly revived and said with considerable asperity :
“ Certainly not; send for the man with the god-like
head ! ” And they all knew the individual she required.
Herr Bandmann—-the Gennan actor who lately pursued
the plain red cow over the fragrant hills of Cali ornia—
was fond of carrying a sharp-pointed sword instead of the
ordinary stage implement. In the last act of “ Othello ”
he once attacked Emilia (Mrs. Steele) with such vigor that
the weapon entered the lower part of her back, eliciting
screams which made some of the audience rise, and fly
from the premises. She recovered from her injuries in a
few days, Imt it was a narrow escape and Bandmann was
a badly-terrified German for a minute or two.
About 17 years ago, when the advt. of the person “with
a view to the above ” was less common than it is in these
times, a now well-known actor was stranded in a cold and
holy Maori land city, and desperately hard-up. The only
visible way to raise the wind was by getting up a big
benefit to himself, and the cold and fioly place didn't, i s
a rule, spend much money on benefits. But he raked tl e
people in by this wicked device : An agony advt.—almost
the first of the kind in these parts—was inserted in tl e
local cause of public gloom—** Young lady, handsome,
plenty of means, desires to meet young man.' Matrimony
no dial object." The last clause might mean anything or
nothing, but the local pietists assumed that" it meant
everything, and rose in fury, and it blew a gale of shocked
he-cats down Princes-street. There were indignant letters
in the local press, and the curse that was delivered from
the local pulpit isn’t quite cool yet. But the answers
rolled in, among them letters from local magnates and
preachers. They all “desired interview.” The wily one
replied to them all, making an appointment in the circle
of the theatre on his own benefit-night, till he saw the
circle would hold no more. Then he made appointments
with the rest in the stalls. And he had a great benefit—
one that the holy city remembers to this day.
Some people won’t be content with less than the Uni-
verse. A couple of years back Barnum and Bailee's
** Greatest Show on Earth ” was formed into a companv,
with a nominal capital of £4l)o,orxi, and for some time
found a happv hunting ground in the provinces of the
United Kingdom. In 1S!)S the profits were £113,343-
more than 25 per cent ; but in 1899 a long continuance of
bail weather brought them down to £37,428. This
paltry dividend of about 9 per cent, was not good enough
for the directors and shareholders, so they are going to
try Germany this year. They won’t have it all their own
way there though, for Germany has always been strong in
circuses. Writer remembers the two big Berlin circuses—
Renz’s and Salamonski’s—with their double, and even
treble, companies, so that one could go on successive
evenings and see each time a different performance. It
was nothing out of the way to see 100 horses standing idle
in their stables.
Not generally known that local scenic artist, Brunton,
is an expert swordsman. When he was assistant to Wm.
Glover, at the Theatre Royal, Glasgow, they used fre-
quently to appear together in the duel scene of the Cor-
sican Brothers—Brunton’s Chateau Renaud to Glover’s
Fabien—and their fencing was a thing to remember.
Two notable deaths in the English theatrical world at
Xmas time were the passing hence of pretty Dorothy
Dene and Mat Robson. The latter, a good low comedian,
with a one-time comic “catch” in his throat (a trick
never quite reproduced by his numerous imitators), was
the son of the original Robson, the man whom all the old
identities swore by when Johnny Toole was still a young
favorite. Toole, at his best, was said to be but a poor
successor to Robson in characters of the Paul Pry and
serio-comic class.
The war has been a godsend to some small theatrical
people who had the good luck to be in the Transvaal
when hostilities commenced. One or two persons more
or less connected with the stage sought the bubble advt.
by being “ murdered by the Boers ” while endeavoring to
get out of the country m order to fulfil engagements else-
where at stupendous salaries, and declared themselves
afterwards quite unable to imagine how the reports got
about. _ One of the “ horrid Boer murders ” which caused
spine wild Australian Jingoes to demand the public execu-
tion of the Boer leaders when captured by way of satis-
faction was only the harmless, unavoidable puff of an
American funny man named Charles Edwards. Edwards,
who is now pkyinjf in London, got himself stain in an
extra-stupendous manner, and his mangled remains
appeared in English cablegrams.
Cora Urquhart Potter’s doctor haB made her give rip
reciting at the London Empire. The tobacco-smoke, she
says, irritates the eyes, nose, and throat of an artist. It
comes likes a fogbank over the float. It saturated her
hair and clothes, so that she could not get the stench
out.

“Melb.”: The tedious tussle of the dramatic elocu-
tionary and musical contests of the A.N.A. has evoked
some genuine talent. Heading the reciters, Eileen
O’Mara, a budding vision of loveliness, with a hayrick of
magnificent tumbled hair a little too much over her eyes,
showed that she also possessed a mind directly she ojieiied
the gates of her mouth and persuaded her sweet voice to
walk out of them—look at that now I Also, Miss Burrows
flung- herself into Juliet in a moment. But the daring and
dashing soliloquy was that of the girl Smith, who out-
Geciled Ward in declaiming a bit of D’Artagnan. The sing-
ing tournament evoked Lily' Henr.v, who swept the board.
A smallish, ungrown, straw-haired, Johnny Coleman-look-
ing girl, her deep contralto was like a hammer on a bell.
Another Crossley in embryo, though her organ, like
Regina Nagel’s, has a painful Australian quality of mono-
chrome, the essence of Marcus Clarke’s Melancholy of the
Bush. I don’t like it, but it hits hard. There" is in it
somewhat of the bullock-bell. On the piano, a sort of
really gifted Crummies’s infant phenomenon, named
Hilda Baker, tackled the “Caprice Aerien” with all the
assurance and intelligence of an Arabella Goddard. Miss
Cussen’s “ Saltarelle ” was an example to show that the
girls don’t all carry about only mackintoshes and goloshes
and cray'fish in violin-cases.

“ F.” : Tom Fitz Gerald’s great unhappiness will be that
he can’t send “hob” wires all over Australia from ’Ome.
Flash Tom n. ver writes a letter : he is too lazy, and then
a wire looks 1.-eehanded. He will spend 15s. on a wire to
convey some information that can keep for a month, and
that one page of letter-paper would carry for twopence,
and then economise the rest of the day. Telegrams are
his one wild extravagance, riot, bust, and all the rest.
He is a telegram-drunk. Dan, on the contrary, neither
writes nor wires—he just comes. And Daniel would give
the boots off his feet to anyone he was told he once knew,
and do it secretly, as if it was a crime.
The breezy letters of “Banjo ” Paterson,
in S.M. Herald, stand easily first in interest
among Australian press correspondence from the
seat of war. And their (to put it mildly) non-
Jingo tone is so noticeable that the public is some-
what astonished to find them printed in such a
quarter. When at Capetown “Banjo” visited
the world-famous Olive Schreiner, sister of the
Cape Premier and authoress of “ The Story of
an African Farm,” whom he found to be dark,
short, strongly-built, brisk, and bright-eyed. Mr.
Paterson proceeds :
It may be news to many Australians to hear that in the
Cape Colony there are more people against the war than
for it, not necessarily Boer sympathisers, but people who
think that the war should never have been entered upon.
When our troops landed from Australia we were as-
tonished to find that the Cape Colony was so much
against the war. The local papers that were for the war
were all clamoring for the arrest or dismissal of Schreiner,
the Premier, but when the House went to a division
Schreiner had a majority behind him, and Schreiner has
always declared that England is not justified in this war.
Olive Schreiner was not long in stating her views to the
present writer. She talks rapidly and energetically, em-
phasising her remark's with ' uplifted finger. “ You
Australians and New Zealanders and Canadians,” she
said, “ I cannot understand it at all, why you come
here light - heartedly to shoot- down other colonists
of whom you know nothing —it is terrible. Such
fine men too —fine fellows. I went to Green Point and
saw your men in camp ; oh, they were fine men—(These
were Colonel Williams’s A.M.O. troops)—and to think
that they are going out to kill and be killed, just to please
the capitalists 1 There was one officer—oh, a fine man, so
like a Boer, he might have been a Boer commandant.
[This was no doubt the burly, blue-eyed Surgeon-Colonel
Williams.—Ed. B.J It is terrible—such men to come and
fight against those fighting for their liberty and then-
country. The English Tommy Atkins goes where he is
sent—he fights because he is ordered ; but you people—
you are all volunteers ! Why have you come ? . . . You
say that England was at war, and you wish to show the
world that when the mother country got into a war the
colonies were prepared to take their place beside her?
Yes, but you ought to ask, you ought to make inquiries
before you come over. You Australians do not under-
stand. This is a capitalists’ war ! They want fio get con-
trol of the Rand and the mines. You have nothing
like it in your country. You have a working-class
that votes, and that cannot be bought to vote
against its own interests ; but, in the Transvaal,
there are just a handful of Boer fanners, a small, but
enormously wealthy, mine-owning class and their depen-
dents—professional men, shopkeepers, and so on, and the
rest are all kattirs.” “Why didn’t the Boers grant the
franchise?” “It was not really wanted. I was in
Johannesburg a few months before the war broke out,
and hundreds of men there said they would not forego
their British nationality for the sake of voting as a Boer.
They are all nomads, wanderers, over there to make
money, and if Oom Paul had gone on his knees and asked
them to accept the franchise they would not have
accepted it. They would not relinquish being British
subjects. But the capitalists insist on getting hold of the
mines, and all the white people are so concerned with
them, their interests so depend on the mine-owners, that
they must go along with them, and now they want the
franchise to take control of the mines from the Boers. It
is a monstrous war, and England will regret it ;
is it just to take the country from the Boers for the
benefit of the speculators? For years this war has been
worked up—all sorts of stories have been printed of the
Boers and their ignorance and their savagery. They are
all lies. I was a governess among the Boers for years, and
no kinder people exist. They are clever, too. Young
Boers go to England and succeed at the Universities ;
they become doctors and lawyers and politicians. It is
much like Australia from what I have read. They are
such hospitable people. Oh, this war is a terrible thing ;
it will be a war of extermination.” “ What do you think
will be the end of it?” “ There will be no end. The Boers
are fighting for life or death, and they have no idea of giv-
ing in. If they are beaten back into the Free State and
the Transvaal that is just the time they will be most dan-
gerous. When the English get to Pretoria with their
army they will then be in a worse position than they are
now. They will have hundreds and hundreds of miles of
railway line to defend, and even if the Boers are scattered
and beaten, they will still fight. The Boer women
now are heart and soul for the war. The Boer
farmer is a curious man. He marries earlv,
usually about IS or 19, and there is not one
man in the army against you but has his wife and child
somewhere. And those wives and children are reaping
the crops and working the farms, so that their husbands
can go to the war. After Elandslaagte, where the Boers
were defeated, one Boer went home. His wife said,
‘ What has happened ; are you wounded?’ ‘No.’ ‘ls
the enemy fled?’ ‘No.’ ‘Well, back you go to the
laager, and fight with the rest.’ Another old man, of 75,
when he heard of the defeat, rose up and took his rifle.
‘I am going to the front,’ he said. ‘ Why, you cannot
see,’ said his grandchildren. ‘ I cannot see at iOOO yards,’
he said, ‘ but I can see well enough at 100 ;’ and off he went
in a Cape cart as he was too old to ride. The small boys
at school at the Capetown schools have all been brought
back to the farms to fight—boys of 14 and 15 are in
the ranks. And all of these people have their
relatives all through Cape Colony; and they are to
be butchered, and the English soldiers are to be
butchered, to suit a few capitalists. It will benefit no
one else. The effect of this war on South Africa will be
everlasting; we have such a large population here who
feel that the war is unjust, and they will never forgive
the English people for forcing it on. ‘ Perhaps the memory
of Majuba Hill had a good deal to do with it? I cannot
think so—the English are not so narrow as to treasure up
memories of a small thing like that. But now it is a long,
terrible war that is before us, and the Boers will be more
dangerous after a few reverses. A few defeats will not
crush them ! When the English get to Pretoria then
there will begin the trouble.” Whatever may be the cor-
rectness of Olive Schreiner’s views' there is no doubt of
her sincerity She says openly what most, or at any
rate very many, South Africans think, arid it is
always well to hear both sides, so I have put down
without comment exactly what her views were.
After an interview with her one comes away with a much
more lively and human interest in “ orir friends the
enemy.” If tilings are as she says, if the Boers are going
to make it a war to the bitter end, then England has a
sorry task before her. If the Boers scatter and break to
the mountains they will be practically' unreachable, and
the English people are too humane to care about levying
reprisals by destroying their homesteads and leaving their
wives and children without shelter. The result will be
that even after the war is over a large force will have to
be kept in the country to maintain order, and with the
Cape disaffected there will be serious trouble for poli-
ticians after the soldiers have got through with their
work. One is more inclined to hope that after a defeat
or two the Boer, sensible man that he is, will come in
under English rule, and rely on the forbearance of that
Power rather than maintain a hopeless struggling fight
which will only prolong the misery of the present war.
THE MODERN COLOSSEUM.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
This world’s a theatre. Just now
The stage is set beyond the water,
Whereon the actors make their bow.
The play’s entitled “ Awful Slaughter.”
To make your nerves all crawl and creep,
The play each day with horror hits you;
And then the prices are so cheap—
A penny every day admits you.
Two hundred thousand actors fight
On plains and kopjes, camps and trenches ;
The slaughter goes on day and night,
And blood the wide arena drenches.
It’s very sad and very bad,
But as war never will be missed hence,
You take it, as ’tis to be had,
To give a spice to your existence.
You wonder what the troops will do,
And what will be the General’s caper }
It gives a pleasant interest to
The cables in your daily paper.
You have a friend who rushed pell-mell
To volunteer, and off has trotted ;
And, though, of course, you like him well,
You speculate il he’ll lie potted.
With slaughter here and slaughter there,
And more to come, you fill your head full:
You like to see your paper bear
The message of the penny dreadful.
If Boers slaughtered all they took,
Twould horrify the British nation ;
But think how sweet ’twould be to book
The tingle of each huge sensation.
And when to Fate the Dutchmen bow
We’ll offer up a loud Te Deum,
And pray God for another row
To fill our modern Colosseum.

warrior with a game leg, was sent on in advance

the combatants paused for breath, whereupon
the lydies attempted to effect by blandish-
ment what valor had failed to accom-
plish. But out they had to go, and, having
nowhere in particular to make for, they proceeded
. hold an auction of their ejected chattels.
Bidding was brisk, and, as soon as a few articles
had been sold, one of the lydies was despatched
for liquid refreshments. At intervals, the per-
spiring garrison would heave out another bed or
washstand, to be also put promptly under the
hammer. Every moment the fun waxed more
furious, and the crowd increased until the entire
street was blocked. At last the bobby, who had
retired to report, returned with another constable
and a cab, and the whole bevy of now wildly
hilarious Totties were run-in for being disorderly
and for not having an auctioneer’s license.

Statutory |g§|gg Declaration.
I, TIIOS. MUDIMAN, Master Tailor, of 327 Elizabeth-
street, Sydney, in the Colony of New South Wales, do
hereby solemnly and sincerely declare as follows:
Whereas 1 suffered severely from a very troublesome
affection of the NERVES, which affected me so much that
1 was quite unable to attend to my business. I tried
several doctors and many remedies, but all of no avail.
Whereas I, as a last hope, obtained some of Herr Rass-
mussen’s Purely Herbal ALFALINE VITALITY PILLS,
which I hereby solemnly and sincerely declare COM-
PLETELY CURED ME within a few weeks, and although
this is over five years ago I have remained well ever since.
The ALFALINE PILLS also cured my wife, and I make
this solemn declaration conscientiously believing- the same
to be true.
Declared at Sydney this 29th day of April, 1898.
THOMAS MUDIMAN.

A Cold Prospect.
A very important point for the consideration of
Australian Tory politicians will arise whene'er
the Commonwealth Parliament is_ formed. Ihe
Tory politicians of the various Upper Houses
the Levis and Embungs and their kind—all resist
the reform of their local Upper Houses upon a
democratic basis on the ground that the ruck, the
common, objectionable ruck, shouldn’t be allowed
either to sit on these august institutions, or vote
at their elections, or have anything to do with
them whatever. Their theory is that an U pper
House is a high and noble institution, breathing a
pure and elevated political atmosphere : and the
mass of the people are coarse and ignorant, and
wear thick boots and ill-fitting clothes and are
foolish-and muddy and accursed. Yet when these
lofty souls want to get into the I ederal benate or
House of Representatives, as very many of them do,
they can only manage it by appealing to the class
j which they have always scorned and declared to be
unfit to be trusted with full political power. Loth
Federal Houses will be elected on the basis of One
Man One Vote, with no property qualification for
members. So Levi and Lbkne/.eu W mn. and
their species, if they want to rise to the dignity of
being Federal Senators, have simply to tell the
people this: “We have always said you were too
Ignorant and gross and hobnailed to be fit to elect
an Upper House. Therefore please give your
votes to the men who have habitually aimed mud
and derision at you. We still say that you are
unfit to sit in, or have a voice in choosing, the
State Legislative Councils, and we will do our
best to keep you there. Kindly express jour
sense of the truth of this insulting estimate of j ou
by supporting us.” And the people will presum-
ablv answer with a loud voice and a loud bad egg
“ N<>.” The existing lory M s.L.G. who want to
qualify for billets in the purely democratic * ederal
Legislature will have to do something, and do it
in a considerable hurry, to prove that they are the
sort of people any sane Democracy would want to
elect as its representatives. If they consent to a
reform of the existing Leg. Councils on the lines
of the Federal Upper House the people maj
thereby gather that they are men who can be
trusted to look after a democratic 1 ederal Consti-
tution, but otherwise they will almost certainly
have to remain behind in obscurity. And . tate
Legislatures will be very obscure places, where
the rain of titles will fall as thinly as the ram in
an old-man drought, and their members will be
very moderately “lion, within the province, and
have a hard back seat at public functions, and the
lofty Federal member will only see them with
difficulty when he meets them, and will have to
think awhile before lie remembers whether thej
are M’s.L.C. or only town-clerks.
Out of 20 dairy-hands in a place only J.i
miles from Melbourne, only four knew the name
of the Premier, anti every one could tell the winner
of the last pony-race. Three knew the difference
between the Transvaal and Cape Colony. Only
one knew the real meaning of “ Door hut H "ere
dead sure it should be put down whatever it was.

commemoration of the departure of the Bushmen s
Contingent. The obverse bears the head of the
Queen and the inscription “ ISWI British Fraiis-

as he spots the Boers, also the inscription Good
luck to Australian Bushmen s Corps, l.)00. Ihe

The famous name of Mick Simmons is
now branded on several of tbe handsomest shops
in Sydney; and the name has Ijecome svnonynious
with up-to-dateness and attraction. -The display
of pipes and tobacco is something for a sightseer
to dwell upon with pleased and astonished eyes;
while the variety of flavors and excellence of
brands is bound to please smokers and 11011-smokers
the world over. Mick Simmons is coming well
to the fore, too, with jewellery, table cutlery,
walking sticks, purses, wallets, card cases, dressing
cases, and a hundred other fancy goods of hand-
some style and finish ; and costing so small a
price that no one minds parting up for the bargain.
As a supplier of sporting goods Mick Simmons
stands first for quality, variety and completeness
The tennis goods are a grand lot; the cricket
goods are as good as W G. Grace ever saw ;
while the boxing gloves, dumb bells and Indian
clubs commend themselves to those gentlemen
who are anxious to make their niaik in life.
Some time ago Professor Schenk burst into
the ring of the great ones of the earth with a
theorv for the arbitrary determination of human
sex. He published lus book and boomed loudly,
and' was employed by kings, councillors, jockeys,
and the rest of the salt of the earth. When the
boom was at the loudest, the Czar of all the
Russias employed him to ensure an heir to the
uneasy throne of the Romanoffs. It was said that
the Czarina did not wish to have a son ; the idea
of bringing a son into the world to be tormented
by Nihilists'terrified ber. She is alleged to have
deliberately reversed Schenk’s treatment ; any-
h >w, the heir turned up a daughter. 1 lien Schenk
slumped badly. Now lie has gone bung altogether
He has been chief of the Embryological Institute
of Vienna University for 28 years and when Ins
boom subsided to a mere cracked noise, the
faculty reached for bis hair. I bey investigated
his alleged discovery, and then subjected the boy-
maker to a “disciplinary examination, and re-
commended that he be sacked and pensioned for
advancing a frivolous idea as a scientific theory.
The Minister of Instruction could not find it in
his heart to deal roughly with one who had pre-
scribed for the Russian Empress, and lie begged
Schenk to complain of old age and apply for a
pension. Schenk accordingly grew old su.ldoiilj,
aud applied to be pensioned off. Winch he
promptly was.

Bill Copley (“Turncoat Bill”), of S.A. j
Parliament, has got the loyal disease so badly that
he and Labor-member Guthrie lately swarmed
into the Government House premises and hauled
down the S.A. flag. Bill claims that only the
British ensign should hang there, and that the
presence of the S.A. flag is a sign of disloyalty
and of a desire to disrupt the Empire. The
beastly Australian emblem has been waving from
the banner at Government House for about two
years without any serious complaint, but the war
filled Bill Copley with a desire to assist the
Empire in some way that would get in the papers
and cost nothing. And it struck him that insult-
ing the flag of the province which pays his salary
was as good an idea as any.

The Queen publishes an interesting article
alleging that the sudden appearance of an army of
foreign youths has just about sol veil the servant-
gal problem in the Motherland. They are general 1 y
spoken of as “German waiters” but are said
to really consist of Germans, Poles, Swiss,
Italians, and Hungarians. They work for low
wages, almost no wages, and many of them are
said to be in receipt of constant drafts from their
own country. Their ostensible reason is that they
wish to learn the English language. _ Why they
should resort to humping coal upstairs, blacking
Ik tots, and beating carpets for less wages than the
ordinary servant girl in order to learn the language
strikes even the old cattism of the Queen as
peculiar. The admiring pen-woman remarks : “ l
have seen foreign domestics who looked like gentle-
men in disguise,” In 18114 the French found the
cheap domestic and low-priced clerk problem
suddenly settled the same way. When the
German armies rolled through France in 70 they
knew exactly the resources of every village
and city they came to, and were always in possession
of the most perfect local knowledge, and were
never led into any traps. They seemed to know
the country better than the French themselves.
Bv-and bye many French merchants and gentry
recognised their old domestics and scribblers in
positions of authority in the invading ranks, and it
struck the French national intellect that thecheap,
educated, foreign clerks and low-priced male
housemaids were military spies who had carefully
mapped out the country and its resources to give
polish to Moltke’s strategy.

For more Than One Hundred fears
SOAP
has maintained its supremacy in the face of the whole
world’s competition. Such a record could' not be
achieved without cause. Temporary successes are com-
paratively easy, but for an article to maintain its popu-
larity generation after generation it must appeal to
something more than passing fancy. This is the case
with Pears’ Soap. It is, and always has been, an honest
product. Everywhere abroad it has found a place in
public favour equal to that for so long held in England.
Men and women alike find it good and reliable. The
man who has once tried Pears’ Soap in the form of a
Shaving Stick wants no other, he takes it wdth him on all
his journeys. The woman who travels and fails to take
a supply of Pears’ must put up with substitutes until
her burning, smarting skin inexorably demands the
“ matchless for the complexion.”
Too Lonesome.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The Star, referring to the case of a widow who received
a sum of money from a relief fund on account of the death
of her husband, says that she married again, was deserted,
and was thus “ left penniless and doubly alone.” Un-
happy female.
Her case was the strangest
In history known,
The poor widdy woman
Left “doubly alone.”
When light-hearted people
Were laughing at ease,
A-moaning she sat, with
Her hands on her knees.
Her money was squandered
And times they were bad
AlaC for the widdy—
Two hungers she had.
Two thirsts she had also,
A brewer would buy ;
But, ah ! for the widdy,
Her tongues remained dry.
The cold wind it filled her
Two empty insides;
She wrung her four hands, and
She rocked her four sides.
“Woe’s me !” she cried sadly,
With two-barrelled moan,
“ To be a poor widdy
Left doubly alone ! ”
For whether she stayed in
Her liu, or went out,
Two grim Lonesomenessea
Were always about.
One Lonesomeness dark on
Tne other one sat —
Like a large-size black hat on
A smaller black hat.
She died, and, I fancy,
Her last words they were
That two Lonesomenesses
Were too much for her.
C.R.
A remarkable case of will-paralysis oc-
curred the other day at Gympie (Q.). A young
miner, fooling with gelignite, held a plug with
inch fuse in left hand and lit it. Changed it then
to his right, and found himself unable to drop it,
though perfectly aware what would happen if he
did not do so, and warned by the shouts of his
companions to let go and come away. Held on
like a hero till the explosion came and took off
four fingers and most of his palm. Can’t explain
anything except that he was nearly breaking his
immortal soul trying to drop it and it wouldn't
dru .
The Funerals of Malachi
Mooney.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
A number of Bungaree farmers, called from the
fields, stood bare-headed about the sick-bed in
attitudes of grievous constraint. Mrs. Mooney,
seated on a low stool, wept sluice-heads, with
wailing and querulous protestations. She had
been replenishing the fountain of tears with
whisky, and now cherished a great grievance
against Malachi for dying, and the time chosen,
and the manner thereof.
“There’s hwhisky by the jar, min,” said the
dying man in a thin wheeze. “ Be dhrinkin’.”
Hogan gravely assumed authority over the
jar, and filled up for the company with judicial
impartiality.
“Good luck to ye, Mullocky,” said Hogan,
raising his cup.
Malachi waved his thin hand in expostulation.
He was beyond all chances of fortune in this
world, and knew it. Hogan temporised.
“Good luck to ye, Mullocky, pwhere ye’re
goin’.”
“ How dar ye doi, Mooney—how dar ye do id ? ”
wailed Mrs. Mooney, throwing her apron over
her head, and rocking her body to and fro.
The company drank with one action, quite
military in its precision, and then looked towards
Malachi Mooney for further orders, and Malachi
lay peacefully, happily dead with a smile on his
lips, and the half-drained mug in his wasted
fingers.
“Oh, ye divil! t’ be dyin’ on me like dthis,”
moaned Mrs. Mooney under her apron. “I’m
disaved in yeh, Mooney! disaved! disaved!
Whurra whroo ! ”
Presently, perceiving that Malachi was beyond
argument, she lifted up her voice and filled the
house with dolorous cries, and wailed dutifully
and monotonously far into the night, when the
chant was taken up by eerie, wrinkled old crones,
smoke-dried grandmothers lent for the occasion by
sympathetic families from the four quarters of the
wilderness.
What a wake that was! It lasted all night,
and right up to the time fixed for the funeral.
There was no end to the willing drinkers, and no
limit to the whisky. Indeed, the miraculous
manner in which tiny kegs, loaded to the bung,
rolled from under the bed on demand, confirmed
the local opinion that “ Mullocky ” Mooney had
more than a finger in the snug still, the smoke
from which curled so artfully up from a charred
trunk on Peter’s Hill, and was thoughtfully given
a supernatural origin by the neighborly people of
the district.
The funeral was advertised to move from the
home of deceased at 10 a.m. sharp. It was a long
march to the Ballarat old cemetery, and an early
start was deemed necessary in consideration of the
fact that Hooley’s funeral, which happened a
month earlier, had been fined for furious driving,
by reason of the anxiety of the mourners to reach
the graveyard before closing time.
The vehicles began to arrive at seven in the
morning, the farmers and settlers driving, and
their wives and “childer” loaded in behind. A
funeral was a “trate” that didn’t happen every
day, and it would have been considered a sin to
deprive the “byes” and “gurrls” of a bit of
“ enjymint ” that cost nothing. But many of the
mourners had been at Mooney’s all night, “ kapin’
the carpse company,” and daybreak disclosed a
baker’s dozen scattered about the farm, sleeping
where they fell. One hung over the dog-leg fence
“ fprninst ” the house, like an old shirt, with down-
swinging arms. Canty, recumbent against the
butt-end of a gum, rigid as a stump, slept so pro-
foundly that the old guttural Brahma-pootra had
perched on his bald and awful head, and was
defying creation with senseless repetitions of his
cracked clarion. Others reposed curled against
the house, and several dotted the paddock like
quaint hieroglyphics, objects of wonderment and
noisy speculation to the familiar pigs.
Michael Morrissey was the first to drive up.
Michael was to occupy an honourable and respon-
sible position at the head of the procession. He
had generously offered the use of his trap as
hearse, and it was appropriately draped for that
solemn office. This vehicle was an American
waggon, and it had been roofed over about two
feet from the floor, and was ordinarily used for
the conveyance of meat, Michael being a butcher.
There was a door at the back, and just room
within for Mooney’s coffin. Quinn’s trotter,
The Imp, was in the shafts. The Imp had
been borrowed for the occasion because he was
the only black horse in the district; but although
his complexion was satisfactory, his disposition
quite unsuited him for so grave a duty. He was old,
and had a semi-bald tail; but there was a peculiar
and aggressive jauntiness about the beast altogether
out of harmony with his years and the situation in
which he found himself. He held his head high,
and pricked his ears, and his tail had a perky ele-
vation that exhibited the bald butt to the worst
advantage, and excited popular derision wherever
he went.
When the friends of the late Malachi Mooney
arrived, they walked reverently into the room
where Malachi still lay on the bed amongst his
monumental candles, and gazed on him for a
moment with pensive sadness, as in duty bound.
“Poremahn, he have the peaceful shmile on
him.”
“ He have, he have.”
And after repeating the sentiment several times,
with nodding heads and much wise clicking of
tongues, having paid their respects to the dead,
they withdrew to the kitchen, and devoted them-
selves to the whisky.
The coffin had been delivered, and stood on two
bush stools in the kitchen, decently covered with
a black shawl. Mrs.. Mooney sat at the foot,
adjacent to a pannikin, and continued to upbraid
Mooney for his inconsiderate conduct in dying,
and “ lavin’ a lone lorn widdy.”
The funeral moved at 11, when it was quite cer-
tain that only one baby keg remained. This keg
Morrissey took with him on the improvised
hearse, as a wise provision for the first half of the
journey, which lay through a barren land.
Many of the mourners had to be helped into
their vehicles, and after the start many remained
in only by a miracle. Morrissey led the way, The
Imp stepping along with a frivolous kind of a
four-footed jig that robbed the cortege at the out-
set of any pretence to dignity. O’Connor’s old
waggonette followed, O’Connor driving carefully,
strapped down, and Mrs. O’Connor and the
“ widdy ” occupying the back seat. Then came
Clark in his spring-cart, driving The Imp’s rival,
Colleen. After him two or three miscellaneous
vehicles, and then a long string of wood drays,
each in charge of an unnaturally rigid and solemn
Irishman perched on a candle box, and each con-
taining one or two women and three or four children,
the former squatting composedly on the bottom of
the dray with their substantial feet swinging out
behind. A dozen sleepy, unshaven, unshorn agri-
culturists brought up the rear, riding two abreast
on large morbid horses that shuffled moodily
through the dust with drooped heads and sagging
under-lips.
The women in the drays maintained a shrill
conversation along the line, but for the most part
the men observed an owl-like decorum until the
Travellers’ Rest was reached, that is if the
puffing of abbreviated black clays be not con-
sidered derogatory to true reverence. Meanwhile,
the day being hot and the way dusty, a couple ol
short halts had served to drain the keg on the
hearse. It was a gritty, drought-stricken funeral
that descended upon the Travellers’ Rest, and
when it moved again it left the wayside inn as dry
as a powder-mill, having drunk up everything in
the bar, and demolished the water-butt.
And now a great spirit of unrest took possession
of many of the mourners, and there was much
whooping and many manifestations of a wild and
unholy desire to convert the procession into some-
thing like a steeplechase. The Imp was stepping
out gaily with his deceitful double-shuffle, game
as a pebble despite his age and infirmities, but it
was Clark with Colleen who led the breakaway.
Springing up with a whoop and whooroo, Clark
whipped his mare alongside the hearse.
“Morrissey,” he cried, “I can bate that
bumble-footed ould crock to the Pint beyant fer
tin bob! ”
“Ye can’t!” roared Morrissey, all the sports-
man stirring within him.
“Ye loi!” Clark fairly shrieked, laying the
whip on.
Michael lashed The Imp, and the veteran,
scenting a contest, snorted defiance, and hit out
with all four afflicted legs at once. Then, bound-
ing over ruts, jumping the boulders, rocking and
rearing, the two vehicles went thundering through
the dust, Colleen leading and The Imp following,
flinging wide his legs with the action of a startled
tarantula as he rushed down the hill, his body
working with the antic spasms of two pigs in a
bag.
The other drivers flogged their stolid horses
into unwonted activity, and in this way the mad
funeral, strung out a mile long, tore through one
affrighted township, scattering sows and suck-
lings, goats, dogs, poultry, and shrieking children,
raising a dust that blotted out half the landscape,
and filling men and women with a wonderment
that lasted many days. Half a mile beyond, The
Imp, with a triumphant tail and starting eye-
balls, flung past the Colleen with a rush and a
roar, neatly carrying away Clark’s near wheel,
which went humming ahead down the well-worn
track.
Morrissey obtained control over his blood horse
and succeeded in pulling up about a mile further
on, and there he waited for the rest of the funeral
in a humble and contrite frame of mind. The
procession arrived in sections, the heavy horses
spent and reeking, and the mourners coated
thickly with powdered clay that caked rapidly in
the sun on their perspiring faces. The women,
particularly the stout ones, tumbled and bumped

Ever have any irritation of the skin ? There are
many forms of it, any of them bad enough to tax
your patience. Hemorrhoid, a plague of the
night; no rest for the sufferer from this complaint.
Eczema, too; hives don’t sound dangerous, but
they cause much misery to those unfortunate
enough to be troubled with them. Doan’s Oint-
ment is “a wonder” for any such trouble. No
troublesome irritation of the skin can resist its
healing, soothing influence. We know it, and
lots of Sydney people know it now.
A nurse, residing at 428 Jones-street, Ultimo,
states : “I have been a sufferer from itchiness of
the skin for some time. I did not know the cause
of it, but it irritated me very much indeed. I
have cured many skin complaints for other people,
but I could not get anything that was good for my
■own ailment until I used Doan’s Ointment, which
J procured one day when visiting Newman’s Phar-
macy in King-street. I think I must have used
'most everything for such troubles, but Doan’s
-Ointment is best of all. It cured the itchiness
which I suffered from entirely and almost imme-
diately.”
The lady gave us these facts twelve months ago,
: and now she speaks further upon the subject: “A
year ago I gave you a testimonial regarding the
medicinal properties of Doan’s Ointment. I have
used this Ointment for various purposes since, and
noticed its splendid curative powers, and must
acknowledge that it is the finest remedy for skin
diseases I have ever come across. It seems to cure
permanently in every case, and its quick effects
are simply marvellous.”
Doan’s Ointment is splendid in all Diseases of
the Skin, Eczema, Piles, Hives, Insect Bites,
Sores, Chilblains, &c. It is perfectly safe and
very effective.
Doan’s Ointment has been tested in so many
ways, in hospital work, in private practice, and
has proved so successful that a special arrange-
ment has been made by which all readers of The
Bulletin who have not already tried it may
obtain a sample pot free by mail by sending us
their names and addresses and a penny stamp to
pay postage. We know that this ointment will
cure these affections, that it will cure them abso-
lutely, and we want you to know it. It is per-
fectly unnecessary for anyone to suffer from Piles
or Eczema for one moment. Doan’s Ointment will
cure them absolutely, but do not take our word for

large size box is sold at 2s. 9d. per box (six boxes
15s. 3d.). If you cannot obtain it locally from

M‘Clellan Co., 76 Pitt-street, Sydney. _

out of all knowledge and restraint, were loud and
fierce in their complainings, and the men agreed
that it was “ ondacent ” and “ agin religion ” to
conduct a funeral at a hard gallop. So Michael
led away again, holding his trotter hard, and pro-
ceeded as reverently and demurely as was possible
with such a horse and so much whisky.
Matty Clark was reported unharmed, and busy
fixing a skid in place of the lost wheel. It was
expected that he would turn back, and be no more
a disturbing element in his neighbor’s funeral.
The procession travelled into the outskirts of
Ballarat without any further misadventure. In
fact, most of the drivers and several of the ladies
were asleep, and the weary plough-horses drowsed
along at their own gait. The Imp, in spite of the
apparent sprightliness of his action, was a very
slow walker, for the reason that he generally
dropped his hoofs in almost the spots from which
he had just lifted them, and sometimes behind.
But at this point, cries of warning and of won-
dernment and disgust ran along the line, and look-
ing back, Morrissey beheld Matty Clark in the
distance, erect in his cart, gesticulating like a
maniac, and rapidly over-hauling the funeral.
Matthew had fixed a sapling under his trap for a
skid, and on this and one wheel he presently
rattled up alongside the hearse again, oblivious to
the threats and expostulations of the mourners.
“ Mike Morrissey, ye divil ye!” yelled Mat,
red, panting, and furious, “to the cemmethry fer
a quid ! ”
“ Niver a won av me,” replied Morrissey, hang-
ing on to The Imp.
“ Yis, be the powers !” roared Clark, shooting
ahead, and slashing viciously at the hearse-horse
as he passed.
Michael clung to the reins, and hauled with all
his might, but The Imp was not to be denied.
Squealing shrilly in reply to the challenge, he
broke into his old, ungainly, link-motion combina-
tion of canter, amble and trot, and spread himself
all over the road in pursuit of Colleen.
A couple of horsemen put their nags to a gallop
to head-off Matty Clark, and in this way the
funeral broke in upon Ballarat, careering down
Humphrey-street, and stirred the city to its
depths.
Fortunately Colleen was headed just before
reaching the main thoroughfare, and Daly and
O’Mara seized upon Matty, who was a small,
bristly Hibernian, and fought like a peccary. They
they got him down and tied him up. Then, after
throwing their turbulent captive into the cart,
O’Hara sat on his chest and led the horses, and
Daly, driving Colleen, now blown and humbled,
took up a subordinate position at the tail of the
procession ; while the funeral, which had paused to
collect itself once more, moved on, followed by a
delighted crowd of children and many envious
adults.
Many astonishing funerals had come up out of
Bungaree into Ballarat East, but Malachi
Mooney’s funeral was the most weird and wonder-
ful that ever invaded any town on the Australian
continent, and news of it seemed to have electric
passage through the place. The improvised hearse
with the well-intentioned effort to rig a pair of
plumes of cock’s feathers upon it, the strange,
jocund horse that hauled it, and the great, red,
clayed-up, hairy, wild-eyed Galway man driving,
were alone sufficient to have brought the whole
population into Bridge-street ; but with the added
attractions—the awful procession of drays, their
dusty, kiln-dried occupants, and the last vehicle
riding jauntily on its. skid, the funeral simply
stopped business, and took possession of the town,
and drew the people after it in crowds.
Morrissey had the reins wound about his wrists,
and with his heels dug in and his eyes obtruding
and all his faculties intensely concentrated hung to
The Imp. The matrons still swung their stout
feet, and here and there a worn-out mourner
slept in his dray, Heffernan and Moore with their
headssuspendedover the tail andtheirmouthsopen.
The police followed too ; .and eyed the procession
dubiously, half-inclined to arrest the whole f uneral;
but by exercising the severest self-restraint and
the greatest cautioij the mourners contrived to
pull through, and arrived at the cemetery with
the coffin in good order and condition at half-past
four.
After the usual preliminaries the coffin was
carried to the graveside by four of the late
Mooney’s most intimate friends, and, considering
all things, their progress down the path was not
as devious as might have been expected, but they
landed the pine casket with a dump that produced
the greatest sensation of the day. The coffin lid
had not been screwed down, and it slipped to one
side, making a revelation. There were many
cries and much commotion when it was seen that
the coffin contained packages of sugar and tea
and miscellaneous groceries, and nothing more.
Malachi Mooney was not there ! Consternation
sat whitely upon every face, and the women
crossed themselves vigorously.
“He’s bin slipirited away !” wailed the “ widdy.”
“ Did annywon see us dthrop him ? ” asked the
dazed Morrissey in a small, awed voice.
Flynn remembered now having packed the
groceries in the coffin the day before. He it was
who carted the casket out from Ballarat, and
having goods to carry at the same time, packed
them into the “ piner ” for “ convanience,” and by
reason of the thirst that' came upon him and
possessed him for two days “ disremembered
lvirything aftherwards.”
In truth the late Malachi Mooney still lay
undisturbed upon the bed in his humble home in
Bungaree, and the last of the yard-long candles
guttered in the brass-sockets at his head. The
cor/fSe had been forr/otten !
And this is how Malachi Mooney came to have
two funerals.
EDWARD DYSON.
Proud Moments.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Dramatis Personae: The Father, the Mother,
seven dogs,, a magpie, a young ’possum, six cocka-
toos.
The Father : Everything’s so dashed quiet.
The Mother (weeping): Oh, my boys, my boys !
The Father : They’ll be getting over the worst
cf their sea-sick by now.
The Mother: Oh, my boys, my boys !
Father : Down, Peter ; down, Sandy; down, I
tell you.
Mother : M-mind P-Peter’s sore f-foot, Father.
The Father : That’s right, Mother, now.
Pluck up a bit, old woman.
(Long, long silence.)
White Cockatoo : George, I’m going to see my
girl, blow you.
Green Cockatoo (lazily): Dash blank six blanks
three dashes.
Father: Shut up, you brutes.
Small Red Parrot: “Cook’s son, dook’s son,
son of a belted h-earl. Cook’s son, dook’s son,
son of a
Father : I’ll wring your necks.
White Cockatoo (sadly): The old man has got
his hair off this morning ; better look slippy, Billy.
(Long silence again. The birds mutter crossly.
The mother lies back in her verandah chair with
her eyes shut. The father leans forward with his
elbows on his knees, and His face buried in his
hands.) .
Magpie (whistling): ‘‘ We’re Soldiers the
Queen, my boys ; we’re soldiers of the Queen.”
The Mother : Oh my boys, my boys !
Father : Look here, they’ll all come ’ome to
us again, Mother. Think of the honor and glory
when they come ’ome again !
The Mother (weakly whispering): Henry
promised me he would see that Billy took his
cough mixture twice a day.
The Father : They might get sep’rated out
there.
The Mother (weakly whispering) : How are we
going to live without them !
The Father • Well, they’ve gone to serve their
Queen, woman. Everyone says how proud we
ought to be to ’ave six sops gone.
The Mother : Oh, 1 ' Father, are you talkin’
about feeling proud ?
The Father (breaking down) : My God, no 1
The Mother (cheering up .a little) : Willie pro-
mised me he wouldn’t forget his
The Father (fiercely) : You musn’t go coddl-
ing soldiers !
The Mother : They’re my boys if they are sol-
diers. *
The Father (cheering up a little): They’re
Queen’s men now.
The Mother : They were my boys first. Hasn’t
the English any heart? Qh me! oh me ! I’m only a
Eoor bushwoman myself, but I’ll kill myself
efore I’d take away another woman’s children
and put ’em out to be killed.
(Long, long, silence. Sunday afternoon slip-
ping away. Father dully wretched, lost and
lonely, Mother dreaming of old times when her
boys were babies. George’s ’possum, crawls
dejectedly on to her knee. Billy’s magpie struts
on to the grass in front and whistles “ When the
Empire calls.” The cockatoos swear and chatter,
call each other George and Harry, and blink little
enquiring eyes round the verandah. The dogs
snore heavily. It begins to get dusk).
The Father : Time to see about the horses.
The Mother : Time to see about the supper.
(They enter the house by different doors. The
Mother finds her way to Willie’s room—Willie,
her youngest—creeps on to his bed and lies there
with her head on the pillow. The Father steals
out into the verandah room that Alfred and George
had always shared, and sits on' the edge of a bed
with a look of stupor on his face).
White Cockatoo (hopping down the verandah):
Stewed eel, mother, Billy’s the boy for stewed eel.
(Silence, with a faint sound from two rooms
breaking on it, almost noiselessly).
(Rapping at the front door. A- visitor come to
supper. The Mother receives him).
The Visitor : I’ve ridden over with Sunday’s
paper for you, Mrs. Cameron.
The Mother (turning white): News!
The Father : You fool, girl. They’re not even
there yet.
Visitor: News of the war, of course. Yes!
News of your boys, too.
The Mother : Father, I can’t
(She falls against him. The visitor rushes for
water. They lay her down, fan her, give her
brandy, and throw water at her. She comes to a
little.)
The Visitor: Why, Mrs. Cameron, I never
meant to frighten you. The news is good news.
It’s about you, too. Ought to be cheerin’ readin’.
(he reads): “ The proudest woman in Australia
to-day is Mrs. Cameron from Boolibooloo. Her
six sons have all gone to the war, and she may
well take first place among all the proud mothers
of the day.” What do you think of that now ?
The proudest woman in Australia, her mind
wandering, her hair wet and lank round her poor
yellow face, her neck unfastened, water splashed
all over her black Sunday moire, lies disconso-
lately along the horse-hair sofa.
Green Cockatoo (dully in the twilight); Dash,
blank, ten dashes, eighteen extra special blanks !
LOUISE MACK.
The Girl of the South.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Her eyes reflect her own blue skies,
With far-off dreamy reaches ;
Her cheeks have caught the russet dyes
Of pomegranates and peaches.
Her breath hath savour of the scents
That her own bush doth lavish,
Her smile hath magic that dements,
Her lips have sweets that ravish.
Her golden hair the sunbeams curl,
And play about its tresses ;
Oh ! Nancy is a winsome girl
Bred of the sun’s caresses.
Her draperies have cunning art—
In blue she is a darling,
In white she plunders every heart,
In anything she’s charming.
She is not vain, nor too precise,
Her blush betrays but pleasure,
She loves whate’er she deemeth nice,
And takes it in full measure.
She is not coy nor very bold,
She hath her fine-wrought armour,
She will not every grace unfold
To any smooth-faced charmer.
She must be wooed by gentle arts,
And won by fond caresses;
For manly men and honest hearts
A weakness she confesses..
Nor will she be convention’s slave,
My free, my winsome Nancy ;
She roams at large like any knave,
And culls the flowers of Fancy.
She is a daughter of the Snn,
Her heart hath caught its brightness- p
The race for her hath but begun,
She treads on airy lightness..
While from the old she gleans the new,.
She is not deep, nor shallow ;
God keep her modest, brave, and true,.
With love our lives to hallow.
V.
